Preface This book is intended as a background and an introduction to a litde-known corner of the Buddhist world, existing in isolation in India. After being closed to outsiders for thirty years, for military and strategic reasons, Ladakh was pardy opened in 1974, and immediately became an important destina­ tion for the serious student of Buddhism and Tibetology. It also holds attractions for the lover of mountains, and the traveller who simply wants to extend his horizons; in addition it has become part of the Asian itinerary of footloose western youth. In spite of its many features of interest, no comprehensive account of Ladakh has been published in English in the nine years since it was opened, and authentic and up-to-date information is hard to come by. I have written this book in an attempt to fill the gap. In it, I have tried to place on record some of the important facts about a part of the world which has been litde studied up till now, and which has often been regarded as no more than an undifferentiated appendage of old Tibet. I believe, on the contrary, that Ladakh has its own vigorous social and cultural identity, of which the Tibetan tradition is only one component; and that it is important there should be an account of it before it is eroded by contact with the modern world. For the benefit of the visitor to Ladakh, I have also included some descriptions of places, particularly of the major gompas, or monasteries, within reach of Leh; and I should like to think that the book as a whole will give him some indications as to what he might keep his eyes open for, and help him to place what he sees in its historical and cultural setting. I lay no claim to originality, much less to having conducted anything in the nature of research. Many of the facts about Ladakh are available to anyone with access to the literature of Buddhism, or of central Asian and Tibetan travel, and the rest to anyone who

viii PREFACE has been lucky enough, as I have, to live there for some years. These, taken together, are my qualifications to write about Ladakh; of them, the second is of far greater importance. I lived for two years in Leh, and during that time formed a deep affection for the land and its people. My husband, Sayeed Rizvi, was Development Commissioner, or head of the district administra­ tion; as well as sharing my affection/or Ladakh, he acquired in the line of business a wide knowledge of the country as it is today, a good deal of which rubbed off on me. This, and the fact that I was living there, going about and meeting the people (though not, to my regret, able to converse with them in their own tongue) gave me something of a ‘feel’ for Ladakh, whose physical, social and mental atmosphere I found deeply sympathetic. In our day (1976-8) Ladakh was a single district, in area India’s largest, in population one of its smallest. In June 1979 it was split in two, the town of Kargil becoming the headquarters of a district of the same name. Most of this book was written before the bifurcation took place; even now it is hard for me to think of Ladakh as more than a single district. Our home was in Leh, and it is central Ladakh, the core of the present Leh district, with which I am most familiar, and on which I have laid most emphasis. I could indeed have confined myself to the present Leh district; but to do that would be to omit all mention of some particularly interesting regions in Kargil district — Zanskar, one of Ladakh’s most beautiful and isolated valleys; Da-Hanu, where the Buddhist Dards pursue a way of life unique in the world, and Shagkar- Chigtan, a nominally Muslim area rich in Buddhist survivals, and with a wealth of oral literature unsurpassed anywhere else. It would also be to ignore the underlying unity, historical, cultural and linguistic, which has for. centuries bound the often sharply dif­ ferentiated regions of the two districts together. Nevertheless, I am conscious that, not wishing to tread where I was unsure of my ground, I have not done justice to Kargil, and to that extent my picture of Ladakh is less than complete. We are no longer in Ladakh; but Ladakh was an experience which has remained with us. A Jjew of our Ladakhi friends, we hope'and believe, have become friends for life; and for me the writing of this book, started before we left, has been a valuable means of preserving that experience, and keeping in touch with Ladakh.

Preface and Acknowledgements to the Second Edition I n the twelve years since this book was published there have been many changes in Ladakh. The encroachments of the modern world, via tourism and the less or more well-conceived development policies of the Government, have continued. These are visible to the eye in the spread of the two towns, Leh and Kargil, many of the new constructions blending into the existing scene, while others are insensitively out of keeping. Attitudes have been affected too, and there is one school of thought which laments the erosion of the characteristically Ladakhi qualities of social cohesive­ ness and the self-esteem of individuals secure in their position and their value in the social order. It is true that until now the education on offer to young Ladakhis has done little to nurture their con­ fidence, and their pride in their own culture. But the exposure to the values of the Western world given by tourism, together with the realization of how much outsiders appreciate certain aspects of their way of life, has had the perhaps unexpected consequence of reinforcing many young Ladakhis’ commitment to and pride in their own culture; the quiet self-confidence of many of them, and their mature and positive attitudes to social issues, are remarkable. This was commented on by a very senior official of the Government of India who attended a seminar on ‘Tourism and the Environment’ in Leh in September 1995. In his tour note he gives this testimonial to the largely Ladakhi membership of the seminar: ‘The levels of consciousness, articulation and sheer enthusiasm were astonishingly higher than in any other seminar I have attended, in India or abroad.’ On the political level too the scene has been transformed since the 1970s. The political process speeded up to an unprecedented degree during the following decade, coming to an unfortunate

head with the agitation of 1989 in Leh, and eventually finding a kind of resolution with the establishment of the Leh Autonomous Hill Development Council, inaugurated in September 1995. At the same time, the even more tragic events in Kashmir have increased the tourist pressure on Ladakh, something which has had both positive and negative consequences, among them the opening up of the overland route from Manali to Leh. The Jammu and Kashmir Directorate of Tourism has on the whole risen to the challenge, exhibiting a feel for Ladakh’s unique qualities, and an awareness of the threat that it faces from the uncontrolled influx of outsiders. Among other sensitively conceived projects, the Directorate has been bringing out brochures and other literature of excellent quality. With so much good and informative literature specificallyt for tourists freely available, the appendix ‘Information for Visitors’ in the earlier edition seemed redundant; it has accord­ ingly been scrapped. So many changes in such a short time: it all cried out to be documented; and so did the virtual explosion in Ladakh studies that has taken place since the late 1970s. Ladakh was closed to the Western world for 25 years after Independence; it is one of the last regions where Tibetan Buddhism is practised in its original setting; some even of its major valleys have only in the last one or two decades been incorporated into the modern world by the construc­ tion of roads fit for wheeled traffic. As a result, since it was thrown open to outside visitors in 1974 it has become a magnet for scholars in many disciplines. Historians, anthropologists, social psycholo­ gists, political scientists, Tibetologists; geographers, geologists, agricultural scientists; students of music, folklore and linguistics, and more, have flocked to document a land and a people whose unique traits, not yet eroded by contact with the modern world, had been unavailable for study for the previous quarter century. Today there is a flourishing International Association of Ladakh Studies, which meets in conference every two years, and there is a move to set up an Institute of Ladakh studies in Leh, to ensure Ladakhi participation in the study of their own country, and access to the fruits of this growing corpus of research. I have myself been a small part of this movement, and my own knowledge of and insight into the Ladakhi scene have increased considerably since 1983. Going through the original text of this book as I revised it for this new edition, I have been embarrassingly Xll PREFACE AND ACKNOWLEDGEMENTS: SECOND EDITION

Although largely specific to Zanskar, Himalayan Buddhist Villages provides information and insights that are relevant to the whole of Buddhist Ladakh, and may be regarded as required reading for any serious scholar of the region — or indeed of almost any part of the Himalaya or trans- Himalaya. Introduction: Where Many Roads Meet I n the twenty-odd years since Ladakh was opened up to out­ siders, the ‘aura of mystery and inaccessibility’ that I referred to at the start of my introduction to the earlier edition of this book has worn a little thin. On the contrary, Ladakh has made its mark on the consciousness of the educated public in India and abroad to an extent unprecedented in the past. This has come about via both the thousands of tourists who have flocked to it every year, and the numerous books published on it, together with articles in the travel sections of the media; film-makers too have found enormous scope for the recording of both rare wildlife and unusual lifestyles. In addition, the region has also attracted the attention of scholars in a number of disciplines, whose research has thrown light on many aspects of the land and its people. With all this, Ladakh is still often projected as the last Shangri-la, a land of deep spiritual values where traditionally a contented peasantry pursued a sustainable agriculture based on self-regulating systems organized in such a way as to regulating to minimize social and individual stress. The truth is always more complex than the bland picture painted by the travel-writers. For one thing, Ladakh is not a single monolithic entity. In its relatively small area (a little bigger than Scodand, if you include the barren plateaux of north-east Ladakh that are under Chinese occupation; a little smaller if you leave these out of account) it encompasses an enormous variety of landscapes, their differences being based primarily on the great range of altitude. There is settled human habitation from 2700 metres to 4500 metres, and some of the camping grounds of the Chang-pa, the nomadic herdspeople of the plateaux of south-east Ladakh, are higher still. Altitude differences entail enormous

2 LADAKH ecological diversities, and these in turn affect the way people adapt to their environment. Yet at the same time there are integrating factors, of which the single most obvious one is the history shared by all Ladakh’s regions. The borders of the independent kingdom that Ladakh was for some nine centuries expanded and shrank according to the relative power of its rulers and those of neighbouring countries. But for most of that time they included all the areas that are part of the region today which, at the level of the ruling family and the aristocracy, were bound together by a series of marriage al­ liances. As far as the common people were concerned, almost every valley was meshed to all the others in a network of local trades in subsistence goods carried on partly by occasional trading expedi­ tions undertaken by the villagers themselves, and pardy by the exertions of a semi-professional class of traders known as Shamma, from the Sham Ilaqa, the Indus valley between Leh and Khaltse. Language too is a unifying force. The speech of the Chang-pa may differ markedly from that of the Purig-pa in Kargil, or the Zanskaris, but they are all mutually comprehensible. There is indeed a single basic culture, of which Buddhism is the bedrock. Buddhism is of critical importance for about half the Ladakhis; while the non-Buddhist communities, Shia and Sunni Muslims, and a small number of Christians in Leh, seem to be not entirely untouched by the characteristic Buddhist values of tolerance and compassion. All this must be responsible for the feeling of ‘La- dakhiness’, of kinship among all the people of the region that survives despite the fact that, since its biftircation into the two districts of Leh and Kargil, and the classification of its people into a dozen different groupings under the rubric o f‘scheduled tribes’, Ladakh no longer exists in any formal or administrative sense. The Tibetan connection is one which has fascinated many of the visitors and scholars, and it is referred to repeatedly in the pages that follow. The diversity among the different regions of Tibet’s far greater area must surely be as marked as that which is so obvious in Ladakh. Even so, south-east Ladakh beyond the Chang-la forms a continuum with western Tibet, and up to the middle of this century movement across the border was un­ restricted. Similar environmental conditions led to similar adapta­ tions in both countries, the most obvious example being the single architectural style existing across the whole high-altitude

INTRODUCTION: WHERE MANY ROADS MEET 3 semi-desert complex of mountains, valleys and plateaux that covers Tibet and its immediate neighbours. The Palace at Leh antedates the Potala by some thirty years and is on a much more modest scale; but the two buildings are clearly in the same tradition as are the great monasteries of both countries. Domestic architecture too is an expression of similar canons of style, though adapted to a different function. Much the same is true of the lifestyle of the people. The staples of the diet of all the peoples of the Tibetan cultural area are the same: tsampa, or parched barley flour, and change a mildly alcoholic drink made from fermented barley, both of them derived from the main crop of the Tibetan plateau; and butter-tea, which reflects the availability of butter from the livestock that are an integral part of the agricultural economy. Tea itself, in the form o f‘bricks’ of slighdy dampened and compressed tea-leaves, must have been obtained through imports from China along established trade routes from a very early period. The same technology too is found all over the huge area of greater Tibet. Water-mills for grinding barley, seemingly identical to those in common use in Ladakh today are described by George Bogle, the first Briton to make an extended stay in Tibet, as early as 1774-5; the amazingly sophisticated system of irrigation by means of channels contouring along the mountain slopes from streams often kilometres away is also current over the whole region, where precipitation is minimal and people must depend for water on the melting of the snow on the heights. Buddhism, in both its First and Second Spreadings, reached Tibet from India, and the role played by Ladakh, along with the neighbouring kingdom Guge, in the Second Spreading, was cru­ cial. But the centre of religious authority eventually shifted east­ wards, and by the thirteenth century AD the Ladakh religious establishment, along with those of the other countries on Tibet’s borders, had been relegated to a position of subordination tc rhe great monasteries of central Tibet, which thus became the focusej of a religio-cultural empire extending from Mongolia to Bhutan, and from Ladakh to the Koko-Nor. The details of religious ob­ servance and iconography as laid down by the incarnations who headed the various monastic schools became the norm over this entire area, as is evident from the descriptions of Bogle and later travellers. Many of their accounts of‘devil-dances’ could have been

4 LADAKH written to describe the monastic festivals anywhere in Ladakh in the closing years of the twentieth century. The impression is confirmed by the autobiographies of former Tibetan monks which have been published in western languages since the Tibetan, diaspora started in 1959, and which to anyone familiar with the Ladakh monastic scene are Rill of familiar echoes. But as striking as the identity of religious observance (no greater, after all, than over an area of comparable sire in pre- Reformation Europe) is the similarity of many social customs and attitudes. Bogle seems to have been the first to describe the Tibetan system of fraternal polyandry (he claimed credit for coining the word); and Michel Peissel found it actively practised in Mustang, the remote trans-Himalayan province of Nepal, in the mid-1960s. Peissel is also one of the few travellers to have described the custom known as khang-bu, or ‘little house’, in which the elders of a family, as soon as the eldest son has reached years of discretion, retire gracefully from participation in affairs, and taking only enough of the property for their own sustenance, yield the head­ ship of the family to him. Recent anthropological and sociological research indicates that a complex pattern involving varying forms of both these customs existed over the whole Tibetan cultural area till 1959, and perhaps still survives in remote parts of Nepal. Both remained current in the villages of Buddhist Ladakh until at least the early 1940s, when polyandry and inheritance by primogeni­ ture, which is part of the same system of family organization, were made illegal by the then Government of Jammu and Kashmir. . They are appropriate to village life where land is the principal source of subsistence, and they still exist in those remote areas that have as yet been touched only lighdy by the winds of change and modernization. Around Leh and elsewhere in central Ladakh, where economic development has radically altered the lifestyle of most people, creating alternative sources of income, they have practically died out. What remain characteristic of Buddhist Ladakh are the high status and complete emancipation enjoyed by women which, while not specifically commented on by Bogle, are implicit in his descrip­ tions of his own relationships with Tibetans of both sexes; and equally in the recent accounts by Tibetans of life in Tibet. A related feature is the absence of anything like a caste system among the Tibetan Buddhists. Class distinctions do exist, in the form of a

INTRODUCTION: WHERE MANY ROADS MEET 5 consciousness among all concerned that a few families form an aristocracy of birth. This is as true of Ladakh today as it was of Tibet in the period before the Chinese occupation. But it in no way dominates social intercourse; indeed, one of the most attractive things about Ladakhi life is the self-confidence of people not belonging to the privileged class, and the lack of arrogance of those who do. Perhaps it is after all part of the complex of Buddhist attitudes that people are assessed for what they are, and not prim­ arily on the basis of their membership of a particular class, or sex. The point need not be laboured further. Ladakh is, and always has been, part of the Tibetan cultural domain; and this will con­ stitute its main interest for a large number of people. And yet the visitor should not make the mistake of thinking that she has indeed stepped straight into old Tibet. In spite of the identity of religion and the similarity of language and custom, Tibetans now living in Ladakh as refugees hardly feel themselves even half-way home. For there is much more to Ladakh than only Buddhism and ‘Tibetan- ness’. Geographically it lies on the edge of the Tibetan plateau, and as such was open to influences other than the Tibetan. Central Ladakh was the bastion of Buddhism against which at last the tide of Islam beat in vain; western Ladakh, while not completely turning its back on the earlier ways of life and thought, accepted the new religion and much of the culture that went with it. Nor did central Ladakh itself remain untouched by outside forces, of which Islam was only the most insistent. Some of the Shamma traders not only scoured an area that included the Kash­ mir valley and Baltistan, but regularly trekked some 400 kilometres into western Tibet. As well as the complex pattern of subsistence trades exemplified by the Shamma, Ladakh was an unavoidable stage for international trade between the Punjab and Sinkiang. Situated at the centre of a network of important routes, Leh developed over the centuries into a major entrepot. This gave the capital something of a cosmopolitan air, reflected even now in the mixed racial composition of its population; while the speech of the town, a derivative of Tibetan as everywhere in Ladakh, is enriched by an admixture of foreign words, mainly Persian and Urdu. The dependence on trade must certainly have contributed to the amal­ gam of qualities that goes to make up/the character of the Ladakhis and their society. Francis Younghusband, who knew as much about the Himalaya and central Asia as any Englishman, had a great deal

6 LADAKH of time for ‘the typical travelling merchants] of Central Asia . . . intelligent, shrewd, full of information . .. [their] habit of rubbing up against men of so many different countries gives them a quiet, even temperament and breadth of idea which makes them very charming company.1 And which, one might add, surely con­ tributed to the development of similar qualities in the societies of which they were a part. At the level of popular culture Ladakh evolved its own highly individual style. It has a wealth of oral literature, some derived from the ancient pre-Buddhist past of Tibet, such as that based on the national epic, the Kesar Saga, and some seeming to spring from the native genius of the people. This includes a corpus of folk-songs reflecting every aspect of everyday life, and yet rich in symbolism and imagery. Ladakh’s music, while perhaps partly derived from Islamic models, has its own distinct flavour, as does its dance, an essential part of Buddhist social life. Traditional pastimes are archery — also popular among the Tibetans — and polo, a game which (in its original form, not as ‘improved’ by the British) exists in the western Himalaya in an area more or less coextensive with the spread of Islam. It has been kept alive in the Leh area by the settlement of Shia Muslims, originally from Baltistan, at Chushot, a village 15 kilometres up the Indus; but it generates wild enthusiasm among Muslims and Buddhists alike, and adds a unique excitement to summer in Leh. Of outside influences, Islam was far from being the only one modifying the ‘Tibetanness’ of Ladakh and the Ladakhis. On the political level, the Dogra conquest of 1834—42 brought Ladakh within the orbit of India; and though till 1959 the lamas con­ tinued to look to Lhasa for religious inspiration, the new genera­ tions of secular Ladakhis started going to Srinagar and the universities of British India for higher education. Thus the modern world, first brought in a small way by the devoted work of Moravian missionaries, made its more permanent mark via India; Ladakh became an integral part of the Indian polity in 1947 with the accession to India of the princely state of Jammu and Kashmir of which it formed a part. After that, but more especially since the 1960s, when the road link with Kashmir was built, it has been involved in the nation-wide programme of development and mod­ ernization which has been the hallmark of independent India’s economic effort. In the context of India’s ambivalent relationship

INTRODUCTION: WHERE MANY ROADS MEET 7 with China, Ladakh’s geographical position gives it a strategic importance which is reflected by a massive military presence. Units of the Indian Army and Air Force are stationed both in the accessible areas of western and central Ladakh, and far to the east and south-east towards the Tibetan border — a region on which, before their arrival, the world south of the Himalaya had hardly impinged; while in the mountains west of the Nubra valley they face their Pakistani counterparts in a years-long standoff, waging an artillery batde at altitudes which will force the rewriting of the military textbooks. For all Ladakh’s geographical remoteness and its social atmosphere more reminiscent of Tibet and central Asia, India is a palpable presence, and one that is there to stay. It is the combination of the spirit and atmosphere of Tibetan Buddhism with elements of other cultures that gives Ladakh its characteristic and piquant flavour. Old Tibet turned in on itself, refusing access to all outsiders except those traders and pilgrims whose journeys were sanctioned by ancient and uninterrupted usage, until the modern world forced its way in with the Young- husband Expedition in 1904. Ladakh on the other hand welcomed whoever wanted to come, which for most of its history meant traders from Kashmir, the Punjab, central Asia and Tibet. Some of them settled in Leh and contributed new genetic strains to the population, as well as taking advantage of the tolerant spirit of Buddhism to establish and practise their religion. While they and the Chushot Baltis seem to be the only immigrant communities of central Ladakh, many of the Muslim families of Dras and the Suru valley in Kargil district can trace their origin to migrants from Baltistan and Gilgit. The Islamic culture keeps a lower profile than the Buddhist, but without it, and without the contributions of the Indian connection, and even of the European missionaries, Ladakh would be a different and a poorer place. Notes and References 1. Francis Younghusband, The Heart o f a Continent, London, 1896, rpt. Hong Kong, 1984, pp. 154-5.

Ladakh: Mountain and Valley l Approaches T here are scheduled air services to Leh from Srinagar and Delhi, and Kargil too may soon be included on the air map of India. Even so, there remains a great deal to be said for coming into Ladakh by road. When this book was first written, and up to about 1987, this effectively meant taking what used to be called the ‘Treaty Road’ from Srinagar, via Kargil and over the three passes: Zoji-la, Namika-la and Fatu-la. That year however the old traders’ route from Manali to Leh was made fit for motor traffic, and thrown open to tourists; and after another three years the growth of militancy in Kashmir deprived the route from Srinagar of its attraction for all but the most intrepid, not to say foolhardy, of visitors. As of the mid-1990s, the majority of visitors fly in from Delhi; it is a relatively small proportion who choose to undertake the tough and tiring road journey from Manali. The road travellers consist either of the young and im­ pecunious, with more time than money and a high discomfort- threshold, who fill the buses; or the rich and adventurous, looking for a new and exciting experience, but able to pay for the individual vehicles and sophisticated camping equipment that will minimize the journey’s rigours. The revival of the Srinagar-Leh road as a tourist route will depend on the eventual resolution of the political problem of Kashmir, and the end of militancy there.

APPROACHES 9 A masterpiece o f road design and engineering, the Hangru loops — known popularly as the Jalebi Bends — carry the K argil-Leh road down from Lamayuru to Khaltse. The main drawback o f flying in is the strain imposed on the human system by the abrupt transition from the level o f the plains to Leh’s 3500 metres. (Air travellers are advised absolute rest for 36 hours after their arrival, and should avoid strenuous activity for at least another couple o f days to allow for acclimatization.) The flight itself, however, over the Great Himalaya and the Zan­ skar range, is spectacular. There are several different flight lines; in general the trend is to get there by a more easterly route, approaching Leh from up the Indus, and leave towards the west, usually turning south above Nyemo, and flying up the line o f the Zanskar river. The first part o f the approach flight from Delhi is over the plains; as the aircraft approaches first the foothills, and then the main line o f the Great Himalaya, it seems to be forced up and up, till it is practically skimming the snow-covered ridges and peaks. Recognizable landmarks are relatively few for most o f the way, unless the easternmost route has been chosen, over

10 LADAKH Rupshu, with the 100-square-kilometre expanse of the Tso-mo- riri Lake shining sapphire blue in a desert-like landscape of rolling brown hills and valleys. As the plane enters the Indus valley and comes close to Leh, it flies over the villages and gompas of Matho and Stok, Tikse, Stakna, Shey and Chushot. Similarly, on the way out, just as visitors to Zanskar may occasionally look up to the sky at the drone of an aeroplane, so the airborne passenger is sometimes able to make out the villages and gompasy and the whole layout of Zanskar s central plain, its two rivers joining and flowing away to the north. Only a few minutes later, the peaks and ridges of the Nun-Kun massif come into view off to the right, piercing the sky above the general level of the range. And so the flight continues, cruising once more only a few hundred metres above the tops of the mountains, until they fall away, and the hot world of the Indian plains rushes up to meet the returning traveller. But the problem with flying in is cultural as well as physical. Ladakh is not only geographically remote from almost everywhere, it is also remote in atmosphere, landscape and culture from anything most first-time visitors are likely to have en­ countered before, unless indeed they are familiar with other parts of trans-Himalaya. To plunge straight into this strange milieu, by air from Delhi, the only cushion between the two being an hour-long flight, cannot but create a sense of unreality, a feeling that Ladakh is somehow unconnected with the rest of the world. The road journey provides a kind of orientation. As it tempers the effect on the body of transition to a great altitude, similarly — allowing the traveller to observe the gradual change from one landscape and pattern of human settlement to another — it tempers the effect on the mind and senses of transition to an unfamiliar environment. It might indeed be argued that the only satisfactory way to enter Ladakh would be on foot or by pony. The receptive traveller may well regret the distance that the wheels of his vehicle put between him and the landscape, and in par­ ticular how he is cut off from its sounds: the sigh of the wind, the roar of rushing water, the whistle of the marmots, the cry of the birds. Today few could afford the luxury of a leisurely march, twelve days from Sonamarg or three weeks from Manali, and indeed with trucks and buses careering along the road, such a march would hold little pleasure. Happily the scope for mountain

APPROACHES 11 walking elsewhere in Ladakh is enormous and does include treks in from Himachal Pradesh and Kashmir. But these considerations apart, the ground routes have their own rewards. They pass through what must be in its stark way some of the most spectacular mountain scenery in the world; and the roads themselves surely rival, in some of the acrobatics they perform up and down the mountain sides, any in the Alps, the Andes, or elsewhere in the Himalaya. The route from Manali, which crosses four passes, three of them over 4800 metres, is open for no more than three-and-a-half months, from the end of June to mid-October. For almost half its length it passes through terrain so high and so barren as to have no settled habitation. There is a minimum of infrastructural sup­ port along the way, and travellers planning their journey should aim to be self-sufficient in food, warm clothing and, for travellers in their own vehicles, repair and survival kits. Buses and trucks go in convoy, individual travel should also be in groups, preferably using vehicles with a four-wheel drive. There are no repair or service stations between Keylang and Leh, and no petroi supply, so the party whose vehicle breaks down have no resources but their own to fall back on. On a road running for 200 kilometres at altitudes of over 4000 metres, such precautions could mean the difference between life and death. Manali itself, formerly an important meeting point for traders from Zanskar, Lahul, Kulu and farther afield, is now, as well as the start of the journey to Leh, a major tourist destination in its own right. As it leaves the town, the route is as pretty as the lower part of the Kulu valley, passing through hamlets and apple orchards by the side of the foaming Beas. From Palchan starts the zig-zag ascent up the slopes of the Pir Panjal, a major subsidiary of the Great Himalaya, through impressive mixed forests of pine and deodar, oak, chestnut and rhododendron, till the road clears the treeline and starts up the open mountain slopes to the Rohtang Pass (3978 metres). The pass itself is broad and flat; just below its crest rises a spring which is one of the sources of the Beas. Neither par­ ticularly high (by Himalayan standards) nor particularly difficult underfoot, the Rohtang is nevertheless regarded with some

12 LADAKH trepidation by local travellers on account of the unpredictable gales and blizzards to which it is subject. Although Lahul, the district which the road now enters, is not actually part of trans-Himalaya, since the Great Himalaya remains to be crossed, it lies in the rain-shadow of the Pir Panjal, and the view ahead could well be a trans-Himalayan landscape. In contrast to the lush green forested slopes of the Kulu valley to the south, the traveller is confronted with a vista of treeless mountains, culminating in snow-covered peaks and ridges stretching away as far as the eye can see. Upper Lahul consists of the valleys of the Chandra and Bhaga rivers, both of which rise in the Great Hima­ laya, close under the Bara-lacha-la, but flow off in opposite direc­ tions looping round to meet near Keylang, the district capital, and flowing off to the north-west as the Chenab. From the Rohtang, the road hurtles down into the valley of the Chandra, which it follows in a north-west direction to its confluence with the Bhaga. The villages are quite different from those of Manali. The Kulu style with its deep wooden verandahs and sloping roofs of slate or shingle gives way to a more Tibetan manner of architecture; the houses with their small windows are constructed from adobe bricks or stone cemented with mud, often whitewashed, and with flat roofs for storing winter forage for the livestock. This style of dwelling will soon become familiar in Ladakh. Although Lahul’s political history has been a chequered one, sovereignty over it being claimed at different times by the rulers of Kulu and of Ladakh, its cultural links were mainly with Ladakh, and its general ambience forms as good an introduction to Ladakh as any. In the days of the traffic in grain and wool between Kulu and the high-altitude pastures of Ladakh and Tibet, the Lahul people profited from their situation mid-way between the two end-points of the trade by acting as carriers. The excellence of their horses was remarked on by William Moorcroft, the first European to traverse the route. Since the discovery about a century ago that conditions in the district are ideal for growing potatoes, the farmers no longer confine their operations to subsistence agriculture. Seed potatoes are a cash crop that can be raised sustainably, year after year, and have brought enormous prosperity to Lahul; most of the fields in the oasis villages that the road passes through are devoted to their cultivation. The high-altitude pastures of the Pir Panjal and the

APPROACHES 13 Great Himalaya’s southern flank are the summer destination of the transhumant Gaddi shepherds, part of whose trek to and from their traditional grazing runs may take them along the motor road. The picture as a convoy of vehicles jostles for way with a flock of several hundred sheep and goats can be better imagined than described. The shoving and thrusting as the vehicles edge their way through, the shouting, the excitement; the Gaddis themselves, Biblical-looking in their rough woollen homespun girdled by a rope wound round and round the waist, often carrying a lamb or a kid in the pouch of their tunic: it all adds up to a scene of good-humoured pandemonium, which at the same time represents a collision between the modern world and another, archaic and timeless. Crossing the Chandra at Khoksar, the first village, an unlovely straggle of slacks and tea-shops, the road remains on the right bank, its line contouring along the mountainside high above the water. Near the confluence of the two rivers at the village of Tandi, it crosses the muddy and turbulent waters of the Bhaga to its left bank, and starts to rise again, following the river up to Keylang, the nearest thing to a town the traveller will see till Leh, still 360 kilometres away, is reached. Now the valley narrows, and the road remains high on the constricting walls of its gorge, passing the two Buddhist monasteries of Tayul and Gemur. It opens out again at Darcha, into a wide plain where the principal trail to Zanskar, along the Barai Nala and over the Shingo-la, diverges from the main road. Across the river, a trekking route takes off through the mountains to the south and east, and back to the Chandra valley. On again, still following the course of the Bhaga, to the bridge at Patseo, till the 1950s an important meeting-place for traders from Zanskar, Kulu and Rupshu, as well as Lahul itself, where salt and wool from the high-altitude lands ahead were bartered for food- grains, tea and other essential commodities brought up from Kulu and Zanskar. From this point almost the only signs of human habitation till the first villages of the Leh district some 200 kilo­ metres ahead, will be the occasional tea-stalls and rudimentary ‘hotels’ of seasonal entrepreneurs catering to the needs of travellers, and a few crude constructions put up by the Army and the paramilitary Border Roads Organization which is responsible for maintaining the road. The sole permanent inhabitants of the desolate region known

14 LADAKH as Rupshu between the Bara-lacha and Tag-lang Passes are a few thousand Chang-pa, nomadic herdspeople living in yak-hair tents. All over the Chang-thang, Tibet’s vast northern plateau land of which Rupshu is the south-eastern extremity, similar communities make the only possible economic use of this unpromising landscape by raising flocks of sheep, goats and yak on widely scattered pastures where the grass, though scanty, is reportedly highly nutritious. The Chang-pa goats are the source of pashm, better known as ‘cashmere’, the raw material of the Kashmir shawl industry and now in demand world-wide as one of the softest, warmest and most delicate woollen fibres. Although the Chang-pa are known as a good-humoured and cheerful people, their lifestyle is one which most of us might consider to be of the most appalling discomfort, especially in winter; even during the brief summer when the tourists are passing, their encampments seem to be on the rough and ready side. It takes an effort of the imagination to visualize the transfor­ mation of the rough, greasy, matted mass of fibres, as the pashm is combed from the goat’s back, into exquisite knitwear fetching enormous prices in Harrods or Bloomingdale’s. The road crosses to the left bank of the Bhaga at Patseo, and continues to follow it up to the grazing ground of Zingzingbar and as far as its source in the lovely little mountain lake of Suraj Tal. Now it hairpins up the stony mountainside to reach the watershed of the Great Himalaya, which it crosses by the famous Bara-lacha-la (4892 metres), a pass possibly unique in having a tri-junction at its summit, with a trail taking off towards the south-east, down the Chandra valley and eventually over into Spiti. The road to Leh carries on in a northerly direction, down past glacial lakelets, following the upper waters of a river, the Yonam, that drains the surrounding mountains but, deep in its trough, can do nothing to temper the prevailing aridity, till it reaches the Chang-pa pasture and camping ground of Sarchu, the take-off point for a truly rugged trek through the mountains to the monastery of Phugtal in Zanskar. For travellers doing no more than skim on four wheels across this bleak lunar landscape en route from one point to another, the names of the pastures are practically irrelevant, except as they may be the stations chosen by the tea-stall owners to provide some basic services. For the Chang-pa, on the other hand, each grazing ground has its own particular character, since their transhumance follows a strict

APPROACHES 15 system of movement from one to another, and they have a keen eye for variations that altogether escape the one-time traveller. It is just short of Sarchu, at Phalung Danda, an isolated rock rising out of the plain, that the road leaves Himachal Pradesh and enters Ladakh. The Yonam valley debouches into that of the Tsarap, a more considerable river fed by the glaciers of the Great Himalaya’s northern flank, which marks the divide between the Great Himalaya and the Zanskar range, and which, after a tortuous course through the mountains, becomes a principal affluent of Zanskar’s Lungnak river. The road has stretches that run ruler- straight, across plains dotted with more isolated rock pillars, be­ tween Sarchu and the bridge over the Tsarap, whose right bank it follows down for some 25 kilometres past more Chang-pa camp- ing-grounds. It then takes off up a side valley, zig-zagging up in 21 hairpins, an abrupt ascent of nearly 1000 metres in ten kilo­ metres of distance, to the Lunga-lacha-la (5059 metres), through one ridge of the Zanskar range. The tortured shapes of rock and mountain, and the amazing variety of their colours do offer some compensation for the exhausting struggle to the top, as taxing for the driver as for the vehicle. The descent is easier, down the left bank of a small stream to the staging-post of Pang, an amphitheatre of rock and sand, where the landscape bears testimony to the awesome power of nature to alter the form of the hardest stone. Its cliffs are honeycombed with caves, and eroded by wind and extremes of temperature into fantastic spires and pinnacles, which remind some world travellers of a mini-Grand Canyon. The crossing of another two rivers near their confluence brings the road out on to the Morey Plain. This seemingly waterless basin, four or five kilometres across and over 40 in length, un­ dulating at an average level of some 4700 metres, seems to offer no possibilities of supporting life. Nevertheless, some Chang-pa do occasionally set up camp there, as a base for reaching pastures towards the top of neighbouring sidevalleys. Even more surpris­ ingly, a lucky few of those crossing it may be able to spot wildlife — mainly in the form of marmots, mountain-dwelling rodents about the size of a beaver, whose luxuriant fur and clumsy seeming gait are belied by the ease and completeness with which they can disappear into their burrows, and frustrate any attempt to examine them more closely; or sometimes the occasional herd of fleet- footed kyang, the Tibetan wild ass.

16 LADAKH At the end of the plain the surrounding mountains draw in, and it is through the valley of a seasonal stream, and past a small lake, that the road approaches Debring, an important Chang-pa camping ground, at the foot of the ascent to the last and highest of the passes, the Tag-lang-la (5325 metres), over yet another branch of the Zanskar range. The ascent is straightforward enough; and once over the pass there is a frisson of excitement at the realization that at last the Indus basin has been reached: the stream that joins the road a little way below the pass is tributary to the Gya river, which flows direct into the Indus. The fields of Rumtse, the first village of Ladakh, poor enough though they may be at an altitude of 4420 metres, are a refreshment to the eye starved for so long of the sight of anything but the sparsest of desen vegetation. As the altitude decreases, the occasional oasis village — the green of its fields contrasting uncompromisingly with the bare brown of the barren mountain slopes, which here lack even the short grassy cover characteristic of Lahul — becomes a little bigger and its vegetation (comparatively) more luxuriant, with here and there plantations of willows, their heads bushy from repeated pollarding. Gya, the first village after Rumtse, was in old Ladakh the headquarters of a tiny chiefship, its ruler enjoying the honorific title of Cho. And so on down the Gya stream — past the village of Miru where for the first time since leaving Patseo the road drops to below 4000 metres — to Upshi on the Indus, a turbulent silt-laden river snaking down its gorge between the Zanskar and Ladakh ranges. Now it is reasonably plain sailing, a two-hour drive along the river to Leh. Some five kilometres below Upshi at the village of Igu, a bridge carries the road over to the right bank; and shortly after, at Karu, a wide valley opens up to the right. This valley is the start of the route over the Chang-la to the Pang-gong Lake and on, either to western Tibet, or up the Shayok gorge on the winter caravan route to central Asia. Another bridge leads across the Indus to the secluded glen where Hemis, Ladakh’s most famous gompa (monastery) is situated. Now the valley begins to broaden out, and right in the centre rises a huge rocky eminence, crowned by the impressive pile of Stakna Gompa. The road now passes through villages, whose fields almost merge into each other: Ranbirpura, carved out of the desert during the reign of Maharaja Ranbir Singh towards the end of the nineteenth century; and

APPROACHES 17 Tikse, the huge pile of its gompa dominating the fields and houses of the village. Shey is situated barely above the level of the river, and some of its land is subject to occasional flooding. Its houses scattered among the fields, the village is focused on the command­ ing bulk of the old palace, atop a ridge running out from the surrounding mountains towards the river, and with an even more ancient ruined fort crowning the heights above. Across the river lie the broad fields of Chushot, and, at the base of the Zanskar range, the villages of Matho and Stok. Nearly there now. Past Choglamsar, with its Tibetan refugee settlement and the Jeevan Sthal, the Dalai Lama’s prayer enclo­ sure; past a kilometre-long mani wall, through the urban sprawl of Leh’s outskirts, the road finally meets the main highway from down the Indus, and in a couple of bounds has got up the hill and into the main bazaar. At last, the bright lights of Leh. Travellers from Srinagar start their journey to Leh by taking one of Kashmir’s well-worn tourist trails up the lovely valley of the Sind river to the resort of Sonamarg. A few kilometres beyond Sonamarg, the road begins its zig-ag ascent towards the top of the Zoji-la (3450 metres). This series of hairpins is known as the Captain Bends, after the memorial to a captain of the Border Roads Organization who lost his life there. It almost defies belief that in November 1948, during the first Indo-Pakistan war, a column of tanks made its way through a near-blizzard, up this punishing ascent, to attack and dislodge the Pakistani forces who held the pass. So improbable was their appearance that when the Pakistani forward troops reported it to their command behind the lines, the latter refused to believe them, and replied that ‘the so-called tanks must be camouflaged jeeps’. A previous assault had failed, for infantry supported only by howitzers could make little impression on an enemy strongly entrenched in so commanding a position. The credit for the success of this one, which saved Ladakh for India, must be shared among the intrepid tank crews, using their vehicles at an altitude and in conditions never before attempted; the engineers who constructed a track for the tanks, in the shortest time and under the enemy’s fire; and commanders who had the dash and imagination to conceive this brilliantly unorthodox solution to an intractable military problem.

18 LADAKH The start of the ascent towards the pass is still recognizably a Kashmir landscape, with its mountain flora, and fir and birch trees. Opposite the steeply rising road, and far below, the Sind river, which it has just left, takes a right-angled turn away in a south-easterly direction along a delectable valley with a footpath, which is the lesser-known route to the holy cave of Amarnath, an important Hindu place of pilgrimage. Ahead, a stream flings itself down an almost sheer ravine from the pass to join it; at the confluence far below is the village of Baltal, which used to be a staging post on the old foot- and pony-road. The summer route, whose alignment is still visible in places, zig-zagged up parallel to and a litde below today’s motor road; in winter the crossing was made by scrambling over the masses of snow that filled the ravine. Today, when travellers are totally dependent on the wheel, the snow that covers the Zoji-la to depths of up to ten metres in winter forms an insuperable barrier, closing the pass for at least six months of the year. But well within living memory, mail-runners and porters from Dras carrying back-loads of trade goods, were cross­ ing more or less regularly throughout the winter, whenever the snow was not actually falling. After the climb, the road flattens out, and the ascent to the pass is so gentle that it would be easy to miss the top if it were not marked. The watershed is inconspicuous, and it is only a litde later that one realizes that the stream beside the road is flowing in the same direction as one’s journey. The Zoji-la, in fact, has been apdy described as less a pass, in the usually understood sense of the word, than a gigantic step cut through the wall of the Great Himalaya to the high valleys and plateaux beyond. But if the watershed is hardly dramatic — the drama of this pass having occurred in the steepness of the climb on the Kashmir side — the same cannot be said of the change in the landscape. The scenery on the Kashmir side is alpine; over in Ladakh it has become stark. Literally, there is not a tree in sight; and though we have not yet come to the complete aridity of most of central Ladakh, the vegetation is confined to short grass and low bushes. All along the road at this point are poles marked off in metres from the ground, to measure the depth of the winter’s snow. For in contrast to inner Ladakh, this area experiences heavy snowfall. Travellers early in the season, when the road has been cleared but the snow has not yet melted completely, may find themselves

APPROACHES 19 driving between snow walls that overtop their vehicle. This is less picturesque than it sounds: by the time the bulldozers have done their work, the snow is distincdy grubby, though here and there white gleams along the lines of rather ephemeral graffiti. The Great Himalaya forms a barrier to the entry of moisture-laden clouds to the rest of Ladakh, but here they find a way in through the gap of the Zoji-la, with the result that the ground is deep in snow for several months in winter. It must be an incredibly hard life for the people here: Dras has the local reputation of being the coldest permanendy inhabited place in the world outside Siberia. At the village of Dras the valley opens out into an upland plateau. Plantations of willow add to the impression of relative lushness given by the fields and meadows in contrast to the bleakness of the surrounding hills. The road continues to follow the waters of the Dras river for another two hours’ journey, passing its confluence with another major stream, the Shingo. As the valley narrows to little more than a gorge, the vegetation diminishes and the landscape grows starker. At the meeting of the Dras with the Suru river, coming in from the south-east, the road turns up the latter, and within a matter of minutes Kargil is reached — the second city of Ladakh, with a population of about 2500, and the natural halting place for the night. Kargil looks very different from the areas traversed hitherto. At about 2750 metres, and with a considerably shorter winter than at Dras, the climate allows of a lush growth of fruit trees and crops, including wheat, wherever there is water. Areas within reach of irrigation being a small part of the whole are thus intensively cultivated, two harvests a year being taken; and the brightness of the green fields and trees — or alternatively the rich golds and reds of their autumn colours — looks almost unreal, as if super­ imposed on the immediately adjoining bare brown hillsides. The town itself is a drab little place, to which the main contribution of the twentieth century would seem to be the perpetual traffic jam in its streets and alleyways. Straggling along the river-bank, a steep mountain slope rising on its other side, it occupies a strip of land too narrow for the construction of a bypass; consequently the main highway runs slap through its main bazaar. Although Kargil is only about twenty kilometres from the Indus as the crow flies, the road does not make straight for the river at this point, but continues on a course roughly parallel with it,

20 LADAKH crossing two passt: higher than the Zoji-la, and making a wide curve to join the river at Khaltse, 100 kilometres from Leh. Across the Suru river from Kargil, the road climbs a huge mound of alluvial deposit to the Khurbathang plateau, which was till a few years ago naked of all but the low creeping vegetation seen on most of the seemingly barren mountainsides of Ladakh. Since 1990, thanks to a major Government project, Khurbathang has been irrigated by water diverted from the Wakha river; this has enabled nearly a thousand hectares to be brought under the plough. The newly irrigated land, which has been distributed among the residents of surrounding villages, is laid out in large rectangular plots, a striking contrast to the small and irregularly shaped fields of the typical space-strapped Ladakhi village. The road descends from Khurbathang to the village of Pashkyum, its patchwork fields covering the valley floor and watered by channels taken off from the Wakha river. Ahead, the valley narrows almost to a gorge, and on the opposite side the Khurbathang channel is visible, contouring along the mountainside. Its concrete structure is again a contrast to the ‘private enterprise’ irrigation which the traveller may have noticed already: earthworks accompanied by strips of green, following the contours of the hillsides, sometimes at amazing heights above the valley bottoms. About an hour’s drive from Kargil, the road emerges into the smiling pasture and arable land around Mulbekh. This is the first predominantly Buddhist area. A small gompa perches high on a crag; and right beside the road, but partly obscured by a small shrine, an enormous rock engraving, dating perhaps from the eighth century, of Maitreya, the Buddha-to-come, watches over the comings and goings of villagers and tourists. From Mulbekh, the road leaves the Wakha river and turns left up the valley of a tributary. At least, this is what one would assume it to be, and what it must have been geological ages ago. A wide pebbly river-bed runs down the centre, and the sides of the valley are seamed and contoured with the beds of its tributary streams. Everyone has seen terrain shaped by water into exactly comparable formations. But here there is no water. That once there was water in plenty cannot be doubted; but the water has gone. Ladakh is a land from which, geological ages ago, upheavals of a violence beyond anything we can conceive drained off the abundant water of rivers and lakes: this is everywhere apparent, but nowhere so

APPROACHES 2 1 vividly, so weirdly, so disturbingly, as on this section of the road, the approach to the Namika-la. Here and there a scanty spread of low creeping vegetation appears, with even an occasional tiny patch of green, growing tenaciously out of some random ac­ cumulation of moisture from the melting of the meagre winter snows. This only serves to accentuate the otherwise all but com­ plete aridity; and inspires wonder at the determination of life to establish itself, given the slightest chance, even in the most un­ promising environment. The road contours up one side of this valley, and at its head crosses the Namika-la, a pass of 3700 metres. The name means Pillar of the Sky, and refers to the great rock formation that stands sentinel over it. The descent zig-zags down a mountainside which the eye made sensitive by the weird landscape on the other side of the pass can clearly perceive to be another from which the water has been taken away; but not so completely. Stream-beds have at least a trickle of water flowing down them, and a corresponding hint of green on their margins; and the valley into which the road debouches is open and cultivated. This is the village of Bodh Kharbu. The road follows the Kanji river up towards the Fatu-la; downstream is the area of Shagkar-Chigtan, rich in historical association and folk culture. In olden times this was the gateway to central Ladakh. It must have been here, around the year 1600, that a Balti army lay in wait for the king of Ladakh, Jamyang Namgyal, after perhaps tricking that inept ruler into crossing the Fatu-la in winter; and having outmanoeuvred and defeated him proceeded to overrun central Ladakh; and it was here that forty years later his son Sengge Namgyal, Ladakh’s most famous king, met a force sent from Kashmir by command of the Great Moghul himself, the Emperor Shah Jahan. The area’s strategic importance is attested by the existence of ancient fortifications. A little beyond where the road turns into the Kanji valley, on the opposite side of the river, stands the ruined casde of Stag-tse, while at the foot of the ascent to the Fatu-la the road passes the old fort of Heniskot. Even before Sonamarg, and increasingly after his entry into Ladakh, the traveller becomes aware of the surrounding moun­ tains, and their amazing saw-tooth outlines against the sky. These become ever more spectacular on the approach to and traverse of the Fatu-la, the last and, at 4100 metres, the highest of the three

22 LADAKH passes into central Ladakh. From the top of the pass the view opens out into a panorama of ridge, peak and valley, mosdy composed of the ramifications of the Zanskar range (through which the road has been passing ever since it left Kargil), but affording an exciting glimpse ahead of a snow-outlined ridge of the Ladakh range, north of the Indus. Even in the middle of summer, however, the temptation to linger and drink in the view is rudely cut short by the bitter wind that howls over the pass without respite. From the pass the road hairpins down to Lamayuru, surely one of the most picturesque and dramatically situated villages in India, if not in the world. A track runs down from the road to the village, clinging to the spur of a steep hillside, at the bottom of which, in a narrow valley, are the fields on which the life of the villagers depends. The flat-roofed, white or earth-coloured houses are piled almost on top of one another, in seemingly the most precarious way; the whole structure (for so it seems to be, rather than a group of independent buildings) is crowned by a gompa or monastery, its white walls picked out in red, rising in tier upon tier above the village. On the sides of the ravine below there is a multitude of astonishing rock and earth formations; these exist elsewhere in Ladakh too, but nowhere are there so many, and with such fantastic shapes. They are alluvial or lacustrine deposits, laid down when a far greater volume of water than now filled this narrow valley; their tortured forms are the result of millennia of wind erosion. But the descent from the Fatu-la is by no means finished at Lamayuru; for in one bound, so to speak, the road has to drop nearly 1200 metres, from 4100 to 2900, the level of the Indus at Khaltse. A few kilometres beyond Lamayuru the road starts to descend steeply once more — and what a descent! From above, virtually the whole of the next stretch can be seen in all its serpentine length of bends, loops and whorls, down to the bridge across the stream, the Wanla river, 600 metres below. Truck- drivers call this stretch the Jalebi Bends — aptly, for it indeed calls to mind the jalebi, with its fantastic curls and whirls. Spectacular though they may be as a piece of road design and engineering, the Jalebi Bends are undeniably tedious to negotiate (at least for the passenger; for the driver they are a challenge). But the tedium is relieved by the view of the mountainside opposite

APPROACHES 23 — on the other side, not of the narrow Wanla river, but of the Indus itself — appearing now on the right hand, now on the left, as the road loops and bends its way down. It presents an amazing range of colours: not only the brown and grey one expects of a rocky mountain face, but red, russet, dull purple, ochre, pink, even green. It is hard to believe that so many colours can occur naturally and in such profusion in non-living matter. And so at last on to the straight, across the Wanla river, which the road follows down a few kilometres to its confluence with the Indus. The Indus, historically and geographically one of the great rivers of the world, from which the very name India is derived; the Lion River, Sengge-ka-bab to the Ladakhis, which has already come 800 kilometres from its source on the northern flank of holy Mount Kailash in western Tibet — where, as tradition has it, it emerges from a lion’s mouth; the Indus which will empty itself into the sea near Karachi, 1600 kilometres on, after receiving the flow of the Shayok, the Hunza, Gilgit and Chitral rivers, the Kabul, and greatest of all, the Panjnad, the combined waters of the five rivers of the Punjab: at this point it is comparable in size to one of the medium-sized rivers of Europe: the Arno at Florence, perhaps. It gives a satisfying sense of geographical continuity to reflect that, however divided by range upon range of mountains, every one of the rivers and streams the traveller has crossed or followed since leaving Srinagar, is part of the immense river system of which this is the central artery, and which drains the area extending from Chitral in the north to the Great Indian Desert in the south; from Lake Manasarowar in the east to the Arabian Sea in the west. Now the traveller reaches the village of Khaltse, and is entering the heartland of Ladakh; the outward signs of Buddhism are everywhere. On the outskirts of every village are chortm, the Tibetan version of the stupa of ancient Indian Buddhism. Mani walls have their facing stones engraved with prayers and invoca­ tions — Om Mani Padme Hum; and strings of prayer flags flutter like faded bunting in the breeze. For the rest of the journey the road follows the Indus up its right bank, but not too closely, for at some points the river swirls through deep gorges, and the road must perforce climb up on to a neighbouring plateau. This happens beyond Saspol, the first big village after Khaltse, and famous, like Khaltse, for its apricots. Its

24 LADAKH smiling fields and orchards are in striking contrast to the bare brown plateau beyond, with only the low prickly vegetation of the desert, which is crossed before the road descends once more in a dizzy series of hairpins of the twin villages of Basgo and Nyemo. Towering above Basgo, an erstwhile royal residence, is a fantasuc system of wind-eroded pinnacles in dull red rock; towards the top of these are an ancient half-ruined fort and temple. In the 1680s, the fort held out for three years when besieged by the combined Tibetan-Mongol army that invaded Ladakh on the orders of the fifth Dalai Lama. Beyond Nyemo, the road rises again, following for a short distance the line of the river, hundreds of metres below, and affording an exciting glimpse of the meeting of the Indus and its biggest Ladakh tributary, the Zanskar river. The Zanskar valley is divided from the Indus by the range to which it has given its name; the river, in turning north to join its parent, has cut itself a mighty gorge through the mountains, and contributes to the joint stream a volume of water rather greater than that of the Indus itself. The road now turns away from the river for the last time — passing, rather unexpectedly, a modern Sikh temple dedicated to Guru Nanak — and crosses one more barren plateau. When it descends once more to the Indus, past the village and gompa of Phiyang, a couple of kilometres off to the left, the valley opens ahead into a broad plain. If, as will most often happen, it is getting on towards evening as the traveller approaches Leh, the rays from the low sun will be coming from behind him with a soft light, casting into relief with long shadows every contour of the moun­ tains rising on either hand, and illuminating the higher snow- covered ridges in the distance. The Indus meanders in a number of channels through the wide bed it cut for itself in the alluvium brought down by its tributary torrents, at a period when both mainstream and tributaries carried a far greater volume of water than they do now. The ancient river-bed is green with cultivation, and the sloping desert to the left of the road is dotted with army installations. The road approaches Spituk hill on the right, with its gompa just below the summit, and the green fields of Spituk village spread over the plain below. Now Leh can be spied eight kilometres ahead, a collection of flat roofs among trees, dominated by the

APPROACHES 25 massive bulk of the now-deserted palace. Just beyond Spituk on the right is the airfield; on the left, after four or five kilometres, military installations give way to the fields of Skara village — so near Leh as to be almost a suburb. At last the road wriggles through the outskirts of the town, round a last spur, and up a small steep ascent. At the top of this is a large gaily painted chorten marking the beginning of the city proper; far above, on the summit of the palace hill, the small fort and the temple of Namgyal Tsemo are silhouetted against the sky. With this, and the drive along the surprisingly broad and gracious main bazaar of Leh, still shaded by the remains of a splendid poplar avenue, the journey has ended; and the Ladakh heardand lies waiting to be discovered.

2 Profile of a Landscape A t its western extremity, the Great Himalaya, the barrier between the Indian subcontinent and the Tibetan plateau, . proliferates into an astonishing complex of ranges and sub-ranges. These, and the river valleys dividing them, form a system of rough parallels in the general direction south-east to north-west. The Shivaliks, the very outermost of the ranges, form the foothills that connect the whole system with the Indo-Gan- getic plain; the Dhauladhar and the Pir Panjal, also south of the main range, enfold the valleys of Kangra and Kashmir. North and east of Kashmir, from the crest of the Great Himalaya to that of the Karakoram, lies Ladakh, athwart the ridges and valleys of yet more major mountain systems. Taking them from south to north, these consist of the Great Himalaya itself, falling to the trough of the Zanskar and Suru valleys; the Zanskar range; the Indus valley, Ladakh’s historical heartland; the Ladakh range, extending into Tibet as the Kailash range, and giving birth to the even more impressive Pang-gong range; the valley of the Shayok, a major tributary of the Indus; and dwarfing them all, the Karakoram, which here forms the watershed between India and central Asia. Some 50 million years ago, the Indian subcontinent, drifting northwards across the Ocean of Tethys on its tectonic plate, slammed into the stationary mass of Asia. The ranges were formed over the next 45 million years by the buckling and folding of the landmasses being squeezed together; as the Indian plate ground its way under the Asian one, the land all along the line of impact was lifted thousands of metres into the air. Thus the Great Himalaya is formed from the base material of the Indian plate, while the Zanskar range consists of layers of sediment from the ocean floor, and the Ladakh range of granite born of the immense heat generated by the friction between the two plates. In Ladakh,

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 27 On the lip o f a gorge — the only vestige o f the torrent that sculpted it being a trickle ofw ater in the pebbly stream -bed— perches the ancientfort ofH eniskot. the suture zone between the continental masses runs a little to the south o f the Indus valley. The drift continues and is the cause o f the frequent earthquakes in the Himalayan region. However, there appears to be much less tectonic and seismic activity in Ladakh than elsewhere in the Himalaya and the Karakoram. At the same time, over the millions o f years since the uplift o f the ranges, the opposite process of erosion has also been at work, so that their original structure has been modified by the displacement and deposit o f material some o f which has in its turn solidified again into mountains. Water, and the movement of glaciers, are the later forces which have sculpted the landscape into the shape we see today. Crossing the Great Himalaya by the dip o f the Zoji-la, the traveller entering Ladakh from Kashmir is hardly conscious of traversing the greatest range o f mountains in the world. Indeed,

28 LADAKH at this point the crest-line of the range remains at a relatively modest level, the highest peaks near the pass being little more than 5000 to 5500 metres above sea-level. South-east of Zoji-la the scale increases, reaching a climax in the mighty massif of Nun-Kun, its two major summits, both over 7000 metres, tower­ ing over their neighbours. From an aircraft flying into Leh from Srinagar, or above Zanskar on the way out, their majestic oudines are unmistakable. On the ground, the best views are from the Suru valley, where it broadens out at the village of Yuljuk. Nun-Kun was first climbed in 1953 by a French group which approached from Kishtwar; later expeditions have approached the massif from the Suru valley. The Suru and Zanskar valleys form a great trough at the foot of the northern, heavily glaciated flank of the Great Himalaya, while opposite rise the mountains of the Zanskar range. The Suru river, indeed, forms the western as well as the northern boundary of the Zanskar range, as it loops in a right-angled turn round a huge mound of alluvial debris, the range’s last gasp, between the villages of Panikhar and Parkachik. The Suru, after receiving the combined waters of the Dras and Shingo rivers a short distance north of Kargil, joins the Indus at Marol — in Baitistan, which is now on the Pakistan side of the Line of Control — at an altitude of little more than 2700 metres; but its headwaters rise high in the mountains of the Zanskar range and the Great Himalaya, where they emerge from the glaciers east of Nun-Kun; they come together to form the mainstream on the plain of Rangdum at about 3800 metres. Rangdum, with its gompa and the attendant village of Julidok, is the last inhabited region in the Suru valley; it is also the destination of the nomadic herdspeople called Bakar- wals, who trek up every year from the Himalayan foothills near Jammu, bringing their flocks of sheep and goats to grow fat on the rich summer growth of grass. From Rangdum the valley rises to 4400 metres at the Pensi-la, the gateway into Zanskar. Visitors to Ladakh tend to give the Suru valley the go-by, even those who drive in from Kashmir stopping only for the night at Kargil, its only town, the half-way point between Srinagar and Leh. Before 1947 Kargil was an important staging post on the routes of the trade caravans, being more or less equidistant, at about 230 kilometres, from Srinagar, Leh, Skardu in Baitistan, and Padum, the principal village of Zanskar. Moreover, in those

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 29 days the Suru valley was surplus in foodgrains; thus it was an important destination for the local traders. Today’s travellers, having no such compelling reasons, seem reluctant to linger there; even those bound for Zanskar usually push through without stopping. And yet, though lacking in obvious tourist ‘draws’ like the gompas of central Ladakh and Zanskar, the Suru valley below Panikhar is one of Ladakh’s most attractive regions. The winter’s heavy snowfall on the northern flank of the Great Himalaya being converted by the sun of summer into water abundant enough to irrigate not only extensive fields, but also plantations of willow and poplar which make it positively lush — by Ladakhi standards at least. Rangdum, though on the hither side of the Pensi-la, is regarded as socially and culturally part of Zanskar rather than of Suru. The Suru valley is entirely Muslim as far as Parkachik, but the only a raison d ’etre of Julidok, the village of a bare half-dozen houses on Rangdum’s bleak elevated plain, appears to be to service the gompa there, and all its inhabitants are Buddhists. Its elevation and the proximity of the Great Himalaya, with its glaciers and everlasting snows, give it an unforgiving climate. There is no certainly that crops will ripen during the brief summer; and the ground remains under snow to a depth of two metres or more during the four or five months of winter. The villagers’ wealth, such as it is, is in the form of livestock, and virtually all their requirements, apart from dairy products, have to be brought from down the valley, or from Zanskar. Even now, although it has been on the main ‘highway’ into Zanskar since 1980, the people’s lifestyle seems to have changed only marginally. Although no doubt some supplies can now be trucked in, horses are still bred for local transport — also perhaps for hiring to trekking groups. Similarly, until the arrival of the motor road, the winds of change and modernization had hardly touched Zanskar. In the villages, where the population are exclusively Buddhist, life and religious practice remained at least till the 1980s much as they always had; and the same is true of the minuscule setdement of Sunni Muslims in Padum. Today, essential commodities and consumer goods which were formerly transported laboriously over the passes by horse caravan, can be brought in easily by truck; the Government has opened a chain of ration shops here as elsewhere in Ladakh. Zanskar has become a favourite destination for trek-

30 LADAKH kers; and change is coming fast. Even so, visitors remain fascinated by the evidently easy, friendly and laid-back character of the people, and the magnificent gompas at Sani, Karsha, Stongde, Bardhan and Phugtal. Zanskar has a distinct geographical character of its own. It consists of the troughs of two rivers, the Stod and the Lungnak (these are the authentic local names, though on many maps they are marked as the Doda and the Lingti, or Tsarap Lingti). True, one of the Lungnak’s main affluents rises as the Tsarap far away to the east in Rupshu. A litde way north of the Bara-lacha-la it is joined by the Lingti and another tributary; it then takes a tortuous course through the mountains in a generally north-western direc­ tion before turning abruptly south through a gorge and past the gompa of Phugtal to join the Kargiakh river descending from the Shingo-la, Zanskar’s gateway to Himachal Pradesh. It is the river formed from the junction of these two streams that is called the Lungnak. Its valley, which continues the north-westerly course of the Kargiakh stream, is for much of its length narrow, rocky and forbidding — the name Lungnak indeed means ‘dark gorge’. The Stod, on the other hand, which carries the meltwater of the great Drang-drung glacier below the Pensi-la, flows down a broad open valley, whose sweeping lines speak of its origin in the movement of glaciers. These two rivers approach each other in almost a straight line, meeting in the wide central plain below Padum. As the Zanskar river, their united waters flow away northwards by a gorge they have cut for themselves through the Zanskar range, to join the Indus at Nyemo. Like the Suru valley, Zanskar suffers heavy winter snowfall; the Pensi-la opens only in June, and is blocked again by mid-October, and even in the central plain the growing season lasts only from the end of April to the second half of September. An immediately striking feature of the landscape of both the central plain and the Stod valley is that it is virtually treeless; this creates an extraordinary impression of space and light. The Zanskar range is an extraordinarily convoluted mass of mountains and valleys in which the visitor, traversing them on the road between Kargil and Khaltse, may be pardoned for failing to discern any pattern or coherence at all. It is cut by transverse valleys carrying the waters of tributary streams to the Indus; the greatest of these is the Zanskar river itself. In winter, when the passes are blocked by snow, the frozen waters of the Zanskar river,

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 31 locally known as the Chadur, form Zanskar’s only link with the outside world, although today, in the mid-1990s, there are per­ sistent rumours that the Zanskaris’ long-standing demand for an all-weather road along the Chadur is likely to be met by the end of the century. The summits of the Zanskar range are in the region of 5500 to 6400 metres; the one which the visitor to Leh is likely to become most familiar with is the elegant Stok Kangri just across the Indus. Geographically, the Indus valley is the backbone of Ladakh; historically, from Upshi down to Khaltse, it is Ladakh’s heardand. All the main places associated with Ladakh’s dynastic history — Shey, Leh, Basgo and Tingmosgang — together with all the im­ portant gompas, outside Zanskar, are situated along this stretch of the Indus, either close to the river, or in tributary valleys within easy reach. The significance of Upshi and Khaltse is that these were the points where the routes from the Punjab and from Kashmir debouched into the valley. These routes of course were far from being Ladakh’s only links with the outside world, but they were the main links with the subcontinent south of the Himalaya. The fact that these were the points that defined Ladakh’s central identity is significant of the strength and importance of its relationships with the world beyond the mountains. The Ladakh range has no major peaks; its average height is a little less than 6000 metres, and few of its passes are less than 5000 metres. Within Ladakh it forms the northern boundary wall of the Indus valley, though when the river enters Ladakh at Dem- chok, some 250 kilometres south-east of Leh, it is actually flowing along the foot of the northern flank of these granite mountains, which it crosses by a great gorge it has cut for itself close to its confluence with the Hanle river. The Ladakh range has a magnificent subsidiary, the Pang-gong range, running parallel to it some 100 kilometres north-west from Chushul, along the southern shore of the Pang-gong Lake and a short way along the Shayok river.
