It is divided from the main range by the Tangtse river, and is actually a more imposing chain of mountains than the parent range, its highest peak rising to over 6700 metres, while its northern slopes are heavily glaciated. The alignment of the Shayok river is unusual. Its longest head­ water, the Chip-chap stream, rises just below the Karakoram Pass, and runs almost due west till it meets some of the main glaciers

32 LADAKH of the eastern Karakoram — the South Rimo, the Chong Kum- dan, the Kichik Kumdan (or Thangman) and the Ak Tash. It is the run-off from these rivers of ice that transforms it from a minor stream into a raging torrent; ultimately it drains the entire southern flank of the eastern Karakoram, including the Saser Spur, as well as the mountains and high plateaux to the east. For 160 kilometres after it receives the meltwater from the Rimo Glacier, it takes a southerly direction with a slight eastward trend; on meeting the granite mass of the Pang-gong range it is forced to turn at an acute angle into a north-westerly course which brings it into conformity with the parallelism of the whole system. Its tributary, the Nubra, plays a similar trick, flowing south for some 70 kilometres after it issues from the Siachen Glacier (the longest glacier in the world outside the polar regions), to drain the western flank of the Saser Spur. It meets the parent river on a wide sandy flat through which they both meander in a number of shifting channels, before ultimately deciding to continue the Shayok’s north-westerly course. A few kilometres beyond the confluence the valley narrows, and there are only a few isolated villages on occasional ledges or alluvial fans adjoining the river, of which Bogdang and Turtuk are the most important, before it enters Baitistan to meet the Indus at Keris. The region comprising the valley of the Shayok from its acute- angled bend down to where it narrows below the confluence, together with the valley of the Nubra itself, is known simply as Nubra, and has several large and pretty villages, among them Deskit, Hundar, Tirit, Tigur, Sumur and Panamik. The fall of the rivers is gradual — less than 200 metres between Panamik, twenty-one kilometres up the Nubra, and the confluence; and the spread and meander of the rivers irrigates a dense natural growth of hippophae (seabuckthorn), a low woody thorny shrub, which not only makes splendid fuel but also serves as effective fencing for the fields. This was an important consideration over the cen­ turies when thousands of horses traversed Nubra every year as the trade caravans wended their way between Ladakh and central Asia. The Karakoram, the climax of the whole system, boasts the highest peaks in the world outside the Nepal Himalaya, and includes K2, second only to Everest. But these giants are well outside Ladakh, in Pakistan-occupied Baitistan. The Ladakh Karakoram is not quite in the same league, though the height and

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 33 extent of its mountain system are imposing by any other standards. The massifs to the north and east of the Nubra-Siachen line include the Apsarasas Group (highest point 7245 metres), the Rimo Group (highest point 7385 metres), and the Teram Kangri Group (highest point 7464 metres), together with Mamostong Kangri (7526 metres) and Singhi Kangri (7751 metres). South and west of Nubra-Siachen, the Saltoro Ridge has throughout the 1980s and well into the 1990s been the scene of the highest artillery battle the world has ever seen; nevertheless both contend­ ing parties, India and Pakistan, have welcomed trekking and mountaineering expeditions to the adjoining massifs, apparendy as a means of asserting ownership and control. East of the Nubra valley, between it and the upper Shayok, lies the Karakoram’s last outflung spur, the Saser Mustagh, a lofty massif rising to 7672 metres, and having four peaks over 7000 metres, together with seven major glaciers all within a 10-kilometre radius. The glacier system of the eastern Karakoram, indeed, is second to none in size and complexity. Like all glaciers, these are subject to a process of expansion and shrinkage, according to principles not altogether understood by geographers and meteorologists. There have been occasions in the last couple of centuries (as no doubt earlier too) when the glaciers tributary to the uppermost Shayok have ad­ vanced so as to block completely the trough through which the river rampages on its course. A great barrier of ice has formed, behind which the waters have backed up in an enormous reservoir; the eventual breaking of the dam has led to devastating flash- floods. The last of these cataclysms occurred in 1929, since when the glaciers appear to be in retreat. North of the Karakoram, out of Ladakh, lies the Kun-lun, yet another formidable and complex range. Thus between Leh and eastern central Asia (the Sinkiang province of China) there is a triple barrier — the Ladakh range, the Karakoram and the Kun­ lun. Yet, over the centuries, such were the compulsions of com­ merce, that a major trade route was established between Leh and the oasis towns of Sinkiang— mainly Yarkand and Khotan. There were different lines of march for summer and winter, and from time to time the route had to be modified according to the advance and retreat of the glaciers. But whichever route the caravans took, the journey involved the crossing of a minimum of four passes and took no less than thirty days.

34 LADAKH In south-east Ladakh the parallelism of range and valley dis­ solves, only the Indus and its companion the Ladakh range main­ taining the general south-east-to-north-west trend. On both sides of this line the twin processes of elevation and erosion have created vast and complex systems of high-altitude plains and valleys, divided by ridges, the outfliers of the main ranges, sometimes only a few hundred metres higher, whose disposition shows no obvious logic at all. These regions are collectively known as Chang-thang, or (literally) the Northern Plain, a somewhat misleading term for this south-east corner of Ladakh. The reason is that they are actually a continuation of the northern plateau land — Chang- thang — of Tibet. Geographically speaking, Chang-thang’s most striking feature is the absence of a consistent slope which would enable water to drain away. Rather, the undulating land forms itself into huge basins, into which snowmelt streams flow, and finding no outlet settle into great brackish lakes. The biggest of these is the Pang-gong Lake, at 4300 metres, an extraordinarily long and narrow basin only six or seven kilometres broad at its widest point, and some 130 kilometres in length, extending far across the border into Tibet. North of the Pang-gong Lake, and separated from it by the mountains of the Changchenmo range, is the valley of the Changchenmo river, a major tributary of the upper Shayok; and beyond the Changchenmo the forbidding high-altitude plateaux of Lingzithang and Aksai Chin. These plat­ eaux are not a watershed; rather they form a complex of elevated drainage systems between the Changchenmo and Galwan rivers — the easternmost affluents of the upper Shayok — and the headwaters of the Karakash, which flows northwards into Sin­ kiang. Such is the complexity of the system, and such the godfor­ saken barrenness of these vast and remote expanses, that the imperialist map-makers of the nineteenth century had the utmost difficulty in fixing on the line of Ladakh’s border with the lands beyond; the present alignment seems to have come about more by accident than by design. Since the mid-1950s the Chinese have been in de facto possession of a huge swathe of the Aksai Chin, which for them forms a vital line of communication between Sinkiang and Tibet. Along the southern shore of the Pang-gong Lake, and in the valleys to its north, there is a scattering of tiny villages where a semi-nomadic population of Chang-pa herdspeople

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 35 stay in summer to cultivate a scanty crop of barley, taking to their tents in winter (a surprising reversal of the normal practice of transhumance) to graze their flocks of sheep and pashmina goats on high-altitude pastures. Here, in the Changchenmo valley and the southern flank of the Changchenmo range, and on the plains of Chushul, climatic and topographic conditions begin to approximate to those in the adjoining areas of western Tibet. There is more rain than in Nubra or central Ladakh, and wide valleys allow for a relatively lush growth of grass to nourish the herdspeople’s flocks. Rupshu, south of the Indus and lying athwart the chaotic outflying ridges of the Ladakh and Zanskar ranges, have a similar topography, but lower rainfall; here too the Chang-pa take their flocks of sheep and goats from pasture to pasture in a centuries-old pattern of migration throughout the year. The only permanent settlement is Karzok, at 4500 metres, by the shore of the great Tso-moriri Lake, a 100-square-kilometre expanse of brackish water draining the surrounding hills. Two other drainage systems, to the east and the north-west of Tso-moriri, each contain twin lakes, one of fresh water which drains into a lower salt-filled basin. Some 60 kilometres due east of Tso-moriri’s southern shore, and only 20 kilometres from the Tibetan border, lies the major gompa of Hanle on the river of the same name. In spite of Ladakh’s great elevation — no part of it being situated at an altitude of less than 2600 metres — the enormous mass of the Great Himalaya creates a rain-shadow, denying entry to the moisture-laden clouds of the Indian monsoon. The main source of water is the winter snowfall on the mountains which, stored for months as snow and ice and glaciers, runs off in summer as meltwater streams which are tapped to irrigate the fields. As the twentieth century draws to a close, there seems to be a general perception in Ladakh that the Great Himalaya is no longer functioning as an effective barrier, and that the amount of rainfall, especially during the months of the monsoon, is increasing. This, if true, is a matter of some concern, as village houses are con­ structed to withstand cold, not rain, and any major climatic change will necessarily have to be followed by a radical modification of the local architecture.

36 LADAKH Tens of thousands of years ago, it is true, Ladakh’s climate was moister and milder than it is now. The geological upheavals which gave the land birth must have been accompanied by dramatic changes in climate. The glaciers we see today, however impressive, are but vestigial remains of those which hollowed out — for instance — the Nubra and Zanskar valleys, filling them with ice up to 500 metres thick. The current tendency of the glaciers all over the Himalaya and the Karakoram seems to be to retreat; how this may be linked with the increasing rainfall of recent years — whether, for example, they are both symptoms of global warming — and whether the one could conceivably cancel the other out, are questions that only extended studies over decades will be able to answer. In the meantime, the possible implications of the observed shrinkage of the glaciers for Ladakh’s agriculture are worrying. Even in those areas which show no evidence of former glacia­ tion, there are striking indications of the waters that used to flow, and flow no longer. The salt lakes on the plateaux to the south-east appear to be the vestiges of a far more extensive system of fresh­ water lakes; and great alluvial and lacustrine deposits elsewhere — most dramatically below Lamayuru — prove the existence in past ages of quantities of water out of all proportion to the small streams we see today. It hardly takes a geologist or a climatologist to perceive this. Everywhere, and nowhere more clearly than on the approach to the Namika-la on the Kargil-Leh highway, we see a landscape whose contours have been formed by water, but in which water is now hardly there. Huge gorges through the mountains can only have been cut by terrific torrents rushing down them over untold ages; now perhaps a small stream finds its way down the centre of the chasm — a stream whose insignificance is mocked by the grandeur of the water-wrought formations at whose foot it trickles. It follows that geological ages ago the evaporation from such extensive lakes and rivers would have been a major determinant of the region’s climate, causing a far higher rainfall than the few centimetres that are now the sum of annual precipitation. Natural irrigation would have clothed valleys and the lower mountain slopes with vegetation. But today Ladakh is a desert — a high- altitude desert. What little water there is has been fully exploited, given the limitations of traditional technology. The traveller has

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 37 already noticed the irrigation channels, constructed in past cen­ turies without the resources of modern engineering and machines, which follow the contours of the hillsides high above the valley bottoms. Any substantial increase in the amount of cultivable land can now come about only by the intervention of those develop­ ment agencies which have at their disposal huge quantities of money and technical expertise. Two such projects, indeed, were implemented during the late 1980s and early 90s, one each close to Leh and Kargil; as a result, the amount of cultivable land in the two districts has gone up by almost one-third. Water indeed is all that is lacking to make even these poor mountain soils fertile. Even in normal weather, and much more so after an unusual spell of rain, apparently bare hillsides are found, on close inspection, to be covered with a carpet of low creeping plants, which nourish the livestock of the local population, and are even enough to provide them with fuel for the winter. Along every watercourse, natural or man-made, clings a strip of natural or cultivated vegetation; and wherever water is relatively abundant, the ubiquitous greys and browns of a parched landscape are relieved by the green of the growing crops, or in autumn by the gold of harvest and the crimson leaves of the apricot trees. In all the valleys except Zanskar, the landscape is softened by the bushy growth of willows, and the spires of poplars. Aptly have these spots, made fertile by the availability of water, been characterized as the oases of Ladakh. It seems to be an irony of nature that this land, through which flows in its upper reaches one of the world’s great rivers, should be a desert, dependent for its water on the storage of the winter’s snow in the glaciers and its release by the sun of summer. The result of all this is a set of ecological limitations that are unlikely to be overcome. Short of major climatic changes that may be in the offing, this desert is unlikely ever to blossom like the rose. Exercises in development like irrigation and tree-planting schemes and exploration for ground-water, even if successful, will do no more than scratch the surface of the problem. Water in abundance is simply not there, and unless the barrier of the Great Himalaya could be conjured away, probably never will be there. This landscape may be modified, it can never be transformed. The regions on the northern flank of the Great Himalaya — Dras, the Suru valley and Zanskar — experience heavy snowfall

38 LADAKH and remain virtually cut off from the rest of the country for several months in the year. Summers are pleasant, though short; in the lower reaches of the Suru valley, however, at less than 3000 metres, the growing season is long enough and warm enough to allow the farmers to take two crops in the year. In the valleys of central Ladakh and Nubra, on the other hand, and even more in the high-altitude valleys and plateaux of Rupshu, precipitation is minimal. During the agricultural year, when water is needed for the crops, the prayer is not for rain, but for the sun to shine to melt the snows that feed the streams that irrigate the fields. Because of the relative lack of vegetation, the proportion of oxygen is less than in many other places at a comparable altitude; and there is little or no atmospheric moisture to temper the effects of the rarefied air. Consequently, the force of the sun’s rays is actually greater than on the plains of India; it is the much higher extent of radiation from the earth that keeps the temperature down. The result is a climate of extremes in which, it is said, a bareheaded man sitting in the sun with his feet in the shade, may suffer from sunstroke and frostbite at the same time. Little heat is stored in the earth, and as autumn approaches the chill increases rapidly. Similarly, in the warmest weeks of summer, nights are cool; conversely in winter, even when the shade temperature remains well below freezing, the sun still gives real warmth. The strength of the summer sun is sufficient to ripen the crops very quickly, which is just as well, as it is not warm enough for sowing till May, and the harvest must be gathered in by September to avoid the first frosts. This refers to the Leh area, at about 3500 metres; 100 kilometres down the Indus, and 400 metres lower, at Khaltse, the harvest of grim (naked barley, the staple crop all over Ladakh) is gathered in by mid-July, and there is still time for another crop, of buckwheat, or turnips or other vegetables. Thus a difference in altitude at the same time involves a difference in climate; and at any given date, a difference of season. Late in May, for instance, when the crops are being sown around Leh, they are already half-grown at Khaltse; a month-and-a-half later, there will be golden fields down the Indus, while at Leh the barley is still green, and the harvest two months distant. At the extreme limit of cultivation, Karzok, on the Tso-moriri Lake, is at nearly 4600 metres. The fields there are said to be the highest in the world, but there can be no guarantee that the crops will ripen in any

particular year; often they are harvested green to serve as fodder for the livestock. PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 39 It is difficult after all to give a generalized description of a landscape 98,000 square kilometres in area, in which human habitation covers an altitude range of over 2000 metres. Each valley has its own character: Zanskar full of space and light; Suru pretty and relatively lush; Mulbekh smiling and homely; Bodh Kharbu strangely forbidding in spite of its openness; Nubra with its thorn thickets and hedged fields; and central Ladakh, full of history and somehow most visitors’ stereotype of what Ladakh is actually all about. Then Rupshu, an open, rolling landscape with fierce winds, and a striking frequency of dust-devils, and Pang- gong Lake, the snowy mountains surrounding it mirrored in its crystalline waters shimmering in an amazing range of blues and greens — both of these regions, inhabited only by the nomadic Chang-pa, seem like a completely different world again. The constant feature is the mountains. They are everywhere, and in every way they have shaped the life of the people. Their contours may vary from fierce and jagged to (less often) rounded and hummocky, but not even in the widest valley, on the broadest plateau, is there any getting away from them. Nor is there any need to wander very far off the beaten track for evidence of the processes by which they were formed. All along the Indus, from Khaltse to Leh, and even more strikingly along the upper Suru valley at Rangdum, can be seen the layers of sediment from which the Zanskar range has been sculpted, their crazy tilt, at all angles between the vertical and the horizontal, bearing witness to the strains and pressures of unimaginable upheavals in the earth’s crust. Less obvious on the ground is the geographically coherent pattern of parallels, which is so clear from the map; it is therefore with wonder and respect that one contemplates the merchant travellers of past centuries who, without the resources of modern surveying techniques and transport, worked out the hard way the best possible routes across this unpromising terrain. Despite the scanty vegetation of the mountain slopes, giving subsistence to sparse populations of wild sheep and goats; despite the alpine pastures of Dras and Rangdum, the summer destination of the transhumant Bakarwals; despite the fact that the Chang-pa

40 LADAKH herdspeople of south-east Ladakh graze their flocks in the open round the year; despite the lushness of the Suru valley villages, with their plantations of willow and poplar — despite all this, the impression that most visitors will carry away with them is of a cold semi-desert landscape. There are those, it must be said, who are appalled by it — who can see the starkness but not the grandeur; the barrenness, but not the way in which this is softened and balanced by the cultivated oases wherever there is water. It is true that the landscape is uncompromising, that potentially fertile soil is left untilled for want of water, and that whole areas are a stony waste. It is true that there is nothing to check the winds that scour the earth, fretting it into often grotesque forms. And yet there is loveliness in this stark scene. The rivers, which in summer when the snow is melting run muddy and brown with silt, become crystal clear as winter approaches, and take on the most amazing shade of ultramarine, as vivid as the turquoises on a Ladakhi woman’s perak. Lakes, whether brackish or fresh, gleam sapphire blue in their setting of bare hills or snowy mountains. For those with eyes to see, even the savage starkness of the mountains has its own beauty. Their crests bite into the sky with jagged saw-like teeth; and from some vantage points (there is one such between four and five kilometres out of Lamayuru on the way down to Khaltse) a vista opens of peak upon peak, ridge upon ridge, stretching away as far as the eye can see — and as much further as the informed imagination can conceive. On some of their flanks, the rock itself shows an astonishing range of colours — not only grey and brown, but dull red, purple, pink, russet, black, even green. And air these can be found again in the micro­ cosm of the pebbles at the traveller’s feet. Even more magical is the way in which their aspect changes with every shift of light. As the day draws to a close, the declining sun throws into relief the contours of the ridges, with their south-east-to-north-west strike, like the flank of the Zanskar range across the Indus from Leh, so that their horizontal as well as their vertical structure is revealed. Then, in the soft evening light, the last lingering regret for the absence of greenery is eventually stilled, and the landscape achieves a harmony with itself which is perfectly satisfying. Humbler beauties at nearer hand are seen against this ever­ present background. A wild-rose bush covered with a profusion of blooms, red, pink, or occasionally yellow; the graceful feathery

PROFILE OF A LANDSCAPE 41 flowers of tall tamarisk bushes; clumps of dwarf iris, their form no less perfect than that of their full-grown cousins in more temperate climes; the virginal white blossom that appears before the leaves on the apricot trees at the beginning of spring: all this fragile loveliness acquires a heart-breaking poignancy from its setting of bare earth and rock and the savage grandeur of the ridges. The robuster beauty of green or golden fields, or apricot orchards, the branches heavy with ruddy fruit or crimson with the leaves of autumn, gains too in the imagination by its nearness to the wilderness. A landscape where cultivation, or at least vegeta­ tion, stretches to the horizon, lets the mind sink into acceptance: this is how things are. But in the desert of Ladakh, this is not how things are, any more than in the Sahara. Here the bones of the earth not only protrude through the mantle which life has cast over them, as they do in the mountains anywhere; the covering is of the scantiest, and the structure shows through in all its purity. It is the beauty of line that predominates, rather than the beauty of texture. The human imprint on this scene of contrasts has in the main been a pleasing one. The painstakingly created oases start abruptly out of the barren slopes, the desert divided from the sown not by any strip of herbage strown, but only by a dry-stone wall. Each oasis has its village, the dwellings as often scattered singly among the fields as clustered together in a group or strung out along a road, but either way blending well into the landscape. The houses, flat-roofed, are built of local stone or sun-dried brick; in Buddhist areas they are often whitewashed to give a neat and pretty effect. Often, in order to leave every inch of flat or gently sloping land for cultivation, they are built up a steep mountainside; where such a cluster of houses is crowned by the piled-up structure of a gompa, (as at Lamayuru) the effect can be spectacular. Leh itself — at least till the recent processes of ‘development’ brought a measure of spurious modernization — was but the largest of such villages, the houses clinging to the slope above the bazaar, and dominated by the great bulk of the Palace; while away up the ridge on the Namgyal Peak — the Peak of Victory — stands the Namgyal Tsemo temple, the focus for the reverence and respect of the town’s Buddhist population. The Ladakhis themselves complete the picture. True, they have not been immune to change and modernization, and the years

42 LADAKH since Ladakh was opened to visitors and other influences from outside have made a lot of people, especially in and around Leh, sophisticated and worldly-wise beyond the imagining of earlier generations. But formerly, though no doubt the social life of the villages was as complex and highly evolved as in any other rural community, you might have imagined most of the Ladakhis created to fit the stereotype of simple, cheerful, honest mountain folk. The people of Suru valley are the possible exception, appear­ ing rather more serious, shy and reserved, especially the women. But elsewhere children laugh and wave to strangers. Women smile and greet whomever they chance to meet, and in contrast with the constraints under which they live in the neighbouring pre­ dominantly Muslim countries, they are obviously confident of their ability to cope with the world outside their homes. Among men and women alike, there is a conspicuous absence of status consciousness: of any exaggerated awareness of the cleavage be­ tween the sahibs of the world and the rest. In, for instance, the bazaar at Leh, this naturalness and ease of manner are palpable; they have captivated visitors for almost two centuries.

3 Creatures of the Wild O f the world’s great mountain chains, it is those of inner Asia — the Himalaya, the Karakoram, the Hindu Kush, the Pamirs, the Kun-lun, the Tien-shan and their sub­ sidiary ranges — that have the greatest variety of both animal and bird life. Even Ladakh, no more than a tiny fraction of this immense continuous mountain mass, is home to a variety of species that is quite remarkable, considering its altitude and the rugged nature of its terrain. There are various types of adaptation that different groups of fauna have been obliged to make in order to be able to fill this unpromising ecological niche. Some are behavioural, like hibernation, or seasonal migration from higher to lower elevations in winter; while others are physiological: the enlargement of the nasal cavity, or the development of thick fur or a thick warm winter undercoat which is shed in summer. In the 1980s and early 90s there have been a number of studies of Ladakh’s wildlife, several of them focusing on the snow-leopard, which (analogous to the position of the tiger in the wildlife of India’s plains) may be taken as the flagship species of trans- Himalaya. Not only does it stand at the top of the food-chain, so that its status reflects the health of the environment as a whole; but its beauty and rarity endow it with a certain symbolic grandeur which has fired the imagination of naturalists and lay people alike. Crossing the Zoji-la, the traveller cannot but be aware of passing from one geophysical environment to another; the character of the wildlife too changes accordingly. The Great Himalaya forms a dividing line between the Indian subcontinent and the rest of the Eurasian landmass to the north; and Ladakh, in its fauna as in other respects, has more in common with Tibet, central Asia and Mongolia than with India. However, as far as birds are

44 LADAKH A snow-leopard in magnificent pelage looks inquiringly at researchers who have just fastened a radio-collar around his neck. concerned, migration forms a link between the Indian and the Tibetan regions. The most conspicuous of the migratory birds is the hoopoe; even more common, though smaller and less easy to spot, is the Indian redstart. Perhaps the greatest surprise among the bird life is the brown-headed gull, which is seen along the Indus in summer, and which breeds along the margins of the Star-tsapuk Tso, Rupshu’s fresh-water lake. Along with several other species of gulls and terns, this too is migratory, staying from May till September, or even later, on the fringes of the Tibetan plateau to nest and breed, and returning to the coasts of India when the cold weather sets in. Other migratory birds which breed in Ladakh are the brahmini duck (ruddy sheldrake), the barheaded goose, and the great crested grebe: these too make their nests in Rupshu, round the fresh-water inlets to the brackish lakes, espe­ cially Tso-moriri. Chushul, the region immediately adjoining the Pang-gong Lake to the south-west, and Hanle, south of the Indus on

CREATURES OF THE WILD 45 Ladakh’s eastern border, have extensive marshes which are fre­ quented by a considerable variety of birds including the Tibetan or black-necked crane, one of the rarest birds in the world. The first sighting was in 1976, when two pairs were observed by an expedition led by the doyen of Indian ornithology, Dr Salim Ali. In 1980 there were as many as six pairs, of which one successfully reared a couple of chicks. The Army, which constitutes the main human presence in these remote areas, is in theory committed to protecting wildlife, and probably there is no wanton poaching of these rare and endangered birds. Twenty years after the first sightings, the species seems to be holding its own. The black­ necked crane is a high-altitude bird, with a short migration range; but nobody has yet discovered the winter home of the pairs found in Ladakh in summer. Migratory species apart, the birds found on the Tibetan plateau fall into two categories: high-altitude birds and desert birds. Ex­ amples of the first are the black-necked crane, the Tibetan raven, the red-billed chough, the Bactrian or white-rumped magpie, the snow-cock, and the chukor or mountain partridge; while among the characteristically desert birds are the sand grouse, sand plover, desert wheatear, horned lark and twite. Some members of both categories are common to the Himalayan and Tibetan regions, and sometimes to the plains of India as well. Such cosmopolitan birds are the ubiquitous house-sparrow, the carrion crow and the chukor. All these may be seen in and around Leh, close to human habitation — the chukor mainly in the fields — as may the Bactrian magpie, and in summer the hoopoe and the Indian redstart. The pied wagtail, a dapper black and white bird, is common near water. Birds that see the winter out, at least at the altitude of Leh, are the sparrow, the crow, the magpie, the grey tit, and the rather rare ibisbill. Flocks of hill pigeons are present well into the cold weather; they are locally migratory, and descend to lower areas of Ladakh in winter. Of the larger birds, the lammergeier or bearded vulture which is a scavenger is very oc­ casionally found near human habitations at high altitudes. The total number of bird species recorded by expeditions to Ladakh in the 1980s and early 90s adds up to an astonishing 225; they include, besides those already mentioned, sandpipers, stilts, stints, eagles, owls, bee-eaters, larks, mynas, warblers, thrushes, tits, and no fewer than fourteen different finches. Those

46 LADAKH on the endangered list include, besides the black-necked crane, the bar-headed goose, the golden eagle, the Tibetan sandgrouse, the Tibetan partridge, the Tibetan snow-cock, and the chukor — although happily this last seemed reasonably plentiful in September 1995, being spotted in numerous small flocks along the Leh-Khaltse highway. The most characteristic bird of Ladakh is perhaps the Bactrian magpie, a handsome fellow, almost iden­ tical with the common magpie of Europe, whose black feathers have a greenish sheen, and who sports a white breast and wing- bands, and a long fan-like tail. While bird-watchers may find much to reward them, those interested in spotting animals in the wild will probably be less fortunate. In any case, they are not likely to see much unless they take to the mountain paths and passes on foot; and even then they will regret that they could not set out on their expedition fifty or a hundred years earlier. For all the big mammals of Ladakh are on the endangered list. The so-called sportsman with a gun has wrought havoc among the wildlife of Ladakh’s mountains, much of it consisting of horned ungulates whose magnificent heads have been more prized as trophies to be displayed on a living-room wall than valued as part of a living ecosystem. It is true that at no time since Ladakh was discovered by the ‘sportsman’ and other sahibs in the mid-nineteenth century has uncontrolled shooting been officially countenanced. Under the Maharaja’s rule forty licences a year were issued; but the nature of the terrain made it impossible to enforce any restriction, so it may be assumed that a lot of poaching went on. Even so, it is reported that as late as 1958 there were sizeable flocks of shapu (the Ladakh urial) and bharal, the so-called blue sheep, and even a few goa, or Tibetan gazelle. By 1970 the shapu and bharal were clearly much reduced, though still present in a few areas, while it was not possible to spot the goa at all. It is assumed that during this period, before wildlife conservation became an issue on which there was wide­ spread public awareness, and when the Army was first being deployed in strength on India’s remote borders with Tibet and China, there was indiscriminate shooting of wild animals to supply fresh meat to the forward pickets — also perhaps to relieve the monotony of life in such lonely and remote outposts. By the Jammu and Kashmir Wildlife Protection Act of 1978 the shooting of some species is now forbidden absolutely, and of

CREATURES OF THE WILD 47 others severely limited; but although enforcement agencies are in place, it is probably in practice no easier to police the mountain trails and control poaching than it was in the Maharaja’s time. However, there is now an enormously greater public consciousness of the vital importance of conservation, and happily this has spread to many of the army officers stationed in Ladakh, with the result that the army is no longer a main agent of the destruction of the area’s wildlife. This consciousness has to some extent been re­ flected in public policy by the establishment of the Hemis High- Altitude National Park, as well as the sanctuaries of Lungnak, Shang, Kanji and Rangdum. Of the various wild goats and sheep that frequent the world’s high mountains, four are represented in Ladakh: the Himalayan ibex, which is a true goat; the bharal, which appears to be inter­ mediate between a goat and a sheep; and two species of true sheep: the shapu and, most magnificent of all, the argali or nayan with its enormous curved horns, a close relation of the Marco Polo sheep found in the Pamirs and parts of the western Karakoram. The bharal is found along almost the whole of the Great Himalaya, on both sides of the crest-line, from Ladakh to Sikkim; although now reduced, it is still the most numerous of the ungulates, and is not immediately endangered either in Ladakh or in its range as a whole. The ibex’s range extends from the Sutlej gorge westward along the Zanskar chain and the northern flank of the Great Himalaya, and on into the Hindu Kush, the Pamirs and the Tien-Shan. It prefers a higher and craggier terrain than the bharal. Although the Brokpa of western Ladakh consider the ibex a sacred animal, they have had a tradition of hunting it; in the past however this would not have been on a large enough scale to make a serious dent in its numbers. More recently, it has suffered in Ladakh not only from indiscriminate shooting by modern ‘sportsmen’, but also as a result of competition from domestic livestock. Even so, it is estimated that several thousand still exist in Ladakh and the trans-montane regions of Himachal Pradesh. The shapu finds its niche in rather lower terrain, especially along river valleys; this brings it into contact with human settlement, the pressure of which has eliminated it from many areas it used to frequent. It is one of the rarest of all the mountain sheep, and is now found only in small isolated populations; its numbers in Ladakh are estimated at about one thousand. It has a wide range, however, extending

48 LADAKH from a little east of Leh, as far west as Chitral. The nayan, on the other hand, has a limited distribution in India, south-east Ladakh and the adjoining part of Spiti being its only known habitat. It occurs however at a few places in Nepal and Bhutan, as well as in Tibet and the Sinkiang, Qinghai and Gansu provinces of China. The nayan favours open rolling slopes above 4000 metres, and is vulnerable to attacks by wolves. Its present population in Ladakh is estimated at little more than 200. Pashm, the raw material of the ‘cashmere* shawl, is produced from a particular kind of goat domesticated by the nomadic herdspeople of western Tibet and south-east Ladakh; but some of the different species of wild ungulates produce a similar kind of soft fine wool, from the warm under-coat which nature has pro­ vided to enable them to survive in winter. Those animals which have it in sufficient quantities to make it worth the trouble and risk of collecting, have in the past been mercilessly trapped and hunted, and Cunningham tells us that ibex in great numbers used to be ‘snared at night and shot in the grey dawn of the morning, when they venture down to the streams to drink’. But it is the Tibetan antelope or chiru, whose local name is stos, which pro­ duces the wool prized for centuries above all others for shawls, known as toosh, or even shahtoosh — royal toosh. The fineness and warmth of this delicate fibre are remarkable; however, today’s conservation awareness makes it evident that to drape oneself in a shahtoosh shawl is no less immoral than to wear a sealskin coat or a karakul cap. The weavers of Srinagar have been making shahtoosh shawls since at least the times of the Mughals, but it is only in this century that the number of chiru has been drastically reduced. Explorers in western Tibet around 1900 tell of herds numbering many thousands on the broad grasslands there; more surprisingly, tra­ veller after traveller on the Leh-Yarkand trade route record their presence in considerable numbers on both sides of the Karakoram Pass, a region where there is an almost total absence of vegetation. Theodore and Kermit Roosevelt, who made the journey in 1926 and one of whose tasks was the collection of specimens for the Chicago Natural History Museum, were quite unable to figure out how they could possibly subsist. ‘Their only food seemed to be the sparse tufts of dried grass that were scattered over the surrounding country at very infrequent intervals. They seemed to

CREATURES OF THE WILD 49 thrive on this meagre diet, for those we killed were as fat as butter-balls.’1 Today, the strategically sensitive area up to the Karakoram Pass is controlled by the army, who probably think they have better things to do than to concern themselves with the status of the chiru, although a proposal for a sanctuary has been made. The chiru is famous for following the same migration routes year after year (in the past this made it easy for the local Chang-pa to trap and kill them), and there is believed to be a small breeding population which wanders in from western Tibet to the Aksai Chin in summer. But the presence of the Chinese army, in force in western Tibet and in illegal occupation of the Aksai Chin, may be presumed to be hardly conducive to the survival of large populations of an animal which not only produces a valuable cash commodity, but whose meat is said to be delicious eating. Even more threatened is the Tibetan gazelle or goa, a small, slender and graceful animal, of which there used to be sparse populations in Rupshu, Hanle and Changchenmo, but which now seems to be headed for local extinction. The wild yak, larger and shaggier than the domesticated animal, and in the past a coveted prize for sportsmen, was found mosdy in the Changchen­ mo valley. A small population of about 20 individuals was reported by the army authorities to he still extant in 1983. Of all the endangered species of ungulates, the one that the visitor is most likely to get a glimpse of when travelling in Rupshu is the kyang, the Tibetan wild ass, which may occasionally been seen from a distance in relatively large numbers, but is too shy and too fleet to be approached closely. They favour open and gendy sloping valleys at over 4500 metres and appear to suffer from competition from the herds of the Chang-pa even in the remoter valleys to which increasing human activity in Rupshu forces them to retreat. Even so, with a population estimated at 1500 in 1991, this species seems to be holding its own. Of the smaller animals, marmots are plentiful on the hill slopes, even quite close to the road, in the Dras area and else­ where; the best time to hope for a sight of them is soon after the road opens, when they are perhaps still slighdy drugged with sleep after their long hibernation. They show no alarm at the sight of vehicles, or even of people emerging from them; but anyone hoping to get a closer look will be disappointed, because for all their clumsy appearance and. waddling gait, they vanish

50 LADAKH very fast when approached, into a network of burrows. There are two races, the Himalayan marmot and the long-tailed mar­ mot, the latter being found only in Zanskar, while the Himalayan race has a wide distribution. Although both are still plentiful, they are under threat not only from predation by feral dogs, but also from trapping for the sake of their fur, while the recent spurt of tourist activity in Rupshu, with uncontrolled vehicular traffic, has the potential to damage or destroy their burrows. There are plenty of voles and hares; and several varieties of pika, also known as the mouse-hare, a small animal common in the mountains of central Asia, of which at least one, the Nubra pika is under threat from disturbance of its habitat. Inevitably, the dwindling numbers of the prey species tell on the health of the predator populations. Not only is their natural supply of food reduced, forcing them to range more widely in search of subsistence, but when they try to make up the deficiency by forays into the herds of domestic livestock, the human popula­ tion in retaliation goes after them with trap and gun. Of big predators the most fascinating to the imagination is the elusive snow-leopard. Apart from the occasions when it approaches do­ mestic herds, sightings of this shy but magnificent animal have been rare, whether in Ladakh, or anywhere else in its range, which once included the entire mountain complex of inner Asia from the ranges of Tibet and the Himalaya to the Sayan mountains on the border between Mongolia and Siberia, above lake Baikal. Its range of altitude is equally amazing, from 1800 metres to close on 5400 metres. Although its beautiful pelt, whose pattern of white, grey and black blends almost to invisibility into the moun­ tain landscape of rock and snow, commands a high price in the illegal fur market, it is too elusive to be the target of systematic poaching; those individuals that fall to the poacher’s gun are probably the victims of opportunistic killing, at least in Ladakh. It is believed that snow-leopards exist in all the high mountain areas of Ladakh, though the density varies according to the availability of prey species; today there may still be some two hundred individuals in the region. Two other members of the cat family are found in Ladakh: the lynx and Pallas’s cat. Both, like the snow-leopard, are shy and reclusive, to the extent that little or nothing is known about the latter (which in captivity looks deceptively like a domestic pussy).

CREATURES OF THE WILD 51 The lynx favours scrubby bush habitat, like the seabuckthorn thickets of Nubra, where it preys on small mammals like hare and pika. But a survey carried out in the late 1980s put its population there at no more than ten to twelve individuals. Canines are represented by the wild dog — shaggier than the dhole of the Indian plains, but essentially the same species; the red fox and the Tibetan fox; and the Tibetan wolf. The foxes, along with other small carnivores like marten, weasel and otter, are nowadays sought after for their fur. It is said that there has been so much trapping, especially by migrant labour from the Indian plains engaged in the ongoing programme of road-building and maintenance, that these animals have practically disappeared from the highways and adjacent valleys. The wolf, which is in most respects the same as the European and American animal, is an important predator of the mountains and plateaux of trans- Himalaya, and is also the greatest threat to domestic livestock. Thus it is the animal to which the local people are — with good reason — the most hostile, and which they are most likely to persecute. This, together with the reduced quantity of its wild prey — nayan, shapu, goa, chiru and bharal — has resulted in a steady decline in its numbers, which a 1983 estimate put at no more than 300 individuals. The brown bear, which is found over an area west of the Ganga headwaters, and as far as the Karakoram and Hindu Kush, is a denizen of rolling uplands above the tree-line, and in Ladakh is found in the vicinity of Dras and the upper Suru valley, including Rangdum. It is omnivorous, hunting small rodents and quite happy to eat the flesh of naturally dead animals; but although on occasion it may raid domestic livestock as well as crops and fruit trees, its shy disposition makes it on the whole less of a nuisance to the human population than the wolf, and it is not much subject to persecution. There seems to be no estimate of its numbers in Ladakh. Notes and References 1. Theodore and Kermit Roosevelt, East o f the Sun and West o f the Moon, p. 63
