5 A Change of Direction W hile recent research has done much to reconstruct the history of old Ladakh, the story still remains tantal- izingly incomplete, and subject to conjecture. It is a drama over which the shadows of time and change have lengt ened, obliterating much and leaving the motives of the players often obscure. Only occasionally a shaft of light illuminates some tiny corner of the action, as when Mirza Haidar, the invader from Kashgar, includes in his memoirs accounts of repeated expeditions to Ladakh; or Bernier, in an unconsidered aside, drops the hint from which we can infer Sengge Namgyal’s trade embargo against Kashmir. Not until the nineteenth century, when the combination of European imperialism and scientific curiosity began to mesh the world together as never before, do the shadows begin to lift; and it is hardly surprising that it should have been an Englishman who rang up the curtain on this remote corner of trans-Himalaya: William Moorcroft, who with his companion George Trebeck, stayed from 1820 to 1822. Moorcroft was a veterinary surgeon — the first Englishman to qualify as such — and had come to Ladakh in the course of a journey to central Asia in search of horses for the East India Company’s military stud, of which he was the superintendent. He and Trebeck were kept at Leh for those years waiting for permission, not eventually granted, to proceed to Yarkand. They made good use of the delay, travelling all over the country and enquiring into every aspect of Ladakhi life. Their Travels— and even more Moorcroft’s correspondence and diaries from which the Travels were posthumously compiled — provide what is still one of the best accounts of Ladakh, many of their observations holding good even today. Another European visitor whose work contributed indirectly to a better knowledge and understanding of Ladakh, was Alexander Csoma de Koros, a Hungarian in search of his racial roots, which he hoped to establish

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 79 The town gate o f Leh, 1904. Situated at the south end o f the Bazaar, it was demolished in the 1950s. through the study of the languages of central Asia. He and Moor- croft ran into each other at Dras, of all unlikely places, and Moorcroft provided him with the introductions that he needed to embark on a study of Tibetan. As a result, he spent several years under the guidance of scholar-lamas in Zanskar, at Zang-la and Phugtal, and his involvement with the Tibetan language and culture became so intense that his original quest was quite sub­ sumed in it. He laid the foundations on which all subsequent scholars in the field of Tibetan studies have built, and is rightly regarded as the founder of modern Tibetology. Politically speaking, the emergence of Ladakh took place twelve years after Moorcroft’s visit, when the awakening was a rude one — something that he had foreseen and tried to prevent. When Kashmir passed under the rule of the Sikhs in 1819 the Ladakhi king, Tshe-spal Namgyal, calculated that his advantage lay rather with the more distant British Government (‘the legitimate repre­ sentative of the dynasty of Timur’, in Moorcroft’s quaint phrase)

80 LADAKH than with Ranjit Singh’s expansionist empire. He made an offer of allegiance to the East India Company’s Government, a move not only encouraged but actively canvassed by Moorcroft:, who even assumed the responsibility of forwarding the offer to Calcut­ ta. He explains his motives thus: On the one hand I averted from an amiable and harmless people the oppressive weight of Sikh exaction and insolence, and, on the other, I secured for my country an influence over a state, which, lying on the British frontier, offered a central mart for the extension of her commerce to Turkistan and China, and a strong outwork against an enemy from the north, should such a foe ever occur in the autocrat of the Russias. (Moorcroft and Trebeck, Travels, vol. 1, pp. 420-1) In this reasoning, we see the classic impulse of early British im­ perialism, the expansion of trade, combined with political motives that later came to dominate imperial strategy under the wry designation of the Great Game. But the days of the Game were half a century in the future; at this point, its hands tied by the Treaty of Lahore of 1809 with Ranjit Singh, giving him a free hand north of the Sutlej, the Company’s Government turned the offer down. It is difficult not to agree with Moorcroft — who seems however to have been unaware of the terms of the 1809 treaty, and the constraints they imposed on the British Govern­ ment in its relations with the Sikh Durbar — in regretting this. Probably inclusion within the British orbit was the only way in which Ladakh could have avoided the political extinction that was otherwise inevitable. For, confined by the 1809 treaty to the area north of the Sutlej, and prevented by British relations with the Sindhis and the Af­ ghans from extending its power south-west and north-west, there was only one direction in which the Sikh empire could expand north-east over the Himalaya. The attraction of Ladakh’s bleak mountainous terrain lay in its position athwart important trade routes, and in particular in its monopoly, guaranteed by the Treaty of Tingmosgang, of the trade in pashm, including that produced in western Tibet. This had been an issue with the Sikh Durbar since 1819, when Kashmir was added to Ranjit Singh’s empire. The misrule and oppression of Kashmir’s previous rulers, the Afghans, had driven many Kashmiri shawl weavers to seek their livelihood on the plains, particularly in the towns of Amritsar,

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 81 Nurpur and Ludhiana. In order to meet the growing demand from these places, a certain proportion of western Tibet’s pashm was now being diverted down the Sutlej, through Spiti and Kin- naur to the plains, bypassing Kashmir altogether and thus further impoverishing its traditional industry. The development of this trade in smuggled wool was encouraged by the British, through whose territory it must pass. They repaired the tracks used by the traders in Kinnaur, and offered the nomadic herdspeople of western Tibet higher prices than those fixed by the Ladakhi and Kashmiri monopolists. Both Ranjit Singh’s own government, and that of his most powerful vassal, the Dogra Gulab Singh, Raja of Jammu, were alive to the importance of this trade. The establishment of the shawl industry in the towns of Amritsar and Nurpur, both of them within the Sikh state, was a development that suited Ranjit, who also undertook to rehabilitate the traditional industry in Kashmir. Gulab Singh attempted to open a pashm route to the plains via Kishtwar, one of the hill districts he ruled over; this elicited complaints to Ranjit from the Sikh governor of Kashmir. But Gulab Singh’s ambitions went further than merely divert­ ing a part of this lucrative trade to his own territories. Ultimately he aimed at getting control over the entire pashm-producing area — in the name of his overlord Ranjit Singh, of course, but effectively for himself. Thus it was that he conceived and planned the invasion of Ladakh. Before embarking on the enterprise, he gave notice of his intentions to the British authorities, and received carte blanche to go ahead. Although in the course of the next few years the Ladakhi king sent repeated appeals to the British agents at various places on their north-western border, along with renewed offers of allegiance, there seemed to be nothing in them to tempt the British to break their treaty obligations to Ranjit. The Dogra commander-in-chief Zorawar Singh accordingly invaded Ladakh in the summer of 1834, taking the route through Kishtwar and Zanskar. Ladakh would seem to be ideal terrain for defence, and particularly for guerrilla operations, and with mini­ mal competence on the part of Ladakhi military leadership, the invasion might perhaps have been easily repulsed. But let alone competence, even leadership of any kind was lacking; for, effec­ tively, Ladakh had no army. The only preparation for defence in time of trouble was that a matchlock was supplied to every

82 LADAKH household, with a supply of powder; when the call to arms came, it was to be answered by one man from each house, armed with this primitive weapon, and carrying on his back bedding for the cold winter nights, and enough provisions — mosdy in the form of tsampa, parched barley flour — to last him a month. Thus we have a picture not of an army in any real sense of the word, nor even of a militia, but rather a rabble of untrained conscripts. With dash and imagination perhaps they could have been turned into a guerrilla force, harassing the flanks and rear of the invading army; it is conceivable that they might in that case have inflicted such damage to the morale, if not to the actual strength, of the Dogras that the latter would have been glad to retreat from this barren and hostile land. But the commanders of this unmilitary force suffered from the same limitations as the rank and file; the most damaging of these was lack of experience, for during the century-and-a-half since the Tibetan-Mongol invasion, military operations had been confined to minor skirmishes on the Balti frontier which can have constituted no sort of preparation for the challenge they now faced. So the Dogras were allowed to advance, without meeting any real opposition, till they reached Sankhu, above Kargil on the Suru river, where a hastily raised defending force gave battle to the invaders. It was defeated, enabling Zorawar to proceed as far as Pashkyum. Now winter was beginning to set in, and the Dogra general felt inclined to beat a strategic retreat. On the other hand, he had to have something to show his master for his four months’ campaign; so he opened negotiations with the Ladakhi court, offering to withdraw on payment of an indemnity of 15,000 rupees. The Chronicle records that while the king and his minis­ ters were on the whole inclined to take advantage of this offer, the queen showed more spirit (or a less realistic assessment of Zorawar’s intentions), and as a result of her intervention, the Dogra envoys went back empty-handed. Again we see the lack of any vestige of military instinct with which the Ladakhis might have successfully followed up the queen’s political initiative. Zora­ war prudently withdrew to Kartse, near Sankhu, to wait for the start of a new compaigning season; he was allowed to entrench himself, and the Ladakhis made no call on their most powerful ally, the numbing cold of winter. They postponed an attack till early April, when heavy falls of snow hampered them as much as

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 83 their enemies; were defeated with grievous lo$s; and found them­ selves harried and in retreat, with the Dogras at their heels, almost all the way to Leh. So complete was the failure of the Ladakhi ‘army’ to defend the country, that when the Dogras reached the Indus, they found envoys from the villages upriver — Saspol, Alchi, Likir and Nyemo — bringing presents and prepared to do homage to Zorawar. At Basgo, the king himself came to meet the invader; and now he had to agree to terms very much worse than those rejected at the queen’s instance a few months earlier. He was to pay an immediate indemnity of 50,000 rupees and an annual tribute of 20,000 rupees, and henceforward to consider himself Ranjit Singh’s vassal. There the situation might have rested if the Ladalchis, instigated it is said by the Sikh governor of Kashmir — jealous of the growing power of the Dogras — had not revolted repeatedly against Dogra domination. As a result of this, first Tshe-spal was deposed, and replaced by one of his ministers; later he was restored, but he died before long, to be succeeded as vassal king by his grandson, Jigmet Namgyal, a boy of only eight or nine. Meanwhile Zorawar was extending his conquests. Adding to his army a contingent raised from the Ladakhi levies, he invaded Baitistan, which he speedily subdued. This was followed by an even more ambitious scheme. The occupation of Ladakh had not had the desired effect of plugging the leakage of pashm to the plains. On the contrary, the disturbed conditions resulting from it had disrupted traditional patterns of commerce, to the extent that an important wool mart had developed at Rampur on the Sudej, bypassing Ladakh altogether, where the value of the pashm coming direct from western Tibet and being passed on to weavers on the plains rose from Rs 35,630 in 1837 to Rs 94,807 in 1840. To add the western provinces of Tibet, including the mercantile centre of Gartok, to the Dogra dominion would be to ensure once and for all control of the whole pashm trade from its very source. A more limited but equally desirable objective was the prospect of plunder held out by the fabled wealth of the Tibetan mon­ asteries. The formal justification for invasion was Ladakh’s ancient claim to the kingdom of Guge, indeed to the whole of western Tibet up to the Mayum Pass, east of Lake Manasarowar. The time seemed opportune for the Dogras to make a forward move. Following Ranjit Singh’s death in 1839, the government

84 LADAKH of the Sikh empire was in disarray and in no position to control its powerful vassal. The British, although they feared a link-up between the Dogras and the Gurkhas of Nepal, were too deeply involved in the invasion of Afghanistan to do anything but watch anxiously; while the Chinese, Tibet’s nominal suzerains, were having their problems thousands of kilometres away to the east, as the opium war with the British dragged its squalid course. Accordingly, Gulab Singh — unaware perhaps of the kind of terrain he would be committing his troops to — ordered Zorawar to invade Tibet. As in Baltistan, Zorawar added to the nucleus of Dogra veterans in his army contingents from among the recendy subdued peoples, Ladakhis and Baltis. He set off from the Indus valley in May 1841, dividing his army into three forces so as to cover the maximum number of important monasteries with their treasures, as well as the major forts. At the onset of winter, enriched with spoils from the gompas of Hanle and Tashi-gang, as well as several in Guge, he was well into Tibetan territory, in control of the sacred lakes Manasarowar and Rakas Tal, and the neighbour­ ing Mount Kailash, and with a garrison installed at the strategically important fort of Taklakot. The objectives of the raid seemed to have been achieved, for the whole of western Tibet, from the Mayum Pass to the Ladakh border, was in Dogra hands. The leakage of pashm to the Indian plains had largely been plugged: in 1841, the value of sales at Rampur had dropped to Rs 17,766, less than one-fifth of the previous year’s total. Winter was approaching, and it might have seemed prudent to retreat while the going was good. On the other hand, a long-term solution to the problem of procuring pashm would depend on conquest and permanent control, or at the very least on an agreement with the Tibetans which could only be imposed from a position of strength: to retreat now would be to lose the major advantage so far gained. It was important then to hold ground; in addition Zorawar probably underestimated the difficulties of the enterprise. Tibet must have seemed to him as easy a nut to crack as Ladakh had been. He might well have felt himself back in 1834, marching unmolested through a country peopled by unwarlike peasants, and meeting with only token resistance before the whole land would submit to the might of his sword. That year, his first experience of a trans-Himalayan winter, he had dug in for the cold weather, and seen it out without coming

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 85 to any harm. He failed to take into account firstly that he was much further from base, and secondly that he was at 4500 metres, an altitude where conditions would be incomparably more severe than at the 3200 of Kartse, where he had spent the winter of 1834. Thirdly, and this is where his calculations were most grievously in error, he discounted the fact that Tibet was not alone and friendless as Ladakh had been. It had a powerful protector in the shape of the Chinese empire which, whatever its problems with the British oil its eastern seaboard, was by no means going to sit back and watch the loss of several provinces of a country over which it claimed suzerainty. The result of a pitched battle between a well-supplied Tibetan force of 10,000, fighting on their home ground, and the much smaller Dogra army, far from their base and demoralized by the intense cold, was a foregone conclusion. In spite of the enormous odds, the Dogras fought bravely, holding the enemy at bay for three days. But their spirit did not survive the death of Zorawar; defeat turned into rout, and only the merest handful of those who fled in disarray from the field survived the bitter weather to make their way through the desolate tangle of the winter mountains and arrive back in India to tell the tale. This brought to an end both Gulab Singh’s dream of a greater Dogra empire stretching far into Tibet, and the flamboyant career of his most famous commander, Zorawar Singh. Zorawar surely possessed a modicum of military talent; but this talent has tended to be assessed with reference to his actual success, rather than to the quality of the opposition he encountered. It would have taken more than the feeble resistance offered by the Ladakhi levies to constitute any real test of his military ability. The invasion of Tibet showed faulty judgement, both military and political, by which the risks of the enterprise were totally underestimated; his failure in this, the most testing campaign of his career, casts some doubt on the military genius with which he has been credited. Zorawar Singh’s death raised hopes among the Ladakhis of regaining their independence; about 2500 of the matchlock-men blockaded the Dogra garrison at Leh in the early months of 1842 and proclaimed Jigmet Namgyal their only sovereign. They were reinforced from Baltistan, and soon by a detachment from the Tibetan army that had turned back the invasion of their own country. However, it now became clear that the conquest of Ladakh had not been a one-man affair — the Dogras after all were

86 LADAKH considerable fighters, trained and hardened in Ranjit Singh’s ex­ pansionist wars. Upon news of the approach of an enemy force of 7000 men, well equipped and armed with cannon, the matchlock-men deemed it prudent to slip away to their villages, while the Tibetans fled east to where the rest of their army was waiting beyond the Chang-la, taking the young king with them. They were pursued, and a battle was fought near Tangtse on the western side of the Pang-gong Lake, in which the Dogras redeemed the honour of their nation and avenged Zorawar’s death. With this restoration of the status quo of 1840, both sides were happy to put an end to hostilities. The terms of peace between the Dogras and the Tibetans were embodied in the Treaty of Leh, concluded on 17 September 1842. The Tibetans, recognizing the Dogras as legitimate rulers of Ladakh, ratified the border ‘as fixed from ancient times’, while the Dogras abandoned all claim to western Tibet. The young king, Jigmet Namgyal, and his family were given the right to reside peacefully in Ladakh, provided they refrained from intrigue against the Dogras. Later they were given the jagir of Stok, a village nestling at the base of the Zanskar range across the Indus from Leh. The Namgyal family remain jagirdars of Stok to this day, though the Ladakhis still honour them for the ancient dynasty they represent, and give the head of the family the courtesy title of Gyalpo or Raja. The nominal king was permitted, if he wished, to continue to send the triennial Lo-pchak mission of religious tribute to Lhasa, on the explicit understanding that this had no political connotation. As regards trade, the Dogras gained their main objective with the Tibetans undertaking to ‘allow the annual export of wool, shawls1 and tea by way of Ladakh according to old-established custom’. Both Ladakhi traders in Tibet and Tibetan traders in Ladakh could take advantage of the privilege of free porterage — i.e. obligatory labour from the villages en route — and accommodation. Later this treaty, con­ cluded between the Dogra and Tibetan officials on the spot without plenipotentiary powers, was ratified by the Sikh governor of Kashmir, representing the Sikh Durbar, and the Chinese am- bans, Residents at Lhasa, in the name of the Chinese emperor. Thus Ladakh became an integral part of the Dogra dominions. For administrative purposes it was bracketed with Baitistan, Leh, Kargil; and Baitistan being the three tehsils of the Ladakh wazarat,

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 87 or Governorship. When the state of Jammu and Kashmir came into being with Gulab Singh’s purchase of Kashmir from the British at the close of the first Sikh War in 1846, Ladakh was part of the new entity, as it remains in independent India. The Dogra wars brought Ladakh into violent collision with the outside world; never again was it able to retreat into the traditional system of limited political relationships in which it had lived till then. The ambivalence of its position between Tibet and Hindus­ tan, which had enabled it to maintain an illusion of independence in the wake of the war of the 1680s, was at an end, and Ladakh was subject to an uncongenial outside power. Compared with many, the autocratic rule of the Dogra dynasty of Jammu and Kashmir was not spectacularly oppressive. Even so, the Ladakhi peasantry were subject to unwelcome cesses in the name of land revenue, which had to be paid partly in cash, and partly in the form of grain and firewood which were stored in state-run supply depots and later sold to the trade caravans. They were also put under an obligation to provide porterage for both official and non-official travellers on the road. Worst of all perhaps was the arrogance and corruption of many of the local officials, both local men and outsiders. The villagers used to dread the time when the tehsildarox any of his underlings were expected to tour their area; not only was there no question of his paying a farthing for his supplies, but he would expect to return with his pockets a good deal heavier than when he set out. At a non-political level, relations with Tibet and central Asia continued much as before. The religious life of the country was still directed from Lhasa, and the caravans still crossed and re- crossed Ladakh with their loads of pashm and tea, indigo, brocade, silk yarn, silver ingots and narcotics. Their centre was at Leh, which continued to be the home of a colony of Kashmiri and Yarkandi merchants, and latterly of a group from Hoshiarpur in the Punjab. From this time, Ladakh and its neighbouring countries gradually began to figure on the map of the world, in which till then the regions north of the Himalaya had been largely a blank, filled up only speculatively, if at all. This was due to the efforts of a handful of surveyors and explorers, of whom the first to attempt a systematic

88 LADAKH account of Ladakh in ail its aspects was Alexander Cunningham, a member of the three-member commission deputed as early as the mid-1840s to survey and demarcate the boundaries between Ladakh and Tibet, and between Ladakh and Spiti in British India.2 The commission split up at Leh, its members going their separate ways. Cunningham’s two companions, Thomas Thomson and Henry Strachey, also published reports on their further explora­ tions, which came as a revelation to geographers and others who till then had been quite unaware of the extent of the mountain complexes — the Ladakh range, the Karakoram and the Kun-lun — north of the Indus. A steady trickle of explorers, sportsmen and even enterprising tourists reached Ladakh in the succeeding decades, and as early as 1853 books on trans-Himalayan travel were titillating the im­ agination of the British public. A longer stay was made by Frederic Drew who, attached in 1862 to the Geological Service of the Maharaja of Jammu and Kashmir’s Government, travelled the whole state, including the farthest plateaux of Ladakh, prospecting for minerals; and who completed his ten years in the Maharaja’s service with a year’s term as Governor of Ladakh. His account of the country and its people is one of the most accurate and sym­ pathetic that appeared in the nineteenth century. He was suc­ ceeded as Governor by an improbable character who has gone down in local tradition as Corporal Johnson. In fact, he was no corporal, but a surveyor earlier employed with the Survey of India, who for many years held the world altitude record, having as­ cended in the course of his duties to the giddy height of 23,000 feet (over 7000 metres). He was also the first (and for many years the only) European in modern times to reach Khotan, one of the most important trading cities on the fabled Silk Route. In 1885 Leh became the headquarters of a mission of the Moravian Church; and it was the mission which, in the following years, initiated the processes of change in a modest way. As well as a new religion, the missionaries introduced such useful skills as knitting and baking, and also the cultivation of exotic vegetables like potatoes, spinach, cauliflower, radish and tomato; the system of storing and preserving root vegetables in semi-underground chambers, so that they are available for the greater part of the

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 89 winter, is also credited to them. Soon after their arrival, the missionaries were asked to take over the small Western-style dis­ pensary maintained by the Maharaja’s Government at Leh; and a succession of doctors expanded its activities by work that can fairly be described as devoted. They built it up into a hospital for in- and out-patients, and travelled widely, bringing medical aid to the poorest and remotest villages.3 The first radio in Ladakh belonged to the mission in the 1940s; elderly people still remember how the battery, which it took two men to lift, used to be carried down to Choglamsar, where the near approach of the mountains to the river causes the wind to blow at gale force up the Indus, to be charged by a windmill; and what a big event it was when the recharged battery returned to town; and how there would be a rush to the mission that evening to listen to the war news. The cumulative effect of all these things must have been to create the sense of a world outside Ladakh: not merely a physical world, the idea of which had been made familiar to the Ladakhis long since by the caravan trade; but a world of new values, and achievements in the material sphere hitherto undreamt of. As a result, ambitious boys of good family began to go out to seek an education, and to discover the modern world. They seem, many of them, to have taken with relative ease and a minimum of cultural disorientation to the material culture of this century. The first Ladakhi to go overseas to study, Sonam Narboo, qualified at Sheffield University as a civil engineer in the 1930s. Later, he was instrumental in reducing the sixteen days it had used to take him to travel to school in Srinagar to a mere two; for he was the engineer in charge of the construction, first of the Leh airfield, and then of the Kargil-Leh highway. Other boys went to the universities of Aligarh, Lucknow and Banaras; now young Ladak­ his, girls as well as boys, are graduating from universities all over north India. But the change would probably have remained gradual — at least until Ladakh was brought within the scope of India’s major effort in economic development, which was not until after the construction of the road from Srinagar in the early 1960s — if it had not been for the catastrophic events of the period since 1947. In addition to their wider impact, these have had the unfortunate, in some aspects tragic, effect of disrupting Ladakh’s centuries-old pattern of commercial, cultural and religious relations.

90 LADAKH In 1948, in the wake of Partition, Ladakh found itself a battle­ field, fought over by India and Pakistan. The struggle for Ladakh was one episode in the larger batde for Jammu and Kashmir; after 400 years central Ladakh again faced invasion from the forces of Islam. The raiders, mosdy Gilgit Scouts from the far north-west of the Maharaja’s dominions, pushed up the Indus valley the early summer of 1948, and took Skardu and Kargil, carrying on over the Pensi-la to occupy Zanskar. In central Ladakh they reached the village of Taru, about eight kilometres west of Phiyang, and only a matter of twenty or so from Leh. The town’s defences at that time consisted of a platoon of the State Force, a volunteer detach­ ment from the 2nd Dogras, who had braved winter conditions to struggle across the Zoji-la the previous March, and a battalion of the local militia, which had been hastily raised in response to the threat from downriver, and was organized by some prominent citizens, including Sonam Narboo, now the engineer in charge of construction of the airfield which was to enable larger numbers of Indian troops to fly in. A few of the raiders managed to reach Leh by an arduous march through the mountains of the Ladakh range behind Taru and Phiyang, dropping down on the city from the north; but not in numbers sufficient to constitute any real threat. Summer saw not only the arrival of troops by air, but also a battalion of Gurkhas who marched up over the passes from Manali. Supplies were also sent up by this route. The raiders were pushed back from the vicinity of Leh, Zanskar was liberated, and a few months later the capture of the Zoji-la by tanks — a feat unparal­ leled in the history of warfare — enabled Indian troops to retake Kargil and secure the route to central Ladakh. Thus Ladakh as we know it today was saved for Jammu and Kashmir, and so for India. But Baltistan was on the other side of the cease-fire line; thus the friendly relationship that had grown up, ancient animosities for­ gotten, during the period when Ladakh and Baltistan were linked together under Dogra rule was brought to an abrupt end. One terrible concomitant of Partition was avoided in Ladakh: there were no riots or massacres on the basis of religion. During the period of confrontation between the raiders and the Indian forces, just down the Indus, the situation in Leh is reported to have been tense almost to breaking point. But the tradition of goodwill between the Buddhist majority and their Muslim neigh­ bours stood the strain, many of the Buddhist leaders setting their

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 91 faces firmly against any threat of violence towards the Muslims. If such violence had occurred, shattering at a blow the centuries- old feelings of mutual tolerance and esteem between the Muslims and Buddhists of central Ladakh, it would have been a tragedy that hardly bears thinking of. The events of 1948 led to the constitutional and administrative status which Ladakh enjoys today, as part of one of the constituent states of the Republic of India. One consequence of this was that it once more found itself in the front linevduring the Indo-Pakistan wars of 1965 and 1971. Both times it was Kargil and its environs, rather than central Ladakh, that bore the brunt. Operations were for the most part confined to the area of the cease-fire line, which in 1948 had been left so close to Kargil town that its main bazaar was actually overlooked by a Pakistani picket. This picket was taken by the Indians in 1965, but was handed back the following year under the terms of the T ashkent Agreement. In 1971 it was retaken, and the line of actual control pushed back to a distance of about twelve kilometres from Kargil town, where it still runs. As far as Ladakh’s internal situation was concerned, the two Indo-Pakistan wars were but episodes — incidents in a conflict of interests outside itself which simply happened to take place within its borders. It was otherwise with the Sino-Indian war of 1962. After occupying the Aksai Chin plateau, the Chinese advanced to Chushul on the southern margin of the Pang-gong Lake. As in 1948, the first weight of the attack was taken by the Ladakh Militia, now a properly organized force; units of the regular army, even by then a considerable presence in Ladakh, also fought with gallantry and devotion. However, they were unable to regain Aksai Chin. As a result of this war, Ladakh emerged for the first time into the forefront of Indian public consciousness. Suddenly its strategic importance in relation to China became a major political issue. The loss of Aksai Chin, a piece of Indian territory measured in thousands of square kilometres, and the circumstances leading to this loss, had a profound effect on Indian attitudes and policies. However, it must be admitted that the Aksai Chin, a barren and uninhabitable waste, nowhere less than 4500 metres above sea- level, though of strategic importance to China as providing a link between Sinkiang and Tibet, had no material value for Ladakh or for India, and its loss has been of little practical effect. The main consequences for Ladakh of China’s threat to India’s north-west

92 LADAKH borders (as opposed to its policies in Tibet and central Asia) were the construction of the motor road between Srinagar and Leh, and the beginning of the military build-up which today even the most unobservant visitor cannot fail to notice, and which has led to considerable modifications — on the whole beneficial — to Ladakh’s life and economy. But neither the war fought on its northern and eastern borders, nor the loss of the Aksai Chin, had such deep and far-reaching effects on Ladakh as the policies of communist China in central Asia, and its occupation of Tibet. In 1949 the frontier between central Asia and Ladakh was closed, putting full stop to the trade, perhaps of a thousand years’ standing, across the Karakoram, and depriving Ladakh of its traditional economic base as the central mart on a network of important trade routes. The following year Chinese forces marched into Tibet, and throughout the fifties, in spite of resistance from guerrilla forces in the eastern province of Kham, which gradually spread to central and southern Tibet, the Chinese tightened their stranglehold till they were effectively in complete occupation of the country. Even so, the trans-border trade in pashm and other commodities continued till the end of the decade, and young monks from Ladakh continued to go for their higher studies to the great monasteries of central Tibet, travelling now via the Indian trade mart at Kalimpong rather than taking the old direct Leh-Lhasa caravan route. Many of them stuck it out till the flight of the Dalai Lama in March 1959, when they joined the stream of Tibetan refugees making for the sanc­ tuary of India. They had to return to Ladakh, some of them with their studies only half accomplished. Abruptly, for reasons entirely external and irrelevant to the logic of its own development, Ladakh was deprived of its ancient reliance on the elders of its faith in central Tibet. For seven centuries Ladakh had looked to Lhasa for guidance in all its religious activity, and as much of its cultural activity as was dependent on religion; now, until the re-estab- lishment of the Tibetan monasteries in India, the Ladakhi Bud­ dhists were on their own. Of the events since Partition, the four wars fought on its soil are in a sense those that have touched Ladakh least. It is the abrupt severance of its ancient ties with central Asia and Tibet that has

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 93 really shaken it to the foundations of its history and traditions. Nevertheless, the economic setback caused by the cessation of the caravan trade has been recouped, and in good measure, largely as a result of three new factors in the situation: the large-scale military presence; the opening up of Ladakh to Indian and foreign tourists in the mid-1970s; and the extension to Ladakh of India’s ambitious programme of economic development. Ladakhis today are at least as prosperous as at any time during the heyday of the caravan trade, and much more so than the people in most districts of Gangetic or Peninsular India. The impact of these events on Ladakhi Buddhism is harder to assess. The Chinese occupation of Tibet, and the destruction of the great monasteries there, on which Ladakhi monks had for centuries been dependent for their higher learning, was no doubt initially a crushing blow. But in the decades since the Tibetan diaspora, the principal monasteries have been re-established in India, and today as in the past young Ladakhi monks are studying in Sera, Drepung, Tashi-lunpo and the other mother-houses of the various monastic schools; only now they travel to Dehra Dun and Karnataka rather than to Tibet. While the education offered in these ‘new’ monasteries reportedly includes some of the secular skills necessary to make sense of the modern world, it is probably true that the real challenge faced by Ladakhi Buddhism^is not so much the destruction of the religion within Tibet, as the spread of the fundamentally non-religious values that seem inseparable from the processes of ‘modernization’. As long as village families continue to have the perception that, giving economics its due but no more than its due, it is in their larger interest to devote one of their children to the religious life, and as long as the chosen children agree, so long will the monasteries of Ladakh flourish, and with them Buddhism and Buddhist culture. The enthusiasm and devotion displayed during the occasional visits of the Dalai Lama, and especially when he conducts an important initiation, are proof that the religion retains its hold on people high and low. But only time will show whether in the long run it has the strength to withstand the combined onslaught of secularism, materialism and consumerism. The politics of post-Independence Ladakh has had its ups and its

94 LADAKH downs, and has inevitably been influenced by the recurrent troubles in neighbouring Kashmir, with which it remains con­ stitutionally yoked in the state of Jammu and Kashmir. The perception that within the state, Ladakh — by far the biggest of its three constituents in terms of size, but correspondingly the smallest in terms of population, and having its own separate identity — was not getting its due, and never would, has resulted in a series of agitations carried on within a shifting framework of political alignments. The demands put forward have varied at different times. Given the compulsions of state and national politics, the demand for separation from Jammu and Kashmir and direct rule by the Central Government as a union territory was probably a non-starter, though obviously this was not realized at the time. But after much political manoeuvring and actual agita­ tion, the Ladakhis did succeed in wringing from the Centre inclusion in the list of Scheduled Tribes, which brings them a package of benefits, mainly as regards education and employment. This however hardly solved the problem of the relationship with Kashmir, and how to eliminate the perceived discrimination against Ladakh in terms of representation in the State Legislative Assembly, as well as in terms of allocation of funds. The bifurca­ tion of the region into two administrative districts, Buddhist- majority’Leh and Muslim-majority Kargil, was not a lot of help, in fact it tended to reinforce the regrettable tendency towards communal groupings. Things came to a head in 1989, when the movement in Leh against the Muslim-dominated State Govern­ ment most unfortunately took a communal colour, being also directed against the Muslim minority there. Violence on an un­ precedented scale erupted, and when the dust setded in a few weeks the Ladakh Buddhist Association ordered and enforced a ‘social boycott’ of the Muslims — an action that seemed to many a negation of all that Buddhism and Ladakh’s admirable com­ posite culture stood for. Happily, the boycott was lifted in 1993, and Buddhists and Muslims came together on a common platform to work for the creation of a local council with legislative and fiscal powers that would give the Leh district a certain degree of independence from the State Government. Prolonged negotia­ tions with the Centre bore fruit in 1995, with the creation and inauguration of the Leh Autonomous Hill Development Council. In principle this should give the people of Leh district a degree of

A CHANGE OF DIRECTION 95 control over their own affairs such as they have not enjoyed since the Dogra conquest in 1834, and the enthusiasm which it has evoked seems to augur well for its successful working. One of the most remarkable things about the unfortunate happenings in Leh between 1989 and 1993 was that they evoked no violent reaction in Kargil. Sandwiched as they were between the actions of militant Islam in Kashmir, and an overdy anti-Mus­ lim movement in Leh, the people of Kargil stood firm, and refused to be tempted into either anti-Buddhist or anti-Government agita­ tion. They must have sometimes been tempted to react, and it is arguable that their steadiness in face of provocation surely con­ tributed to the peaceful resolution of problems in the Leh district. Although they have sometimes been described as narrow-minded and bigoted Muslims, the political maturity they have displayed during the crises of the early 1990s gives the lie to any such slur. Their decision to refuse for the moment the offer of an auto­ nomous Council like that inaugurated in Leh is further evidence of their level-headedness. They may indeed resent, as much as the Leh people, the domination of Kashmir in the politics of the state; but situated as they are, their relationship with Kashmir is neces­ sarily closer than that of the Leh people, and as long as the problem of Kashmir remains unresolved, it seems likely that the Kargilis will prefer to keep their options open.
