6 Hazardous Trails L adakh might seem isolated, tucked away in the midst of mountain ranges among the world’s most formidable; nevertheless in the old days it was situated squarely between some of the great mercantile towns of south and central Asia, and there is evidence that traders may have been crossing its moun­ tainous terrain as early as the ninth century. AD Close to the old bridge over the Indus at Khaltse are the ruins of an ancient fortified customs house, identified as such by an inscription referring to the ‘lord of the trade in the lower valley’ — a high-sounding tide for a prosaic customs officer. Even more intriguing, rock inscrip­ tions at Tangtse near the western end of the Pang-gong Lake were apparendy engraved by Nestorian Christian merchants from the region of Samarkand in perhaps the ninth or tenth century. Willy-nilly, the caravans had to toil up Ladakh’s high passes and traverse its dusty valleys in order to effect the perennial exchange of textiles, dyestuffs and spices from Hindustan, shawls and saffron from Kashmir, dried apricots from Baltistan and latterly a range of manufactured goods from Europe, against tea from China, carpets, raw silk and charas (a narcotic, the resinous exudate from the hemp plant) from Sinkiang, and pashm (cashmere) from Tibet as well as from Ladakh’s own south-east plateaux. Indeed, there was not one trade, but several trades, all affecting the polity and economy of Ladakh in different ways. Together, they not only defined the relationships of Ladakh and Ladakhis with neighbour­ ing countries, but contributed to the modest degree of prosperity so frequently remarked on by nineteenth-century travellers, par­ ticularly in contrast with neighbouring Baltistan. Not all the trades were unique to Ladakh. All along the great Himalayan divide, from Bhutan through Sikkim and Nepal to Almora, Garhwal, Kinnaur, Spiti and Lahul, there was from time immemorial a brisk exchange of subsistence goods between the

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 97 In sketches by a Ladakhi artist, a brigand threatens a trader; a porter rests his load on a forked stick; Chang-pa barter barley against salt with a Muslim villager from lower Ladakh. plains and foothills to the south and the high-altitude plateaux to the north. These were complementary economic zones; and some Himalayan communities developed an extraordinary expertise as middlemen, dealing mainly in salt from Tibet’s salt lakes, which they sold as far south as the Terai, and foodgrains from the outer hills which they supplied to the Tibetan herdspeople. Other com­ modities were traded too, at various times and along different routes; but salt and grain, together with wool from the great flocks of sheep grazing the Tibetan plateaux, were the staples of what was essentially a subsistence trade conducted on the basis of barter. Ladakh had its own version of this trade, which was basic to its economy. But there was one major difference between the trade in this farthest west sector and that existing elsewhere. This was pashm, the raw material of the Kashmir shawl industry. No doubt pashm can be produced anywhere on Tibet’s immense northern plateau land of Chang-thang, where the nomadic herdspeople, the

98 LADAKH Chang-pa, raise great herds of yak, sheep and goats. Today, in the late twentieth century, the Chang-pa all over Tibet are increasing their stocks of goats vis-h-vis sheep, to meet the world-wide de­ mand for ‘cashmere’. But up to the middle of the last century, there was only a single outlet for this delicate fibre, and that was Kashmir. Like the trading communities of the east and central Himalaya, the Ladakhi subsistence traders too dealt primarily in salt, sheep’s wool and foodgrains. But they also dealt in pashm, a high-value luxury item, for which on resale they were paid in cash. This gave the trade a whole different dimension. The importanceof the pashm trade was such as to have a decisive effect on Ladakh’s political destiny. Among the most important clauses of the 1684 tripartite Treaty of Tingmosgang, between Ladakh, Tibet and Kashmir, was the one regulating the trade, and giving Kashmir a virtual monopoly of the precious wool. Right from its production by the nomadic Chang-pa on the windswept plateaux of western Tibet, it was to be handled by Kashmiris. One group of merchants was to be stationed at Spituk, near Leh, and only they were to be permitted to lift it from western Tibet and bring it down to Ladakh, where they had to resell it to other Kashmiris from Srinagar. Both segments of the trade soon came to be the monopoly of a few mercantile families. Even today there are Muslim families of Kashmiri origin in Leh and Chushot, who were dealing in pashm right up to I960, when the Tibet border was closed, and some of whom enjoyed special privileges in Tibet, presumably a vestige of their former semi-official status. And in Srinagar there are still merchants known as Tibet Baqals, dealing in pashm from Ladakh, the successors of the small group that for close on three centuries monopolized the trade in pashm between Ladakh and Kashmir. It was no doubt in order to lay his hands on this lucrative trade that Gulab Singh undertook the conquest of Ladakh, and later invaded Tibet. But, although the Treaty of Leh in 1842 purported to restore the status quo ante, in the long run the Kashmiri monop oly established by the Tingmosgang Treaty could hardly be re-es- tablished. On the one hand, the direct route from western Tibet to the Punjab, through what is now Himachal Pradesh, which had been opened up in the 1820s and 1830s, could not now be plugged, and over the next few decades merchants from Rampur Bashahr, an important market-town north-east of Shimla on the

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 99 Sutlej, established a presence at Gartok, the main trading centre in western Tibet, from where they carried pashm directly to the Punjab. On the other hand, the monopoly enjoyed by the Leh merchants of Kashmiri origin was gradually breached by a few rich Buddhist merchants of Leh, and by the farmers of the Sham Ilaqa, the name given to the Indus valley below Leh, who claimed the right to carry foodgrains and other necessities of life up to western Tibet, and deal with the Chang-pa in pashm as well as in wool and salt. The peasant traders of these villages — Nyemo, Nyurla, Ting­ mosgang, Khaltse and beyond — known as Shamma, dealt more in sheep’s wool than in pashm, their average purchase of the latter commodity being in the region of200 to 400 kilograms. But their very numbers, estimated at 300 to 400 in this century, meant that this represented a substantial amount. One remnant of the old Kashmiri Muslim monopoly remained: the Shamma apparendy had to travel much farther than the Leh area merchants, many of them to a district called Gertse, a 20-day march beyond the Tibet border. Probably they were not formally prohibited from doing business in the district of Rudok immediately adjoining Ladakh; but by the time they broke into the trade, the Leh merchants must have established a network of business relationships with the Rudok Chang-pa, who had little left over for the Shamma. The latter, accordingly, had to undertake a trek of iVi months out and 1 Vi months back, carrying their trade goods on strings of donkeys, and often driving a flock of chang-luky the big-boned sheep of Chang-thang, in front of them on their return. Their first halt once they got back to Ladakh was at Leh, to meet the Tibet Baqals, and sell their pashm for cash. The latter drove a hard bargain, and though the Shamma seem to have enjoyed profits of 100 to 200 per cent, this seems modest enough when considered against the expenditure of time and effort that went into the operation. The trade in pashm no doubt benefited not only the Shamma themselves, but also, by increasing their purchasing power, the communities of their villages as well. But it was a middlemen’s trade, by no means basic to the Ladakhi lifestyle. The product of Tibet that was crucial was salt, which was necessary to feed to the livestock, as well as to season human food and tea. Most of the salt that came into Ladakh was brought down not by the Shamma,

1 0 0 LADAKH but by the Chang-pa themselves, who collected it from the salt lakes, and carried it on the backs of thousands and thousands of sheep. They came every year in October, but only as far as the Chemrey-Sakti valley some 40 kilometres above Leh, at the foot of the Chang-la, which was the last area where the mountain slopes had enough grass to provide pasturage for their animals. There they met and did business not only with the Shamma — who after selling their pashm in Leh, and depositing their purchase of Chang-thang sheep’s wool at their village homes had retraced their steps back to Chemrey — but also with villagers from all over Ladakh almost as far as Kargil, and even from Baltistan. These villagers had brought whatever grain they could spare from their own fields, loaded on to horses and donkeys or — the very poorest of them — hefted on to their own backs. The barter rate seems to have remained steady over many years, fluctuating around one measure of barley to two of salt, by volume. The ordinary villagers would purchase just enough salt for their own consumption, and perhaps a little over for resale locally. But the Shamma, enterpris­ ing businessmen that they were, bought in bulk, and carried it far beyond their own homes, into the Suru valley, and as far afield as Baltistan. There they bartered it once more for grain, or for dried apricots, which fetched them a good price in cash either in Srinagar, or in Leh, where they found ready buyers in the traders from central Tibet. Thus, making a profit on every transaction, the Shamma criss-crossed the whole of Ladakh, reaching out to the neighbouring countries, spreading the surpluses of particular areas, and making up the deficiencies of others. Some of them were away from their villages for up to ten months out of twelve, preferring to winter with their donkeys in Baltistan, where living was cheaper. This was possible because of the joint family system. Only one adult male from each family went trading, and usually there would be enough people in the household — brothers, wife, unmarried sisters, even children and aged parents — to pitch in and get the field-work done. Zanskar, with its scanty population and its secluded position, was supplied with salt by the Chang-pa of Rupshu, whose twice- yearly trips almost as far as Padum continued till well into the 1980s. Some of this salt found its way, by barter and re-barter, to the Suru valley, and even over the Great Himalaya to Kashmir and Kishtwar, where it paid for semi-luxuries like tea and rice.

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 1 0 1 Thus, quite apart from pashm, even the subsistence trades of Ladakh were carried on according to an amazingly complex pat­ tern. The Shamma may perhaps not have been in the same league as the Sherpa, the Thakali and the Nyishangba of Nepal, the Bhotias of Kumaon, and the Bashahris and the Lahulis of what is now Himachal Pradesh. But as shrewd and energetic businessmen with an eye to the main chance, they were clearly cast in the same mould. * Pashm and salt apart, trade with Tibet was limited, and that with Lhasa remained largely based on two official missions established under the Treaty of Tingmosgang. The Lo-pchak Mission from Leh to Lhasa went every three years, carrying gold, saffron and textiles as offerings to the Dalai Lama, as well as gifts for other important lamas. As well as these ceremonial offerings, the Lo- pchak was permitted to carry 200 animal-loads of trade goods, for which free porterage was provided by the Tibetan authorities from Demchok on the border right up to Lhasa. Thus it could, properly managed, yield a considerable profit to those running it. Interest­ ingly, there was no stipulation that these should be Buddhists, and after the loss of Ladakh’s independence, when the responsibility of sending it was taken on by the Government of Jammu and Kash­ mir, it was for many years conducted by one particular family of Muslim merchants named Radhu. It may be safely assumed that the fortunes of this family were substantially augmented by the near-monopoly they held for many years over the Lo-pchak, but they were already prosperous and successful traders when they took it up. By the beginning of this century their business empire extended not only between Leh and Lhasa, but to Shigatse and Gartok in Tibet, Yarkand and Kashgar in Sinkiang, and Srinagar in Kashmir. Branches in all these cities were run by members of thef extended family, whose trade was said in 1906 to bring in a net annual profit of Rs 25,000 — a very pretty sum at that time. During this century there was only one other family in Leh doing business on anything like the same scale, Buddhists whose capital was derived from their extensive properties, including thousands of head of livestock kept for them by Chang-pa in western Tibet. They conducted the Lo-pchak a couple of times, and even took the occasional private caravan to Lhasa.

1 0 2 LADAKH In the other direction, an annual trade mission came from Lhasa to Leh, authorized by the Government of Tibet, and carrying mainly tea — a minimum of 200 animal-loads was stipulated by the Treaty of Tingmosgang. For this reason it came to be known as the Cha-pa caravan. The tea was carried in the easily portable form of ‘bricks’ of dampened and compressed tea-leaves. As any­ one who has lived in Ladakh may guess, the greater part of the Cha-pa’s 200 loads of tea was not re-exported, but kept for consumption within the country; nevertheless, till well into the nineteenth century, Kashmir’s entire consumption of tea was supplied from Ladakh. The leader of the Cha-pa mission, whose title was actually Zhung-stong, meaning official trader, was given a ceremonial welcome by the representative of the former royal family, as part of the Losar (New Year) ceremonies held in the Leh Palace every year up to the 1940s. The main commodity he carried back was dried apricots. But without the facilities afforded by the special status of the official missions, direct trade between Leh and Lhasa was such an enormous undertaking that few businesses were prepared to get into it, and those that did, only occasionally. Travel time between the two centres was two-and-a-half months, at least for the slow-moving caravans, and much of the way was through uninhabited country. It was impossible to time it so as to avoid making at least one leg of the journey in winter when not only was the weather bitterly, numbingly cold, but blizzards could set in, severe enough to force a caravan to turn back. As if that were not enough, the roads — such as they were — and the plateaux of Tibet were infested with brigands. These ruffians kept clear of the Lo-pchak, on account of its special religious and political status, but they reckoned the caravans of private traders fair game. They rarely killed, unless resisted, and often made a point of sparing enough horses or mules and supplies to enable those they had looted to make it to the nearest habitation. But an attack meant certain total loss of the merchandise. By the second quarter of this century, the establishment of Kalimpong as the principal centre of trade between India and Tibet, together with the development of modern systems of communication in India, had rendered the direct route between Leh and Lhasa practically irrelevant. Now the quickest and most

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 103 convenient way of travel between Leh and Lhasa was the detour all the way round via Srinagar, down to the railhead at Rawal­ pindi, and on by train to Siliguri, the railhead for Kalimpong, from where mule transport was available on hire for the rest of the journey. Trade with western Tibet in salt, foodgrains and pashm remained brisk till the 1950s, tailing off after the Chinese occupation of Tibet and finally coming to an end about I960. But there were no private caravans to Lhasa after the early 1930s, and the last Lo-pchak set off from Leh in 1942. But it was the long-distance trade in luxury goods between the Punjab and Sinkiang which really distinguished Ladakh’s eco­ nomy from that of other Himalayan and trans-Himalayan regions. Similar trades existed elsewhere, no doubt. Up to the middle of the eighteenth century India and Tibet maintained a busy com­ mercial relationship via the Kathmandu valley; and after a lull of nearly a century-and-a-half a similar trade was established in this century, via Kalimpong and Sikkim. Far to the west, the caravans used the Bolan, Guleri and Khyber Passes to ferry goods between Sind and the Punjab in India, and the various provinces of Af­ ghanistan. What made Ladakh’s situation unique was its mid-way position between trading centres so distant from each other that it took the caravans a full month from either end to reach Leh; and also the fact that the traffic along this most punishing of routes appears to have carried on for centuries without a break, the only interruption in the three hundred years of its recorded history apparendy being the twenty-five years or so following Sengge Namgyal’s closure of the border with Kashmir. This was not a trade from which Ladakh and Ladakhis profited directly to any great extent. The commodities being traded were neither produced in Ladakh, nor for the most part consumed there. Where it did make an impact was in the demand it created for transport and services, all along the routes, but especially in Leh and its hinterland, Nubra, and the Dras-Kargil belt. It also attracted to Leh merchants from central Asia, Kashmir and the Punjab, §ome of whom settled there, giving the little town a cosmopolitan atmosphere, faint whiffs of which linger yet. In the early years of this century, Leh became one of the headquarters of a community of traders from Hoshiarpur — the first town in

104 LADAKH the plains at the end of the Kulu route from Leh to the Punjab — whose main business was in charas from Yarkand. As the trans-Karakoram trade dwindled in the 1930s and 40s, some of them opened retail shops in Leh, several of which are still extant. The Hoshiarpuris retain their links with their home town and few of them have settled permanently in Ladakh. Some of the Muslim merchants from Sinkiang and Kashmir however married Ladakhi women and made their homes in Leh; their descendants, among whom are the Radhu family, form a distinct community known as Arghon. In their houses or — more accessible to the visitor — in the antique shops of the bazaar may be found copper jars, plates and cooking pots of exquisite workmanship, and other artefacts in the great tradition of Islamic design, which have surely arrived there as part of the baggage of some caravan from Srinagar or Yarkand. The great dish of Ladakhi haute cuisine (which otherwise shows traces of Chinese influence derived from the Tibetan con­ nection, relying largely on noodles and meat-filled dumplings or mok-mok) is Yarkandi pulatc rice richly cooked with meat and spices, a creation again in the great Islamic tradition. The origins of the trade are lost in the mists of time; but it seems likely that it is of great antiquity. Yarkand and Khotan, its termini in Sinkiang, were important staging posts on the southern branch of the fabled ‘silk route’ between China and the Mediter­ ranean, and appalling though the hardships of the Ladakh-Kara- koram route between the Punjab and Yarkand might be — a journey involving a minimum of two months travel time — there was in fact no feasible long-term alternative. West of Ladakh, the passes over the watershed in Baltistan, Hunza and Chitral were either encumbered with glaciers, or issued in impassable gorges; or else they were infested by bandits and slave-traffickers. To the east, the Tibetan authorities were paranoid about keeping out all foreigners; while the high plateaux of Aksai Chin and Lingzithang were uninhabited and totally lacking in any kind of facility for caravans, or even grass for the horses. Thus over the centuries the caravans congregated at Leh to prepare for the crossing of the Karakoram, or to recover from it; and gradually there, and at the other staging posts along the different lines of march, a network of serais and other facilities was established, which further rein­ forced the advantages of the route. As time went on, the tide of commerce over the Karakoram

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 105 ebbed and flowed, sensitive like all long-distance trades to politi­ cal developments, but strong enough to survive all vicissitudes until the revolutions and other radical changes of this century destroyed its very base. Moorcroft was intensely interested in it, and the establishment of commercial relations with Yarkand through Leh was a cherished, though subsidiary, aim of his journey. He lambasted the East India Company’s Government for its ‘misplaced squeamishness and unnecessary timidity’ in neglecting the opportunity his presence afforded to do this, thus leaving the whole vast area of ‘Central Asia, from China to the Caspian Sea’, to which he conceived Yarkand to be the gateway, wide open for economic penetration by Russia. On his own responsibility, acting ‘on behalf of the British merchants of Cal­ cutta’, he even concluded a commercial agreement with the Ladakhi authorities, who granted the former freedom to trade with and through Ladakh at moderate duties, and permission to establish a permanent ‘factory’ or office at Leh. Moorcroft en­ visaged from this a flood of British goods swamping the markets of central Asia’s illimitable expanses; little did he realize that it Was not only the Company’s apathy that was against the expan­ sion of trade on such a vast scale, but also the incredible severity of the Karakoram route, something of which he seems to have had no realistic conception. It was not until the 1860s that India’s British rulers came round to Moorcroft’s view. Even then their interest was less in the trade as such, rather in the possibilities it might offer as a means of establishing a sphere of influence in eastern central Asia, then temporarily independent of China, to counter the threat of Rus­ sian expansion in that direction. The two diplomatic missions that toiled over the Karakoram to Yarkand and Kashgar in 1870 and 1873, with the aim of concluding a commercial treaty with Yakub Beg, the de facto ruler; the high hopes with which they set out, and their total lack of success, constitute one of the more bizarre and entertaining episodes in the history of the British imperial adventure.1 Even if successful, the effort would have been in vain, for by the end of the 1870s the Chinese were back, this time for good. The perceived Russian threat from that direction being thus eliminated, official interest in the trade waned rapidly, except among a few enthusiasts in the Moorcroft tradition. Foremost among these was Robert Shaw, the first British person

106 LADAKH indeed to travel to Yarkand and Kashgar, whose initial journey had done much to stimulate the official perceptions of the diplomatic possibilities of the trade. His vision found expression in the foun­ dation of the Central Asian Trading Company, although this survived only a few years, Shaw’s agent, a Scot called Andrew Dalgleish, carried on trading on his own account. Dalgleish led an adventurous life, engaging in exploration as well as trade, marrying a Yarkandi wife, and even, it seems, dabbling in espionage. For fourteen years he was actively engaged in the trans-Karakoram trade, himself regularly struggling across the fearsome passes with his caravans. It was on one of these expeditions, in 1888, that he met his death, not from cold or exposure, but (an unnecessary additional hazard, it might seem, on this most punishing of moun­ tain routes) from the bullet and knife of an assassin. Dalgleish’s caravan fell in with another small party a few marches short of the Karakoram Pass, and they travelled on together as far as the camp­ ing ground just over the pass. An acquaintance in the other party, an Afghan with a shady reputation called Daud or Dad Moham­ mad, invited Dalgleish to his own tent; and there shot and hacked him to death. The motive for the murder was never established. Daud Mohammad was arrested for it two years later in Samarkand, but died in prison — by his own hand, according to the Russian authorities there — before he could be brought to trial, or throw any light on his action. A memorial to Dalgleish was erected on the spot where he was murdered; his body was brought to Leh, and buried in the Christian cemetery. Thus the first and only Briton to engage in the trade across the Karakoram met an untimely and mysterious end, though his body was not left among the bones that whitened along the Yarkand trail. The only tangible result of the diplomatic initiatives of the early 1870s was that the British, having got into the act, established a presence in Leh, which they maintained right up to 1947. This presence was embodied in an officer of the Political Department, attached to the office of the Resident in Kashmir, and enjoying the designation of British Joint Commissioner. He spent three months of every year in Ladakh, based at Leh, where he had a local official called the Aksakal working under him. His primary duty was to look after the interests of the traders, both Indian and foreign; he also toured extensively overseeing the maintenance of the trade routes. From the late 1890s, the Srinagar-Leh-Karakoram route

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 107 was designated a free highway under the commercial treaty of 1870 between the British and the Maharaja of Jammu and Kashmir; even now eiderly Ladakhis refer to it as the Treaty Road. The trade seems to have been at its height in the second and third decades of this century, when 10,000 horses are said to have passed through Nubra in one direction or the other every year. The commodities being traded varied over time, but in general — in the nature of things — they consisted of merchandise low in bulk and high in value. Although the Leh—Yarkand route was not (contrary to popular misconception) the Silk Route, it was pre­ sumably one of the Silk Route feeders in the early centuries when silk was actually passing through Yarkand and the other oasis towns of south Sinkiang on its way from China to the Mediterranean. And during the 125 years of the Karakoram route’s recorded history, silk was in fact an important item of commerce, going from India to central Asia in the form of Banaras brocades and other woven fabrics, and from central Asia to India mainly in the form of yarn for the weaving towns of the Punjab. Other important commodities at different times were — from India, cotton piece- goods, leather and skins, Western medicines, spices, and dyestuffs, including indigo, for the south Sinkiang carpet industry; and from central Asia, woven and felt carpets, mosdy bound for Srinagar, assorted European textiles, tea, pashm, precious stone? and jewel­ lery, and coarse cotton cloth for consumption in Ladakh itself. For about a hundred years, the Punjabi merchants made enormous profits from the import of charas, this was the only commodity that the Government took an active interest in, monitoring its movement from the moment it entered Ladakh till it was dis­ tributed to the retailers from special warehouses in Hoshiarpur and Rawalpindi, and taking their cut via duties. For a short time around the middle of the nineteenth century, indeed, when the main item of export from India to Chinese central Asia was opium from the Kangra hills, the trade would seem to have consisted mainly of an exchange of narcotics. At most periods, the balance of the trade was in India’s favour, and was made up by imports of bullion from Yarkand, much of it in the form of silver ingots. There was no wheeled traffic in Ladakh until the 1960s; most of the transport across the Karakoram was on pack-horses, especially

108 LADAKH the big-boned Yarkand breed, which could carry loads of about 100 kilograms. But the shaggy double-humped Bactrian camel, the principal pack-animal of inner Asia, whose load-bearing capa­ city was almost twice that of a horse, was also used a good deal, especially on the northern part of the route, beyond the Kara­ koram Pass. Its performance on the mountain trails was magnificent, and it seems to have been unaffected by the rarefied air at 5000 metres and above; but it found the glaciers that encumbered some of the passes hard to cope with, and thus it was only occasionally that it came all the way. Great was the excitement in Leh on the rare occasions when a camel caravan entered the bazaar; school children played truant in order to run to the serai to see this marvel. A few enterprising traders in central Ladakh and Nubra acquired camels for local use; they failed to thrive in the Leh area, but conditions in Nubra seem to suit them and a small population survives there, proving their worth as pack- animals and — now that Nubra is open to outside visitors — being exploited as a tourist attraction. As well as horses and camels, yak were also used on the Kara­ koram route, having a specialized function. Three of the passes on the summer route were more or less heavily glaciated: the Khardung-la just behind Leh, the Saser-la, which took the route over the Saser Spur from the Nubra to the upper Shayok valley, and the Sanju-la over a ridge of the Kun-lun in Sinkiang. It may not be impossible to lead laden horses over the fissured surface of glaciers, but it is an enterprise fraught with danger, to both the animals and their burdens. The yak however took the rough with the smooth; and, trans-shipped on to their broad backs, the merchandise was carried safely up the most boulder-strewn mo­ raines, over the steepest and iciest of slopes, the horses being led across unladen. Yak were also used to push through the snow and open a trail over the passes at the beginning of the season. The people of the high-altitude villages near the foot of such passes were able to supplement the meagre income from their fields by hiring out yak to passing caravans. The pashm trade, and the subsistence trades in salt and food- grains, were carried partly on horseback, but more commonly by donkeys and sheep. The Shamma mostly used donkeys, the local breed being able to carry a load of about 50 kilograms. In spite of their diminutive size, they were reckoned the most suitable

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 109 animal for the work, as the soles of their feet were tough and able to withstand prolonged wear, and they were stout-hearted beasts which would carry on till they dropped. The Chang-pa who brought salt to Chemrey carried it on the sheep from their flocks — the handsome big-boned chang-luk. Each animal carried ten to 15 kilograms in a pair of woven bags slung across its back. These remained in place until the caravan reached its destination, and the Chang-pa were ready to dispose of the load of salt or grain. Lhasa-bound caravans consisted mostly of mules. As for the routes trodden by the patient animals with their burdens of salt, grain or pashm; charas, indigo, silk yarn or Banaras brocade — there were five major lines converging on Leh. The direct route up from the Punjab was the one that so many visitors take today, via Mandi, the Kulu valley, the Rohtang Pass, Lahul, the Bara- lacha, Lunga-lacha and Tag-lang Passes, to Upshi on the Indus, and thence down the river to Leh. This was Moorcroft and Trebeck’s route in 1820; it carried a busy traffic until the early years of this century, and was in occasional use by Punjabi traders right up till the 1950s. The trail from Srinagar was roughly the same as today’s motorroad; indeed there are places where the old way, diverging from the new, is clearly visible. But it was not till after the Dogra conquest that a proper track was made and bridges constructed. The Jesuit priest Ippolito Desideri, who travelled it in 1715, gives an idea of what it was like then. The rivers were bridged with jhoolas, a system of ropes made of plaited twigs suspended above the torrent, which made his head swim — trekkers in the interior of the Zanskar range may even today be obliged to negotiate precisely the same kind of jhoola. The road, never more than a mere indication, had in places been swept away by landslides so that it was necessary to cut steps with an axe. ‘In some places there was really no road at all, only large boulders and rocks covered the ground over which we had to climb like goats with great trouble and difficulty. As no animal can travel over such bad roads, the whole journey from Kaschimir to Lhata [Leh], which takes forty days, must be done on foot.’2 And this was the pashm trail, along which travelled every last wisp of the precious wool used in the manufacture of shawls famous the world over for their softness, warmth and rich yet delicate design.

1 1 0 LADAKH From Baitistan north-west of Ladakh, the principal route was up the Shayok from its confluence with the Indus, then over the Chorbat-la (5100 metres) and down the Hanu river to rejoin the Indus below Khaltse; there was also another link from Skardu, straight up the Indus to its confluence with the Suru-Shingo, and on to Kargil. Both routes were appalling, the road at many places as it crept round cliff-faces taking the form of rickety wooden galleries supported in space far above the turbulent river waters by struts driven into crevices in the rock-face. Even the adven­ turous Shamma, taking Chang-thang wool and salt to Baitistan to barter for dried apricots, took care to select only the strongest of their donkeys for the trip. But even the rough and fragmented roads of western Ladakh were small beer compared to the hardships and difficulties of the way across the Karakoram to Yarkand. This actually involved two routes, for use in summer and in winter. In spite of the bitter weather, many traders preferred to travel during the cold season. In summer, streams and rivers were torrents, swollen with melt water from the glaciers, rampaging down their gorges and impos­ sible to negotiate. But the chill of winter froze them up, or reduced them to a mere trickle. Thus the winter route followed the river valleys — up the Shayok and down the Yarkand river — and avoided the worst of the passes. Travellers in summer had no choice but to tackle the mighty ranges head on, and this meant getting across no fewer than six major passes, including the three glaciated ones, the Khardung-la, the Saser-la and the Sanju-la. Summer or winter, there was no way (except by opting for the Aksai Chin route, not really a viable alternative) of avoiding the Karakoram Pass, highest of all at 5575 metres. But the Karakoram in fact, its terrific elevation notwithstanding, was an easy pass, situated at a point on the crest-line where, by some freak of microclimate, there was only moderate snowfall, even in winter. One of the worst stages on the summer route was the Depsang Plain, a couple of marches south of the Karakoram Pass, at about 5400 metres, the highest plateau of its size in the world. It was described with uncharacteristic vividness in an official document: ‘this bleak barren undulating plateau, from which the world around subsides, the highest hilltops only appearing above the horizon.’ Although the going underfoot was not difficult, the rarefaction of the air put a tremendous strain on the breathing of

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 1 1 1 man and beast alike. For a hundred miles on each side of the Karakoram Pass, it was impossible to stray from the proper path which was marked by the bleached bones of horses that had succumbed to altitude and exhaustion. The route took its toll of human life as well, and it is said that no ponyman left Yarkand for Leh without including a shroud among his personal effects. The route from Leh to Lhasa led either along the Indus, or over the Tag-lang-la and across the Rupshu plateau, to Demchok on the border; it continued up the Indus to Gartok, past the pilgrim destinations of Mount Kailash and Lake Manasarowar, and con­ tinued over the Mayum-la and down the Tsang-po to Shigatse and Lhasa. In contrast with the routes to the west and north of Ladakh, the difficulties and hardships of the journey lay less in the roughness of the trails than in its very length, and in the appalling weather that might be encountered on the way, to say nothing of the brigands. Desolate it might be, but at least grass and water were available at almost every halting place. The same was true of the more northerly line — via the Chang-la, Chushul and Rudok — taken by the Shamma and other pashm traders. There is quite a lot of precipitation over western Tibet; and though the valleys and plateaux are divided by range upon range of mountains, these are not so high or so forbidding as to make travel difficult. In summer, indeed, as some of the former traders recol­ lect it today, there trading journeys were like a picnic. The weather remained mainly clear, and even the well-to-do Leh merchants who carried tents only bothered to pitch them during the oc­ casional rainy spell. Wildlife was abundant, and had little fear of humans, since the Government of Tibet forbade hunting. The streams teemed with fish, to the extent that when you drew water, the bucket was filled with them and you had to strain them out and throw them back. Ducks, cranes and other migratory birds were legion. All these trades — whether in luxury goods between regions far distant from each other, or in subsistence commodities between adjacent economic zones — carried on for centuries, if not mil­ lennia; and it is not fifty years since they came to an end. The last Lo-pchak Mission went in the early 1940s, and the Cha-pa stopped coming a year or two later. The Karakoram route was

1 1 2 LADAKH sealed in 1949 in the wake of China’s communist revolution; after that only a couple of caravans of refugees came across, the last one in 1953. Traders kept on going to western Tibet and bringing back pashm almost till 1960, in spite of an increasing Chinese presence there; than that trade too folded. But caravans of sheep carrying loads of salt were coming regularly into Zanskar from Rupshu as recently as the mid-1980s. The different trades and their demands led to the development of classes of men whose whole way of life was trade and travel. The Shamma are only one example. Besides the traders themselves, drawn from a wide area of north India and central Asia, there were also professional ponymen known as kiraiyakash in Leh, Nubra and Yarkand, who might have traversed the Karakoram route, or parts of it, dozens of times. In the tract between Dras and Kargil, one of Ladakh’s poorest regions, the only thing that lifted most families above the level of bare subsistence was the carrying trade between Srinagar and Leh, of which, in the first half of this century, these people had a virtual monopoly. From May to November, they ferried goods on their strings of horses between the two centres; and in winter, when the Zoji-la was snowbound, they made frequent crossings on foot as far as Sonamarg, during breaks in the weather, carrying on their backs loads of merchandise for merchants who had mistimed their journey and got stranded at Dras. It is said that sometimes they even carried people — the women and children of parties of Mecca-bound hajis from Yarkand — across the pass on their backs. All over Ladakh there is still a scattering of men, in Leh and Nubra, in Saspol, Nyurla and Khaltse, in Mulbekh, Kargil, Dras, the Suru valley and Zanskar, who spent much of their early lives on the trails, with their caravans of horses, donkeys or sheep. They bound the whole region together in a complex network of sale, purchase and exchange that spread the surpluses of particular areas, and made up the deficits of others. Today the trails are deserted, except where they coincide with the arteries of modern traffic; otherwise, almost the only movement along them is of trekkers and climbers pitting their strength against the grandeur of the mountains and serviced by the successors of the traders of old. This too represents a kind of economic activity; but it is one over which the local people have little control. If the visitors come,

HAZARDOUS TRAILS 113 they will earn; if for some reason (as in Kashmir) the stream of tourists dries up, they will be in trouble according to the extent of their dependence on this source of income. Trade in old Ladakh was an essential element in the fabric of life; and though the expense of time and effort that it involved was clearly enormous, so indeed was the difference it reliably made to the prosperity of both the country and its people. Notes and References 1. Unfortunately though understandably, the participants themselves did not see it in quite this light, and the first-hand accounts of the expeditions are dead boring. The most concise and entertaining account that has come to hand is that in John Keay, When Men and Mountains Meet 2. Desideri may possibly have underestimated the capacity of the sure-footed mountain horses to tackle trails as rough and broken as those he describes. But thejhoola bridges were certainly not negotiable by horses, which would have to take to the water to cross the rivers, at the risk of getting their loads wet. Alternatively, the loads would be taken off the horses and carried across by the porters or ponymen. It was probably to obviate the danger of the delicate fibre getting wet that, as Desideri’s categorical statement is confirmed by Godfrey Vigne 130 years later, at least all the pashm was carried down on men’s backs.

Ladakh Present 7 The Daily Round T he loose suzerainty claimed rather than exercised by the Mughal empire from the seventeenth century onwards was transformed via the Dogra conquest and the estab­ lishment of the Jammu and Kashmir state into the situation in which Ladakh finds itself today, as an integral part of the Indian Union. Till 1979 it was a single district; in June of that year it was bifurcated into the two districts of Leh and Kargil. The boundary between them runs north-west along the line of the Zanskar range, so that the Zanskar valley is included in Kargil district, as well as Dras, and Purig, the area within striking distance of Kargil town, comprising the Suru valley, Mulbekh and Bodh Kharbu. A little beyond the Fatu-la the boundary turns north to cross the Indus, so that most of the Sodh Ilaqa, the area along the river below Khaltse where the Brokpa, the Buddhist Dards live, also falls in Kargil. Leh district includes the Indus valley from Khaltse upstream, Nubra and Rupshu, together with the western part of the Pang-gong Lake, and (notionally at least) the Changchenmo valley and the high-altitude plateaux of Lingzithang and Aksai Chin. The total area of the two districts is about 98,000 square kilometres; and their population may now be over 170,000 (132,299 by the 1981 census),1 giving a popula­ tion density of over 1.7 per square kilometre.

THE DAILY ROUND 115 On the outskirts o f Leh, a field is ploughed before the onset o f winter. An immem­ orial scene, but for the newly built glass-room on the house at the field ’s edge. But this is actually an unrealistic way of looking at it, consider­ ing that the greater part of the region is uninhabitable, most of the population being clustered together in the small enclaves where cultivation is possible. For a start, a considerable area (37,500 square kilometres, according to the 1971 census) in the north-eastern corner, the plains of Aksai Chin and Lingzithang, is under the illegal occupation of the Chinese. However, these high-altitude plateaux are a desert, incapable of supporting human life; their loss does not affect the real issue, which is the area of cultivable land. Of the 60,500 square kilometres that remain when the Aksai Chin is left out of account, less than 300 square kilometres are under crops, vegetables or fruit; thus the pressure of the population on the cultivated area is very great — almost 600 per square kilometre. This of course includes the population of the two towns, and the nomadic population of Chang-thang, who do not live directly off the land. But the townsman and the nomad have to eat; ultimately their food too comes from the earth.

1 1 6 LADAKH As in every other region of India, Ladakh’s population has grown during the present century, rising from 63,519 by the census of 1901, to 105,291 in 1971, a moderate increase of 66 per cent in 70 years, which contrasts with the alarming 25 per cent increase between 1971 and 1981. Assuming that the amount of cultivable land today is roughly the same as that available in 1901, it means that at that date each square kilometre had to support about 300 persons; while by 1941 with the population registering almost exacdy 76,000, the pressure on the fields had gone up to nearly 350 per square kilometre. There are Ladakhis with a clear recollection of conditions before 1947-8 (when the present phase of Ladakh’s history may be said to have begun), who are emphatic in their assertion that apart from small quan­ tities of rice, which is regarded as a luxury article, there was no import of foodgrains up to that date. But the figures given above cast some doubt on the correctness of this; indeed, the early writers state categorically that Ladakh is not self-sufficient in food. Moorcroft in the early 1820s tells us that the Ladakhis ‘rear an inadequate supply of food’, and that grain is imported from Kashmir, the Punjab and the provinces south of the Himalaya; and Cunningham, including Lahul and Spiti in his calculations, gives figures to prove the impossibility of Ladakh producing enough food to sustain its inhabitants, estimating the total annual value of grain imported at a little less than 250,000 rupees. Research into the complex pattern of the inter-regional subsistence trades in the first half of this century seems to show that, while there was no large-scale organized import of foodgrains, those peasant traders whose circuit included Kashmir and Kishtwar, regions south of the Great Himalaya, did regularly bring back not only rice, but also maize and a selection of coarse grains for everyday subsistence, like buckwheat. Considering that the fields of Ladakh supported not only the local population, but also an estimated 20,000 Chang-pa in western Tibet as well as in the south-eastern parts of Ladakh itself, it is difficult to believe that there can have been no import of foodgrains at all, even though this was not highly visible. Various factors have contributed to keeping the population relatively low. There were social constraints: according to Cun­ ningham’s estimate in 1847, something like seven per cent of the population of both sexes chose the religious life and became monks

THE DAILY ROUND 117 or nuns, remaining celibate. The relative prosperity of Ladakh compared to neighbouring Baltistan where because of Islam poly­ gamy was practised, was accounted for by nineteenth-century travellers as the effect of polyandry. This indeed, as part of a marital system of great flexibility,2 seems to have operated as a balance between the demand for labour on the land, and the danger of over-population. But there was in addition a high rate of infant and child mortality, showing that in the past the harsh­ ness of the environment acted as a check on the population*, so that it at no time outstripped the resources necessary to provide every individual with the means of survival. Obviously the cruel climate makes the level of basic necessities much higher for a Ladakhi than for a peasant in, for instance, north Bihar. Thus what seems like a modest degree of prosperity actually represents, as much as the poverty of the plains, the threshold of survival. Outside Leh and Kargil, the economic and everyday life of Ladakhis has not yet altered radically, though every year brings more changes. To describe it briefly is no easy matter, for there are great differences among the different areas of the two districts. In the two towns, for instance, there exists a small class of families, both representatives of the old aristocracy and others, who are wealthy by any standards. They made their fortunes in the distant and not-so-distant past, by the time-honoured and universal methods of Government service at the highest levels; ownership of land; trade; and most recendy, as contractors to Government in the large-scale programme of public works that has been going on ever since the construction of the Srinagar-Leh highway in the early 1960s. The life led by these families is comfortable, but not ostentatious; many of them are now using their capital to invest in Ladakh’s most recent source of profit, the tourist trade. The ordinary people of the countryside, peasants and herds­ men, live scattered over an area of 60,000 square kilometres, at altitudes from 2700 metres to 4500 metres above sea-level. Such a range of altitude naturally implies tremendous climatic and environmental variations, which in turn impose great differences on the people’s way of life. To take extremes, there is little in common between the Sham Ilaqa and Sodh Ilaqa, the lower regions down the Indus, between 2700 and 3200 metres, where summer is long enough for double cropping and autumn sees a rich harvest of fruit, and the bleak upland plateaux of Chushul

118 LADAKH and Rupshu, where winter lasts for six or eight months, and temperatures may fall to minus 40"; where there is no cultivation worth speaking of, and the nomads herd their flocks of sheep and pashmina goats in the open entirely at the m6rcy of the weather. The other desperately poor region is Dras which, with its heavy snowfall and long winter, can harvest only one crop; it has the added problem of a severe shortage of firewood for winter fuel. Although it is on the main road into Ladakh, few benefits accrue to it from the traffic across it, for unless there is a blockage on the road the tourists and truck-drivers stop at Dras only long enough to refresh themselves with a cup of tea. Historically, indeed, Dras has been impoverished by its position on the road to Kashmir. As reported by Moorcroft, its inhabitants were subject to a system of forced labour for porterage for which they got no recompense, the profits of their labour going to their joint suzerains, the king of Ladakh, and the malik, or lord, of the neighbouring Kashmir area of Sonamarg. In the first half of this century, the carrying trade between Srinagar and Leh, of which the people of Dras and the neighbouring villages almost down to Kargil had a near monopoly, did a lot to bridge the subsistence gap. Zanskar, while it shares some of the characteristics of Dras — a long hard winter with heavy snowfall — is relatively better off. It is one of the few regions where even now all possibly cultivable land has not yet been brought under the plough; and with its small population, the amount of cropping and livestock is enough to have made it a surplus area. Zanskar butter is still exported to Leh, and till the mid-1980s the Zanskaris were bartering grain from their fields with the Rupshu Chang-pa for salt. Rangdum, on the other hand, geographically belonging to the Suru valley, but socially and culturally part of Zanskar, seems to have remarkably little going for it. Except for the three months or so when the Pensi-la is open, it is on the road to nowhere; it is too bleak and exposed for crops to ripen reliably; and the climate is bitterly cold, with summer lasting only two or three months, and snow covering the ground to a depth of up to two metres for almost half the year. Lower down too the Suru valley shivers under shoulder-high snow during winter; but its lesser elevation, the availability of water, and the rich silty soil make it one of Ladakh’s most produc­ tive regions, with two crops a year being taken from the fields of

THE DAILY ROUND 119 the villages on its lower stretch, some 25 kilometres from Trespon and Saliskot down to Kargil. Downriver from Kargil the Suru, joined by the Dras-Shingo, flows through what is little better than a gorge; but occasional villages perched on ledges above the river produce some of the best of Ladakh’s apricots. Mulbekh, Bodh Kharbu and Shagkar-Chigtan which, together with the Suru valley, make up the old principality of Purig, are beyond the reach of the heavy snowfall along the northern flank of the Great Himalaya; at less than 3300 metres they are fairly productive, occupying a middle place in terms of the relative prosperity of the different regions. For die last three centuries, Leh has held an unchallenged position as the commercial as well as the administrative capital of Ladakh. The prosperity that the caravan trade brought to the town spilled over into its hinterland, compensating for the limita­ tions imposed on agriculture by the length and severity of winter. Nubra is one of the relatively kindly areas, comparable to the Sham Ilaqa, being situated slightly lower — between 3000 and 3200 metres — than the Leh area. It too benefited from its position on the main caravan route to Yarkand, many of its fields in the old days being given over to the cultivation of lucerne (known in Ladakhi as ol), which the villagers sold at a consid­ erable profit to the passing caravans as fodder for the horses. In addition, its extensive thickets of hypophae (seabuckthorn), which makes excellent Riel as well as fencing, obviate the necessity to use animal dung as fuel, freeing it for more appropriate use as natural fertilizer. In regions where cultivation is possible, it is broadly true to say that agriculture is the main occupation. Below 3000 metres wheat is grown, while elsewhere the staple crop is naked barley. Known in Ladakhi as grim, it comes in several varieties appropriate to different soil and climatic conditions, and is often sown in rotation with peas, a nitrogen-fixing crop which contributes to the fertility of the soil. In the Sham Ilaqa and the Sodh Ilaqa — say from Saspol downriver to where the Indus leaves Ladakh — and in parts of Purig, cultivation of grain is supplemented by fruit-grow- ing; these are the homes of the apricot, the only cash crop and, apart from pashm, the only product exported from Ladakh in any quantity. Wherever apricots grow, apples can too; and in the Sodh Ilaqa there are even mulberries, a particularly sweet variety of

1 2 0 LADAKH melon, and grapes from which the Brokpa make a perfectly palatable white wine. The Ladakhi farming family, conscious of the limitations under which they have to work, husband their resources with extreme care. Wherever the availability of firewood makes them inde­ pendent of animal dung for fuel, they make a point of keeping their animals to graze among the stubble for as long as possible after the harvest, so as to take advantage of the natural manure they give; this indeed is one of the main objects of livestock-rear- ing, since there is no other means, in this cold dry climate, of recycling crop residues back into the soil. Their system of sanita­ tion is geared to the same end. Ladakhis use dry closets built a few feet above the ground, in which the refuse, falling through a hole, is collected in a lower chamber, from which it is removed at the end of winter and taken out to the fields. In the cold dry climate it decomposes cleanly, without any offensive odour. After the harvest, the fields are ploughed once before the earth hardens so much in the grip of winter as to become unworkable. As soon as the streams and irrigation channels unfreeze in the spring, the fields are watered, then ploughed, manured and ploughed again. Sowing may be done broadcast or in the furrow, and is followed by a period of intense activity, for weeding and watering have to be done regularly. The water is snowmelt, taken from mountain streams, and conducted to the crops by a system of channels and sub-channels, the flow being regulated simply by large clods of earth placed across them. Weeding starts about three weeks after the seed has been sown; as well as direcdy benefiting the growing grain, it supplements the supply of fodder available for the livestock. The work of weeding and watering is done mosdy by women, who apart from ploughing can and do undertake every kind of activity connected with agriculture — a custom carried over, perhaps, from the days when a considerable number of the able-bodied men left their homes in summer to go trading, or employed as porters and pony-drivers with the caravans. The results by any standards other than those of modern mech­ anized and scientific agriculture are superlative. Moorcroft, who knew about such things, was full of praise for the crops he saw. ‘In no other country have I seen an equal surface in barley as regularly covered with plant, and never plants with better heads.’3

THE DAILY ROUND 1 2 1 This he attributed partly to the extremes of temperature to which the plants were exposed in this harsh climate, combined with the dry atmosphere and the irrigation with very cold water; but partly also to the care taken in preparing the fields and in looking after the growing plants. Talking of a particularly splendid crop near Bodh Kharbu, he declared, ‘It was the finest crop I ever beheld; and a spirited English farmer would have thought himself sufficiently repaid for a ride of many miles by a sight of it.’4 High praise indeed from a man of post-agricultural-revolution England, whose profession as a vet must earlier have taken him riding long distances among the fields and farms of rural England. Conditions in many of Ladakh’s villages have not changed greatly between Moorcroft’s time and ours, and recent research has confirmed the excellence he remarked on. Studies in Zanskar in the early 1980s, before the arrival of the motor road had made any appreciable difference to traditional practice, established that ‘grain yields in Zangskar are much better than most, and nearly as high as those obtained from intensive high input farming in Europe’; in some villages this was achieved without recourse to rotation or fallowing.5 In the villages, as well as in Chang-thang where the nomads lead a pastoral life, almost every family keeps some livestock. The systems of crop husbandry and animal husbandry, indeed, are so perfectly integrated, that neither would be possible without the other. As well as the obvious products — meat, dairy products, and wool — those outputs of the livestock that are vital to the farmer are manure, dung-fiiel, transport and draught-power. The plough is drawn by the dzo, a hybrid between the male yak and the common cow. For the purpose of breeding these — for the offspring of the dzomo (the female dzo) is not highly valued, the male being sterile — most farmers keep a cow or two; while several combine in the ownership of a yak, known as gon-yak or yul-yak which, rather like the Brahmini bull of the Indian plains, wanders free to perform his duty of impregnating the cows. In Chang-thang, where there is virtually no agriculture, animal husbandry is a way of life, and the people’s entire existence revolves round the seasonal migration with their flocks from pasture to pasture. The chief source of the nomads’ livelihood is the pash­ mina goat, source of the pashm that is the raw material for some of the world’s finest and most expensive fabrics. Pashm is the

1 2 2 LADAKH warm undercoat which nature has provided for many animals living at that altitude — yak, dogs and camels as well as goats wild and domesticated — to enable them to survive the long months of below-freezing temperatures in winter. Although it is Ladakh’s most important product, it is only recently that the nomadic lifestyle of the Chang-pa herdspeople, and their system of rearing the goats that produce it, together with their other valuable live­ stock, has become the subject of serious study. Many questions remain, but enough is now known to establish the fact that their system of migration and utilization of the pastures is a highly sophisticated one which takes full account of natural processes and is in accord with the scientific principles of grasslands ecology. The Chang-pa’s other two animals are the yak and the chang- luk sheep.6 From the yak come the hair out of which their tents are woven, milk and butter (though not in great quantities), meat, when an animal has to be slaughtered, and above all power, for it is primarily a carrying animal. The chang-luk is also a pack- animal which provides in addition coarse wool and ultimately meat. But, whether in Chang-thang or in the primarily agricul­ tural areas of Ladakh, meat production is the least of the purposes of livestock rearing. In Ladakh, as all over the Tibetan Plateau, even the Buddhists (though not the lamas) eat meat as occasion arises. Nevertheless they are keenly aware that the real value of their animals lies in the produce they give recurrently — dung, milk, wool and power; they have more sense than to deplete the sources of these by indiscriminate slaughter. Meat is rather a by-product of the necessary process of culling the flocks, which takes place before the winter so as to avoid wasting scarce fodder on animals which have outlived their usefulness. This system has the incidental advantages of providing a supply of meat during the season when the climate gives natural refrigeration, enabling it to be kept for many weeks or even months; and of providing a source of protein when the weather puts the greatest strain on the human body. Otherwise, the diet available to villagers in the pre-modern system seems poor enough, and sadly lacking in fresh fruit and vegetables, though this diet or something like it has provided nourishment to the populations of the Tibetan plateau for mil­ lennia. The staple food is tsampa, parched barley flour; and barley is known to be one of the most nutritious cereals. The tsampa is

THE DAILY ROUND 123 either mixed with butter-tea, or chang— a mildly alcoholic drink, best described as fermented barley-water, drunk by all the Tibetan peoples — to form a kind of gruel, or it is moulded into dumplings and cooked in a vegetable broth. It is probably true to say that everyone has enough to eat — they could not survive otherwise in this climate — but there is little enough margin. Nevertheless, the Ladakhis are a sturdy people, and the Zanskar researchers found a healthy population, though with indications that this remains coupled with a relatively high infant and child mortality rate.7 Over generations, the Ladakhi physique has adap­ ted in response to the harsh environment. In particular, to cope with the rarefied air they have to breathe, they show unusual strength of the heart and lungs, which few outsiders, even after years of acclimatization, will be able to match. Where a plainsman may pant, or need to stop for breath, the Ladakhi will forge ahead, showing no signs of distress. Like all the hill peoples of India, the Ladakhis can shift loads that seem incredible to the pampered products of a sophisticated civilization. This too has been a way of adapting to life in a land where there were no roads fit for wheels (and where, as a result, the use of the wheel for transport was unknown), and so the only way of carrying goods from one place to another for a man too poor to own pack-animals was on his back. Frederic Drew was impressed by the stamina of his porters: I have had women employed to carry my baggage, according to the custom o f the country, who have done twenty-three or twenty-four miles with sixty pounds on their back, and have come in at the end singing cheerfully. (Drew, The Jum m oo an d Kashmir Territories, p. 248) Similarly, by the standards of most outsiders, the Ladakhis are impervious to cold. These adaptations vary in degree according to the varying conditions within Ladakh itself. The nomadic herds­ man from Chang-thang, used to an altitude of 4500 metres, will feel intolerably oppressed by the summer heat in Leh, and the thickness of the atmosphere, and is said to think of Srinagar much as eighteenth-century Europeans thought of Calcutta — as some­ where terminally hot and unhealthy, from where they will be lucky to return alive. These qualities must have been acquired by generations of natural selection, operating partly through the harsh means of

124 LADAKH ’ high infant and child mortality. One result of this is that at the other end of the scale, longevity has been in the past greater in Ladakh than in other places with a kinder environment, the census figures (1961) showing that the percentage of the population over 55 years of age was over 10, while the figure for other districts in Jammu and Kashmir varied from 5.5 to 8 per cent. No doubt there are particular health problems, and these seem to relate largely to maternal and child welfare, and nutrition in general. Recent research and the experience of local doctors has indicated rather a high incidence of respiratory disease, the result partly no doubt of exposure to smoky cooking stoves in ill-ventilated kitchens. Another cause is the gritty loessic dust stirred up by the wind that scours Ladakh’s barren mountain slopes, the effect of which on the lungs is said to be akin to the industrial disease silicosis. Otherwise the lifestyle, though physically demanding, is on the whole a healthy one. Men and women alike spend as much time as possible out of doors, even in winter, gratefully savouring the sun’s warmth. As in every rural society, the Ladakhis have an indigenous system of medicine — actually the Tibetan system — based on herbs and other natural substances. Its practitioners who are called amchis undergo a long and rigorous course of training. Of recent years, this system has been regaining the popularity it is said to have lost in the 1970s and early 80s, when the tourist influx led to a craze for all things Western, at the expense of traditional values and methods. It has even been incorporated into the administration’s health-care network. Traditionally, there was little or nothing by way of formal education except in the gompas. What village children needed to acquire was an understanding of the soil and the crops and the livestock and how to manage them; and this they picked up, like village children in all pre-modern rural societies, simply by being with their elders in the fields and pastures, watching, participating, and gradually, as they grew up, taking responsibility according to their age and capacity. Boys who took to the religious life in the gompas — usually one son from every family — were obliged to master the Tibetan script in order to read the holy books; other­ wise there was little need for even basic literacy. Drew however remarked that the Ladakhis’ intelligence, though slow, was sound; and added approvingly that the literate Ladakhi had a distinct

THE DAILY ROUND 125 edge over his counterpart in Kashmir or elsewhere in India, in the matter of reading and comprehending a map. Technology in Ladakh is of the simplest. The absence of any kind of wheeled transport in the traditional scheme of things comes as a surprise at first, for ignorance of the wheel is usually regarded as the hallmark of a society still at a primitive level of culture. But it soon becomes clear that the reason lies, like so much else, in the environment: in the rugged mountainous terrain which makes the wheel utterly impractical as a means of transport. It is far more sensible to use sturdy pack-animals, or even to heave a load on one’s own back, than to try to invest the same amount of energy in propelling a cart up a steep and stony incline, or in holding it back on the downward slope. This does not mean that the use of the wheel for other purposes is unknown, where energy really can be saved. Most obvious of these is the prayer-wheel; of a more workaday application is the millwheel — not as in Europe a ponderous affair set vertically and needing the strong current of a large volume of water to turn it, but rather a circular set of paddles set horizontally and operated by a fairly modest flow, its axis prolonged to turn the nerher millstone. Although this type of water-mill is in common use in Tibet as well as in the Himalayan regions of India and Nepal, it is believed to have reached Ladakh from Baltistan; the Baltis, though ethnically related to the Ladakhis, have been credited with superior engineering skills. Really primitive in Ladakh — as almost everywhere in India, in the plains and the hills alike — is the plough, simply an iron-shod piece of wood, guided through the earth by the farmer’s grasp on its handle and drawn by the docile dzo. Other agricultural tools, the hoe and the spade, are equally simple. As for the manufacture of homespun cloth, the spindle is often no more than a sturdy twig, about 20 centimetres long and the thickness of a child’s finger, worn smooth by years of use, and dangling free, or with its spin supported in a tiny saucer. The looms are equally unsophisticated. Outside the Government-sponsored weaving centres, the flying shutde is unknown even now, and the thread of the weft is passed laboriously from hand to hand — a procedure which necessarily limits the cloth to narrow widths. Construction methods have the same simplicity; anything that is built, is built entirely by hand. Houses are made of stones

126 LADAKH collected from the ground, or of hand-formed sun-dried bricks. The traditional rammed-earth technique, to whose strength and durability the walls of Leh Palace and most of the gompas are testimony, has fallen into disuse in central Ladakh and been lost; but there are still master-masons in Kargil who are competent in it. Either way, the only plaster used is mud which, in a climate as dry as Ladakh’s, serves the purpose admirably. The roof-beams are of timber, usually poplar, the interstices being filled with neatly laid bundles of willow twigs, and the whole covered with earth, well beaten down. The fields have been prepared for cultivation in the first in­ stance by clearing the ground of the stones with which it is everywhere encumbered; these stones have provided the material for the dry-stone walls that divide the fields from each other, and also buttress the steps where sloping land has had to be terraced. Moorcroft also describes a system whereby walls are constructed along the mountainsides towards their base, and left for years to trap the alluvium brought down by the melting snows, thus laying the foundation for future cultivation. But the most impressive technological feat achieved in old Ladakh was undoubtedly the amazing system of irrigation. For those villages with a convenient natural source nearby there has obviously been relatively little problem in conducting the water to the fields through a network of distribution channels. But there are other villages which have grown up where there is land suitable in other ways for cultivation — flat or with a gentle slope — but far from any stream. Anonymous and unsung engineers, many of them Baltis if local tradition is to be credited, have constructed channels, often several kilometres in length, taking off from the nearest stream, and contouring high above the valley bottoms, their alignment marked by the fringe of greenery along their margins. As impressive as the technological achievement is the spirit of co-operation, by which on the one hand, everyone accepts their obligation to contribute to the maintenance of these chan­ nels, while on the other, there is rarely any dispute about the sharing of the irrigation water, which is almost invariably done by consensus, and on the basis of old established systems. This is only one example of the co-operative spirit which informs every aspect of village life. Village communities, existing in a situation of strictly limited natural resources, have always been

THE DAILY ROUND 127 concerned to keep the occasions for competition and conflict to a minimum, and for this purpose they have developed a number of formal and informal mechanisms of co-operation and mutual help. The family that needs to build an extension on to their house or repair the walls of their fields, can call on their neighbours for help, which will be given gladly, and with the confidence that when the occasion arises the debt will be repaid in kind. Draught animals and even ploughs are shared between households; families will take it in turn to send one or two of their young people to look after the whole village’s flocks in the grazing grounds; and at harvest time several families combine to gather the crops, today in your fields, tomorrow in mine. By such means, the relationships between families are strengthened, and at the same time scarce resources exploited to the best possible advantage. In eveiy part of Ladakh except Chang-thang, winter is relatively an idle season. Once the harvest is gathered in and the last plough­ ing done, there is no more work in the fields until spring comes and they have to be prepared for sowing. Of course animals have to be looked after in winter as in summer; but in the villages they stay close to their quarters, which are usually on the ground floor of the dwelling-houses, so tending them is primarily a matter of giving them their water and fodder, no more. The main work of this season is weaving, which is done on local looms often set up on the sunny rooftops. Apart from these, even the new kinds of economic activity brought by the processes of ‘development’ and ‘modernization’ come largely to a halt in winter. No construction of a modern or traditional kind can be accomplished, as the intense cold induces cracks in every kind of cementing agent, whether mud-plaster or lime-mortar. In the old days there was a tradition of migrant labour out of the Kargil region: in winter the able- bodied men would trek down to the Punjab and the hill-stations of UP, where their robust physique and willingness to endure cold and other hardships made them much in demand as unskilled labourers. But increased prosperity, largely derived from the work available during the six months’ construction season on various projects of the public works programme, in Leh as well as Kargil, or on the continuous process of road maintenance, has nearly put an end to this. In the Buddhist areas, winter is punctuated and given colour by the annual festivals of the various gompas, most of which take place at intervals during the cold season. People come

128 LADAKH from far and near to attend these traditional religious ceremonies around which gathers much of the atmosphere of a tamasha. Thus there emerges the picture of a hardy people, their lifestyles varying according to the conditions in different regions; unsophis­ ticated, perhaps, yet with an intuitively intelligent approach to the environmental constraints with which they are faced. In Nubra, Zanskar and the Suru valley, as in the Sham Ilaqa and Chang- thang, the rhythm of life is dictated by the cycle of the seasons; ploughing, seed-time and harvest; the rearing of animals, birth and death among the flocks, and their wanderings in search of pasture. Traditionally, it has been a life lived close to nature, modified by only the simplest of technological devices, where an extraordinarily delicate balance operates between man, his physical environment, and the rhythm of the seasons.
