8 Polyandry and Polo T he Ladakhis are the product of a long blending of different races, of which the two most important were the Dards and the Tibetans. Although the Baltis down the Indus to the north-west do show some Tibetan racial characteristics, and speak an archaic dialect of Tibetan, it is noticeable that the further west you go in Ladakh, the less the people’s appearance betrays any large-scale infusion of Tibetan stock. The people of Purig seem to be basically non-Tibetan, with perhaps some admixture of Tibetan genes; but in Dras and along the Indus below Khaltse, there are pockets of more or less pure Dard population, betrayed as such not only by their appearance, but also by their language. This belongs to the same sub-family of the Aryan group of lan­ guages as Kashmiri; but the Kashmiris being in contact with a wider world than the Dards, their speech has acquired a grace and polish that Dardish lacks, as well as a much wider vocabulary derived largely from Sanskrit and Persian. The dialect known as Shina and spoken in Gilgit, the original Dard homeland, is ac­ cepted as the standard form of Dardish; elsewhere the language has split up into forms said to be no longer mutually comprehen­ sible. The one spoken at Dras now has a strong admixture of Kashmiri words and idioms, while the speech of the Da-Hanu people has been influenced by their Ladakhi neighbours and contains many Tibetan-derived expressions. The Dard communities of Dras and the Indus valley — where they number only a couple of thousand concentrated in the villages of Da, Biama, Garkhon and Darchiks, together with Hanu up a side valley, the approach to the Chorbat-la — seem to be not so much survivors of the presumed Dard population that inhabited Ladakh before the coming of the Tibetans, as the descendants of later immigrants. Those at Dras have settled at various times in the relatively recent past — that is, since their homeland in Gilgit and

130 LADAKH Polo below the fort in Skardu (Baitistan): an artist’s impression early this century. adjoining areas fell under the influence of Islam. The migration of the Da-Hanu people, on the other hand, took place before the conversion; consequently they are the only Buddhist Dards left in existence. And yet their Buddhism is very different from that of the Buddhism of central and eastern Ladakh. Their customs differ markedly from those of the Tibetan-descended population of those areas; and their cosmic system, as expressed in the hymns of their triennial Bono-na festival, show distinct traces of the pre-Buddhist animistic religion known as Bon-chos. They are known as Brokpa, people of the pastures; and among the most striking of their customs is a dread, and almost complete avoidance, of washing. For ceremonial purification they burn the fragrant twigs of the pencil cedar. The riddle is how they have managed to retain their ancient religion and way of life almost unchanged over the cen­ turies, although settled on one of the main lines of communication between Ladakh and Baitistan. Perhaps the fact that they are strictly endogamous has something to do with it.

POLYANDRY AND POLO 131 What is believed by some scholars to be a remnant of the original Dard population exists in almost every village of central and eastern Ladakh, in the shape of a group known as the Mon — a Tibetan word used generally to refer to the non-Tibetan populations living or originating south of the Himalaya. Distin­ guished by their non-Tibetan features, they are for the most part carpenters and musicians; all the professional musicians, the players of suma and daman? are Mon; and in particular contexts the word Mon has come to have the secondary meaning of musician. Although there is no caste system in Buddhism, never­ theless the Mon, together with other distinct groups, the Gara or blacksmiths, and the Beda, wandering minstrels, are regarded as being of inferior status, in a way which cannot fail to call to mind the disabilities suffered by the lower castes of Hindus. In central and eastern Ladakh, the great majority of the people bear on their features the imprint of their Tibetan origins, until in Chang-thang an almost pure Tibetan population is found. In Leh and its neighbourhood, the Tibetan look is often seen in combination with some reminder of Indo-Iranian blood — wavy hair, for instance, or a prominent nose, or a heavy beard; and a few faces look pure Indo-Iranian. The Arghon community of Leh pride themselves even now on their descent from foreign mer­ chants who setded there during the great days of the caravan trade. They came from Srinagar, and from the great trading cities of central Asia — Yarkand, Khotan and Kashgar. Nineteenth-cen­ tury writers on Ladakh show a properly strait-laced disapproval of the willingness of Ladakhi women to enter into unions with these settlersr today we would be more tolerant, particularly in view of the presumed imbalance between the numbers of men and women consequent on the system of polyandry. In any case it seems to show an admirable lack of sectarian spirit on the part of the girls themselves, and presumably their families too. Cer­ tainly, the Arghons are today among the most influential and respectable citizens of Leh, and possibly the best-looking too. Nineteenth-century travellers were unanimous in their praise for the character of the Ladakhis, who are described as a simple, cheerful, non-aggressive people, and, in Drew’s words ‘much given to truth-telling’. Most would agree that this assessment remains on the whole as true today as it was when it was written. Even now the crime rate is extraordinarily low — crimes of violence

132 LADAKH are few and for between, and till recently few people even in the town of Leh bothered to lock their doors at night. As late as 1977 there was not a single policeman in Zanskar: no crime, hence no need for the police. No doubt personality differences between individuals are as great here as anywhere else; but as a rule today’s visitor should find no difficulty in his dealings with the local people. The Ladakhi character seems to be naturally democratic. It is true that, despite the virtual absence of a caste system among the Buddhists there are clearly defined class distinctions. Respect, even deference, is paid to members of aristocratic families on the basis of their birth alone; but this falls very far short of either servility on the one hand, or arrogance on the other. A man of the humbler classes stands up straight and looks his social superiors in the eye. This basic humanity in the sense of real appreciation of a person’s worth irrespective of their position in terms of class, is palpable in the main bazaar at Leh, where more often than not the members of the old aristocracy, as much as the modern ‘sahibs’, are indis­ tinguishable in dress and appearance from others; when they are recognized, they are usually seen in easy and unconstrained con­ versation with people of humbler origin. This is surely related to the almost complete emancipation of women in Buddhist society, something that strikes the traveller all the more forcibly when his approach to Ladakh has been through Muslim areas where women are kept in social seclusion, though not, among the peasants, restrained from working in the fields. The cheerfulness shown by people of all sorts and conditions in central Ladakh may well be due partly to the fact that one half of the population is not kept in a state of perpetual subjection by the other. In Buddhist Ladakh, wherever there are men to be seen, there are women too; and one of the most charming features of Leh bazaar is the group of ladies who sit along the footpaths with their baskets of freshly gathered vegetables, spinning and chatting with one another and with the passers-by as they sell their produce. The emancipated position enjoyed by women, as well as various other indications, leads one to conclude that among the Buddhist peoples of Tibetan origin sex is not the bogey it appears to be in most other societies — to an exaggerated extent in some Asian ones. As Michel Peissel puts it in his book on Mustang, ‘sex is not smothered in inhibiting taboos’. People are accepted as individuals,

POLYANDRY AND POLO 133 primarily as human beings rather than as members of this or the other sex; consequently women, not being either hedged about with a spurious sanctity or shunned and feared as the root of all temptation, have remarkable dignity and self-confidence. These attitudes have communicated themselves to many of the Muslims of the Leh area among whom, apart from a few families of very high status, there is no seclusion of women. Among the Kargil Muslims, rather more traditionalist attitudes prevail, except in a few families of education and high social status. Today in Leh, and to a lesser extent in Kargil, women are playing an increasingly prominent role in professional and public life. At the same time, there is no question of women being dominant, any more than men; and the Buddhist custom of fraternal polyandry by no means indicated a matriarchal or matri- lineal system. Rather, it went hand in hand with inheritance by primogeniture, preference being given to the male line, and so operated to prevent the subdivision of property. Although poly­ andry appears as the most striking feature of traditional family life, it was in fact not that basic; the essence of the system rather was the monomarital principle — only one marriage per house­ hold in each generation. This enabled a balance to be struck between a family’s need for labour in the fields, and the danger of producing more children than those fields could support. The mechanics of the system were simple. O f a number of brothers, usually at least one was dedicated to religion as a lama; this would normally be one of the younger ones, unless the eldest had a strong vocation in that direction. The heir to the property would be the eldest layman among them, and any remaining brothers were free either to go and make their own way in the world (for which, in traditional village society, opportunities were strictly limited), or to remain in the family home, in which case there was a clear understanding that they would be subservient to the eldest brother. This involved them in marriage to his wife; if they wanted to marry on their own account they had to leave and set up a separate home, without being entitled to a share in any part of the family property. Within the polyandrous marriage, the pater­ nity of the children was regarded as irrelevant: all the children were accepted as being of the eldest brother, whom they addressed as ‘big father; the younger brothers would be ‘litde fathers’. In a family in which there were no sons, an exacdy reverse system

134 LADAKH operated. The eldest daughter, the heiress, married a husband, known as magpa, who would usually be a younger brother in his own family. The magpa enjoyed no rights over her property; his only role was to work for his wife’s interests, and to help her to produce heirs. Her sisters were given the same rights in the magpa as were the younger brothers in a polyandrous marriage. Thus, depending on the circumstances of each particular family, mar­ riages could be polyandrous, polygynous, or monogamous — a beautifully flexible system. It might be thought that either way this kind of multi-partner marriage would lead to every sort of personal jealousy and rivalry; however, research into family sys­ tems elsewhere in the Himalaya and Tibet indicates that the least stressful kind of family organization is that which, by preventing the family spreading out into numerous branches, and optimizing the number of members in relation to the land to be worked, reduces to a minimum the possibilities for competition and con­ flict. While the Ladakhis are no doubt as subject as anyone else to family tensions, these have tended to be alleviated by patterns of behaviour derived both from the unquestioning faith in Bud­ dhist values, and from the ‘firm sense of personal identity and social position in a community’,2 which is characteristic of Ladak­ hi village life. The system in various forms existed all over the Tibetan cultural area, but only — as several recent studies have established — among landed proprietors, or those holding their estates direct from the Government; it was appropriate neither to landless labourers (of whom there were few if any in pre-modern Ladakh) nor to the urban and commercial classes. It was indeed only one component of a social structure geared to the end of preventing the fragmentation of hereditary estates into uneconomic units, and maximizing their productivity in an ecology of low carrying capacity, by ensuring a sufficient supply of labour to work the fields, while at the same time guarding against an explosive growth of population. The main losers in the system were those women who had no choice but to sacrifice their reproductive potential in the interest of social and economic stability. Some of them entered the religious life as nuns; the rest remained on the family estates and contributed their bit to the family labour pool. Both poly­ andry and inheritance by primogeniture were made illegal by the Government of Jammu and Kashmir in the early 1940s. Although

POLYANDRY AND POLO 135 for many years the laws abolishing these age-old customs were largely disregarded in Ladakh, polyandry seems to have gradually decreased, and is now almost absent in central Ladakh, surviving mainly in Zanskar and in the remoter villages where socio­ economic conditions remain relatively unchanged. As for the division of estates among all the sons of a family, it is only of very recent years that instances of this have come to notice, and it certainly does not seem to bode well for the maintenance of sustainable agriculture on village estates. On the other hand, Muslim law and practice provide for the division of holdings between all the children in a family; and there are no indications that in the Muslim areas of Ladakh — in the Suru valley, for example — it has led to a decrease in the size of holdings beyond the point at which they can be worked economically. Two other social features complemented the system of poly­ andry, working with it to keep the population within the limits set by climate, terrain, and the often meagre supplies of water. One was the monastic system, which took a fair proportion of the male population out of the reproductive stakes;3 and the other the custom known as khang-bu, or ‘little house’ (khang-chung'm Zans­ kar). When the eldest son had reached years of discretion, and had married and produced an heir, the parents retired gracefully to a small house adjoining the main property, taking one or two animals with them, and retaining only as much land as they needed to support themselves. All the responsibilities of managing the property devolved on the eldest son, who was now recognized as the legal owner; the parents had no further claim on it. If there were ‘two fathers’ alive or even more they would all share their retirement in the little house together, probably taking any un­ married daughters with them. This system is surely analogous to the practice of the Tibetan dynasties, in which the heir-apparent would be associated with the work of government in the old king’s lifetime, the latter sometimes abdicating in his favour. The pre­ mium put on youth and vigour in preference to age and experience is one of the most attractive things about Tibetan social thinking. It does not seem to indicate any lack of respect for age, whose wisdom after all remains available to energetic, though inexperi­ enced, youth. An interesting and perhaps unique feature of village life in Ladakh is the pha-spun, an institution that even now gives solidarity

136 LADAKH and support to every Buddhist family. This is a grouping of be­ tween four and fifteen families bound together by their worship of a common lha, or deity. In central Ladakh and Nubra, pha-spun usually consist of unrelated families, whereas in Zanskar all the families in the group share a common patrilineal descent. The pha-spun becomes operational on the major occasions of family life: birth, marriage and death, which involve large-scale entertain­ ing of fellow villagers. Their role is particularly crucial at the time of a death, when the pha-spun members take over from the grieving family all the practical work of organizing meals for the large numbers of people who have come to condole, and the monks who are conducting the religious rites. Not only that, they undertake the more distressing duties of the occasion, like preparing the body, and accompanying it to the cremation site. These social devices seem to spring from the same view of life as the systems of co-operation and mutual help developed by generations of Ladakhi villagers in their working life. Taken to­ gether, they operate so as to minimize conflict and optimize the use of scarce resources; as such they clearly originate more in a shrewdly pragmatic spirit than in any other-worldly theories of altruism. Ladakhi villagers are acutely conscious of the environ­ mental constraints within which they have to operate, and seem to understand intuitively that goodwill among neighbours is in fact a survival mechanism. Thus they accept the systems evolved by their ancestors as the framework to ensure that for any family help will be forthcoming when needed. Other devices make it possible to arrive at sensible forms of compromise between each family’s own rights and needs and those of others. When any occasion for dispute does arise, the tension is often diffused by one party gracefully backing off and refusing to make an issue out of it; when this is impossible the matter is almost invariably settled in an amicable spirit, through the mediation of common friends. At the same time it must be assumed that the Ladakhis arc no more saindy than their counterparts in villages anywhere else in the world. In the days before the interventions of Government provided, as they undoubtedly have, some minimal security for the less well-off, the rich were not above exploiting the poor. Property, as always and everywhere, could be used to breed more property, and the rate at which big landholders lent grain for seed to their less privileged neighbours is said to have been usually in

POLYANDRY AND POLO 137 the region of 25 per cent. If due to a family’s continued misfortune the liability kept on accumulating, they might find themselves in a kind of debt bondage to their creditors. The principal safeguard against pauperization in such circumstances seems to have been the strong public opinion at the village level against the alienation of land. Pragmatic as they are, the Ladakhis have traditionally paid as much attention to maintaining good relations with the unseen powers of the world as with their village neighbours. Earth, water and air, every rock and every tree, are teeming with spirits, some malevolent, some beneficent, all in a delicately balanced relation­ ship with the inhabitants of the physical world. The intermediaries between the two are the lamas, part of whose vocation is to interpret the one to the other, counselling people how to avoid offending the spirits and how to propitiate them if offence has unwittingly been given. In their capacity as astrologers they are able to give authoritative advice as to the propitious time and manner for starting any enterprise, whether sowing or reaping, going on a journey or celebrating a marriage. All this they may do either routinely, as part of the standard procedure of the gompas, or when specially consulted by a lay person, or asked to perform some particular propitiatory puja; or there are some who become the vehicles for particular spirits as oracles, in which state they speak with the very voice of the spirit. The most famous oracles in Ladakh are those of Matho Gompa. A particular spirit may on occasion choose to inhabit the body of a lay person. Often these spirits are feared as being capricious and not necessarily benevolent, and the chosen receptacle may resist or deny their power. Once it has heen accepted however, the person — woman more often than man — undergoes a process of training and initiation by qualified lamas to enable her to handle it. The aim of such training is to harness the spirit-force and transform it into the power to heal; when this has been ac­ complished the oracle — lha-ba — starts regular practice as a healer. Western-trained anthropologists have interpreted the event of possession in terms of hysterical disturbance associated, often, with changes in a person’s circumstances, such as a young woman’s shift of residence on marriage. In this context, the lha-ba training at the same time develops and controls a pre-existing multiple personality syndrome. The Ladakhis, looking through their own

138 LADAKH cultural spectacles, see it rather differently, regarding the lha-ba with awe and reverence for the personal power which has enabled her to bring the spirit energy under control. They accept without question the effectiveness of the spirit-healing, which is said usual­ ly to involve the lha-ba in sucking evil matter out of the afflicted person’s body. Westerners who have attended such healing ses­ sions have been disconcerted by the intensity of the psychic power they could sense being unleashed; and there have been occasions on which they have, somewhat to their own surprise, been cured of their ailments. It is interesting to compare some aspects of Ladakhi life today with accounts of a hundred years ago, of which one of the most detailed and reliable is that by Frederic Drew. Many of Drew’s observations hold good today; the differences point to a distinct increase in prosperity since he wrote. For example, he says that, while tea is a favourite beverage, most people are too poor to be able to afford to drink it every day. Today, there may be a very few households in the poorer regions where tea, liberally laced with butter, is not a daily drink; but generally speaking tea is no longer considered a luxury, but a necessity of life. Again, according to Drew, All [Ladakhis] have a rooted objection to washing. I was told that there was a custom of bathing once a year, but I could never get any satisfactory corroboration of the report. Their clothes, worn next to them, are never washed, but are affectionately kept around them until they fall to pieces. (Drew, The Jum m oo and Kashmir Territories, p. 248.) Here too things have changed. It is true that during the long cold months of winter there is little inclination to wash all over, and even from among outsiders staying in Ladakh it is probably only a few orthodox Hindus, for whom it is a ritual necessity, who take regular and frequent baths. But even in winter most Ladakhis (with the exception of the Da-Hanu Dards, and possibly of the Chang-thang nomads) make an effort to keep themselves accept­ ably clean; while in summer bathing is commonplace and every stream is the scene of vigorous laundry work, particularly during the early weeks with a winter’s grime to be washed out of heavy woollen clothes. Clearly, a hundred years ago cleanliness was a

POLYANDRY AND POLO 139 luxury few could afford; the fact that it is no longer so is a pointer to an improved standard of living. Another such pointer may be deduced from Drew’s description of the people’s clothes. The goncha, he says, the enveloping woollen coat worn, with differen­ ces in style, by both sexes, is their sole garment: nothing is worn underneath. Nowadays tht goncha— which men in cosmopolitan Leh often discard in summer in favour of Western-style dress — usually conceals, not only a full set of warm underclothing, but shirt, trousers and sweater as well; the women’s goncha too is worn over a complete salwar-kameez suit. The goncha continues to be the national dress of Ladakh; and a good deal of the charm of the villages, and of Leh itself, is derived from the unselfconsciously picturesque appearance of the people. The goncha is a supremely practical dress for a climate of severe cold; and the men’s version is a distinctly stylish garment. It is, in effect, a double-breasted calf-length coat; it is cut wide, and the extra material pulled to the back to form two pleats, which are secured by a brighdy coloured cummerbund. It is fastened on the right shoulder and down the right side with brass buttons and loops, and the edges of the stand-up collar are piped with silver brocade. The gonchas of the poorer people are made from coarse homespun woollen cloth, usually dyed to a dark shade of maroon. The well-to-do will use a variety of material for their gonchas, even, lightweight synthetics for summer wear. For formal occasions, there can be few more stylish garments than a goncha in black velvet, silver gleam of piping at the border and collar, and a cummerbund of shocking pink. To the eye of an outsider, the women’s goncha is a good deal less elegant than the men’s. It has a full skirt, gathered into a great number of small pleats, and it too is secured with a bright cum­ merbund. Older women, particularly in the villages, add a goatskin tied around the shoulders, wool inside. This may be replaced by a multicoloured embroidered or brocade mantle with long silk fringes. For formal wear, the outfit is completed either by a hat, or by the perak, the traditional head-dress. This is a long strip of leather, reaching from the forehead half-way down the back; it is covered with cloth to which are stitched rows of turquoises graded in order of size, together with a jewelled amulet- box. Silver chains and strings of coral beads complete what must be one of the most curiously ornate head-dresses ever devised

140 LADAKH anywhere. The Ladakhi woman actually carried her fortune on her head; over the years, as family resources permitted the purchase of more stones, it became an heirloom, passed from mother to daughter. The perak is worn over a foundation which protrudes on both sides in two stiff wide flaps; although the ears are not covered, the fashion is said to derive from the request of a queen of Ladakh for a head-dress which would protect her from the draughts that gave her earache. The hat seems to be a compara­ tively new fashion, for nothing like it is described in the nineteenth-century accounts. It is rather the shape of a top-hat, but smaller, so that it sits more perched on the head. The brim is cut away over the forehead, leaving something like two horns on the two side’s. It is usually covered with brocade, or black velvet embroidered in silver thread; a red lining shows at the upturned brim. From the occasional sight of such a hat much lower In the crown than usual, and giving much of the appearance of a cap with ear-flaps, it may be deduced that the hat is a stylized develop­ ment of such a cap. It is worn by women, and by men of the older generation — the women’s straight, the men’s at a rakish angle. In spite of the somewhat bunchy cut of her goncha, the well- to-do Ladakhi lady in full fig has a striking and opulent ap­ pearance. Her best goncha is often in heavy figured Chinese silk: a reminder of the caravan trade through which, in the old days, such luxury goods were imported from great distances. Its usually sober hue is an appropriate background not only for her brighdy coloured brocade mantle, but also for the heavy strings of jewellery — baroque pearls, turquoises, coral and amber — that adorn her neck and ears. Fashions change, even in Ladakh, and what was ‘in’ this season may be quite out of style the next. The chuba, the more stream­ lined and elegant Tibetan gown, which was frequently chosen for semi-formal wear by the ladies of Leh’s ^lite at the time the earlier edition of this book was written, is hardly seen today; and both the perak and the hat — without which no respectable woman would have stepped out of her house in the 1970s — are now confined to stricdy formal occasions. For everyday wear in the mid-1990s, the goncha is complemented by the dupatta, the light chiffony scarf originally part of the north Indian salwar-kamecz outfit, which is draped over the shoulders. Some nineteenth-cen­ tury travellers’ accounts have intriguing descriptions of the

POLYANDRY AND POLO 141 women’s goncha of the period, its skirt made of a large number of strips of woollen material, in different colours, or resist-dyed in roundels, stitched together and falling in gathers. This was not observed at all in the late 1970s; but fifteen years later, it has made a comeback — not indeed for normal wear, but for the troupes of dancers who perform at archery festivals and other social oc­ casions. The styles of dress just described are common, with minor variations, to all the areas of Buddhist Ladakh. The members of Muslim communities settled in these areas on the whole conform to the prevailing fashion, apart from the perak and probably the hat. The Kargil Muslims, however, have a rather different style. The men wear the goncha — made usually of homespun woollen material in its natural colour, not dyed maroon like the Buddhists’ — but only in cold weather. In summer they discard it in favour of an outift rather like the fashionable ‘Pathan’ garb: a cotton knee-length shirt over baggy trousers cut like a salwar, and topped by a loose waistcoat. On the head they almost invariably wear a knitted woollen cap. The goncha is as foreign to the Kargil Muslim woman as it would be to a Kashmiri or a Punjabi. In the villages she wears a loose woollen kurta — not unlike the Kashmiri phcran, but less voluminous — over a salwar, her head and shoulders are draped with a shawl. The more modern and stylish women of the younger generation in Kargil town have more or less adopted the Punjabi salwar-kameez, they too will keep their heads covered with a dupatta. In their architecture, as in their dress, the Ladakhis show a natural aesthetic sense. The villages blend into the landscape; and their houses, so satisfactorily adapted to the environment, delight the eye. They are built of sun-dried bricks or local stone — not quarried, simply picked up from the open desert — and are plastered with dried mud. In a climate where only about 13 centimetres of moisture are precipitated annually, cement is a dispensable luxury. The flat roofs are made of a thick layer of earth well stamped down, and laid on top of a bed of willow twigs which are in their turn supported by the wooden beams of the top-storey ceilings. It is a little surprising to note that in Dras, the Suru valley and Zanskar, all of them subject to heavy winter snowfall, the pattern of flat-roofed houses — which originated, presumably, in the low-precipitation valleys of central Ladakh and

142 LADAKH Tibet — is retained. The expressed rationale for this is the need for space to store supplies of fodder for the winter. Snow is never allowed to melt on the roofs, but as soon as it stops falling, is shovelled away with special implements. This appears to be a satisfactory enough solution in winter; but these roofs are far from rain-proof, and with the evident possibility of a change of climate involving increased precipitation, it may become necessary for future builders in Ladakh to rethink their roofing methods. Without cement, and without the mechanized methods of modern construction, the building of a Ladakhi house is a fairly simple affair and above all it is a noiseless one. Today, widi over 15,000 visitors (more than the whole population of the town) coming every year and spending money, Leh has something of the aspect of a boom town, and a great amount of new construc­ tion is going on. But it is a boom town on its own terms. Even where a decision has been taken that a new building in traditional style should be strengthened by steel-and-cement pillars at the corners, construction does not imply the mechanized thumping, crashing, clashing and general soul-shattering racket that assault the ears and fray the nerves in any modern city. In Ladakh the only mechanization may be the use of a three-tonne truck to bring the construction materials to the nearest road-head; for the town centre is already fully built up, so new houses are being built on the outskirts, in the midst of fields, often approached only by a footpath. From the road-head, labourers carry loads of stone and brick on their backs to the site, lightening the task with rhythmical antiphonal work-chants. The actual construction is done by hand, and is not highly skilled work. Apart from a carpenter to execute the decorative work applied to the lintels of doors and windows, and a mason, whose sole qualification need be to know the difference between a straight and a crooked line, there is no need for any skilled labour at all. All that said, there are nevertheless, as the twentieth century draws to a close, a remarkable number of ugly buildings in both Leh and Kargil. Many of these have been perpetrated by the administration — the unimaginative application of stereotypes developed elsewhere; and the same can be said of the Army, which is such a palpable presence in the two towns, particularly on the wide valley slopes below Leh. Part of the responsibility however must rest with the Ladakhi nouveau riche; a few of their

POLYANDRY AND POLO 143 new houses and hotels are built in nondescript yet pretentions suburban style, though in many others modern conveniences are incorporated into buildings that blend charmingly into the landscape. For the most part, however, apart from the introduction of electricity, the main departure from tradition in the houses of even the well-to-do today is the extensive use of glass. This has clearly made all the difference between comfort, and a mere toleration of discomfort. It must surely be a recent innovation, for until the motor road was built, the import of glass, except perhaps on a very limited scale, was obviously not an enterprise to be under­ taken lightly. Now, in the towns and those villages which are easily accessible by road, not only do many of the houses have their windows glazed in the usual way, but every reasonably prosperous home has a ‘glass room’: a room one or two of whose outside walls, facing south or west, are glass from top to bottom. The value of such a room is incalculable. Winter in Ladakh is cold — for two or three months bitterly cold — but it is a bracing cold with clear skies and bright sunshine for most of the time. The sun is so strong that if the cold air is screened off, even in deepest winter it will make a glass room warm enough so that no other form of heating is needed during the day. Since the early 1980s, the Ladakh Ecological Development Group has been working to popularize more efficient ways of utilizing the abundant free resource that solar energy is. For space-heating they have intro­ duced the Trombe Wall, which is simply the usual type of south- facing mud-brick wall, painted black, and covered with a double layer of glass; a system of ducts keeps the warmed air circulating by convection, and the wall acts as a heat storage reservoir keeping the temperature in the room at a comfortable level for several hours even after the sun has left it. Inside as well as out, most Ladakhi houses give evidence of innate good taste, not yet corrupted by contact with a more vulgar civilization. Before the introduction of glass, and even now in the villages, the centre of the Ladakhi home was the one room which in the nature of things would always be warm — the kitchen. The kitchen of an old-style Ladakhi house is a delightful room. It is large, and usually rather dark, since the number of windows is kept to a minimum in the interests of warmth. Around the walls are shelves of polished wood bearing

144 LADAKH an array of pots and utensils ranging from beautifully worked antique copper pots, the relics of the Yarkand trade, to the more utilitarian items usual today: aluminium steamers in several tiers for cooking meat-filled dumplings (mok-mok), handleless saucepans, Indian style; and the ubiquitous pressure-cooker. Ready to hand is the gur-gur, the cylindrical wooden brass-bound churn used for mixing butter-tea. In the centre is a free-standing stove of baked earth or iron, sometimes decorated. It burns wood, and its chimney-pipe goes out by a hole in the ceiling. In the villages, instead of the wood-burning stove, there will be an open hearth burning cakes of dried animal-dung. The bellows is an important fixture. The facilities for cutting vegetables and wash­ ing dishes — the more messy but indispensable parts of kitchen work — are tucked aWay unobtrusively somewhere. One wall is left free of kitchen paraphernalia for low carpet-covered divans and low wooden tables (chogtse) where family and guests may sit. It is as homely and dignified an arrangement as one could wish; and though clearly evolved in response to climatic and other limitations, it has the great additional merit of allowing the cook to do her work without being segregated from the rest of the life of the house: perhaps yet another expression of the emancipated way of life of the Buddhist woman. In the old days, and even now in the villages, the kitchen served as sleeping quarters for the whole family in the cold months of winter. For many years now, the wood- or dung-burning stove has been supplemented in the houses of the well-to-do by the primus- type stove burning kerosene, which makes possible the efficient use of that now indispensable appliance, the pressure-cooker — a boon for high-altitude cooking. Now, with the introduction of the gas cylinder into Leh and Kargil, the traditional kitchen has undergone a further metamorphosis. In the homes of somewhat Westernized Ladakhis it has tended to lose out, being reserved for the use of family and close friends, other guests being entertained in the glass-room. The introduction of devices other than the cooking-stove for space-heating points in the same direction. On winter evenings, the glass-room is warmed by the bokhari, the cylindrical free-standing stove used in Kashmir, where it burns wood; here it is adapted to give a fiercer heat by burning hard coke or kerosene oil. The glass-room may, as a concession to modernity, have a few chairs, but its basic furnishing consists of

POLYANDRY AND POLO 145 divans, and chogtse, which are often elaborately carved and painted. There is no separate dining-room, and if a meal is being served, it is brought to where the guests are sitting, and arranged on the V The Ladakhi language is a dialect of Tibetan, and will be un­ familiar to most visitors; but most of the people with whom the traveller comes in contact have a sufficient grasp of Hindustani — some of the Leh Muslims indeed speak a very chaste Urdu — and in Leh and Kargil towns many of them are fluent in English, while more have at least a smattering. The overseas visitor will find no more difficulty about language in Ladakh than anywhere else in India. The presence of outsiders no longer excites much curiosity in Leh or the villages on the beaten track. In more remote areas, or those opened up only recently, visitors may find them­ selves objects of interested scrutiny, especially by the children. Early tourists did Ladakhi children little service by offering them handouts; bizarrely, many of them seem to have had the habit of distributing writing implements, if the almost universal request, ‘one pen’, is anything to go by. In Zanskar which was ‘discovered’ largely by the French, it is more often ‘one bonbon’. But there is nothing in the least offensive about this entreaty; rather there is an almost invariable impression that the kids, filled with mischief to the eyebrows, are trying it on. The all-purpose greeting is ‘Jooley’, and people are delighted when it is reciprocated. In Muslim areas ‘Salaam Aleikum’ is more appropriate, with the reply ‘Waleikum Salaam’. The sociable nature of the Ladakhis is perhaps stimulated by the climate of their land: by the long winters when agricultural work comes to a standstill, and the people, with little to do, must keep themselves occupied as best they can. In most villages, during the long winter evenings, there are story-telling sessions, when the villagers gather to listen to stories drawn from their rich folklore, told by that one among them whose gifts have made him the acknowledged tale-teller of their group. Every family event is the pretext for a celebration, with song and dance, at which great quantities of chang are consumed by men and women alike. The dances are slow, grave and graceful, the steps involving subtleties not immediately apparent to the

146 LADAKH uninitiated; every Ladakhi Buddhist learns them from childhood. Uncompromising standards are applied to appraise and criticize the dancers’ performance. A flick of the wrist or pointing of the toe may make or mar a person’s reputation as a dancer or alter­ natively, may betray that he or she comes from a different region, whose style of dancing, it goes without saying, leaves much to be desired. These are social dances, and all take part, participation being governed by a strict code of etiquette. At a wedding, for instance, groups will come out from among the guests — a group from the bride’s family, then one from the groom’s; a group of youngsters, followed by representatives of the older generation, and so on. The privilege of leading the dance is given to the senior member of whichever group is dancing; in the dances not confined to particular family groups, the invitation to lead the dance is given to the senior member of the best family present — ‘best’ in terms of social status. In the old days it could be a matter of mortal insult, generating all manner of family quarrels and even the instigation of civil suits, if someone felt slighted by not having been invited to take the lead to which they felt themselves entitled. This is one of the ways in which the consciousness of class gains expression; but it is not obvious to the outside observer, whose attention is focused on the slowly changing patterns of the hand- and foot-movements of the dancers. Group by group they come out, the men and women sometimes separately, sometimes to­ gether, dance their dance, and then go back to their seats. It gives a feeling of timelessness; the continuity of tradition is palpable; and for a moment of tranquillity there is the illusion that the Ladakhi way of life is immutable and secure. Apart from chang, the characteristic beverage of Ladakh is gur-gur tea, the butter-tea consumed in one form or another by all the Tibetan peoples. The Ladakhis are particular about using fresh, not rancid butter. This is added with salt and milk to a well-boiled infusion of tea to which a little bicarbonate of soda has been added. The mixture is then churned in a churn or gur-gur (the word is onomatopoeic) till all the ingredients are well blended. Gur-gur tea is made in bulk, can be kept hot (traditionally in a decorative copper pot on a charcoal brazier; nowadays more often in the prosaic thermos), and is drunk in enormous quantities by the Ladakhis, thirty or forty (coffee-size) cups a day being quite a normal quota. It is both warming and nourishing, particularly

POLYANDRY AND POLO 147 when mixed with tsampa — altogether a comforting drink in a cold climate. To an outsider it may taste more like a bland soup than tea. For the guest in a Ladakhi house, whether alone or in a party, there is a certain formality about the drinking of either chang or gur-gurtea. Most probably she is sitting on a carpet-covered divan, only a fraction above floor-level, with a chogtse in front of her. A glass or a small cup is placed on the chogtse, and filled up. A tea-cup will be covered with a lid of chased metal to keep it hot. Etiquette demands that the host should never let the guest’s cup be less than full; conversely, it is polite on the guest’s part to refrain from drinking until the host is ready to give her a refill. Accordingly, she does not touch her drink until the attendant returns with the jug and urges her on. Only then may she turn her attention to her cup or glass, sipping tentatively or draining it down, as she pleases. It is as well for the visitor who is hoping for an entree into Ladakhi homes to acquire a taste for these two beverages. Gur-gur tea may present a little difficulty, though if she thinks of it as soup rather than tea, and if she has no aversion to the taste of melted butter, she should be able to do all right. But the richness given it by the butter may make it difficult for her to stomach an amount which will satisfy the hospitable instincts of her host. Chang should present no problems at all. It is in effect fermented barley-water; it has a pleasant sourish taste. While it may be drunk without great enthusiasm at first, most visitors find it grows on them. The traditional summer pastimes of the Ladakhis are archery and polo; and in the Leh area football is very popular. Championships are organized, and fans are as ardent and vociferous in support of their chosen teams as their counterparts anywhere else in the world. The standard of play is comparable to that of amateur football anywhere; no allowances have to be made for the altitude. The same cannot be said of archery; and as far as the Leh area is concerned, the so-called archery festivals seem to be little more than excuses for a summer party. Boldly patterned shamianas shelter a crowd decked out in its Sunday best, the men sober yet stylish in their smartest gonchas, the women colourful with their brocade mantles and heavy strings of jewellery. As important as

148 LADAKH the shooting itself are the interludes of dancing and miscellaneous entertainment, and chang flows freely. There is nothing to show that, apart from these ‘festivals’, archery is taken at all seriously as a sport, and the flight of the arrows tends to be erratic. Neverthe­ less, as with the dance, a strict etiquette prevails. The competing teams must be captained by someone from an aristocratic family, preferably the senior member present. Whenever he or any other person of high social status takes the bow, the music which accompanies the shooting swells in volume and increases in tempo to do him honour. In Kargil archery has fewer extraneous accompaniments. The rather austere form of Islam followed there frowns on such frivo­ lities as music and dancing, and the question of drinking chang does not arise; so an archery festival is actually what its name implies, and the standard is much higher. The archery season in Kargil is in March, the slack season before the spring ploughing, and the archers practice for the competition throughout the winter. Whereas the Leh people are content with makeshift bows fashioned from willow branches, some of those used by the Kargil enthusiasts are objects of high craftsmanship, made to what is said to be a very ancient design incorporating a thin layer of ibex horn, which imparts a high degree of springiness to the bow, and velocity to the arrow. Polo is indigenous to central Asia and parts of the Himalaya, but its precise origins are lost in the obscurity of time. The Iranians have claimed it for their own, and so have the Chinese, and it was being played by the princes of Byzantium in the twelfth century. It seems to have been the early Muslim rulers who first introduced it into India, and it was popular at Akbar’s court; but it died out, except among the ruling families of some Rajput states, and was unknown to the British during the early days of their rule. It survived however on India’s far eastern border, and in the western Himalaya where it had been played from time immemorial inde­ pendently of its existence in medieval India. The sportsmen of the Raj first played polo in Calcutta about 1860, having discovered it in Manipur. It had already been described by travellers in the western Himalaya like Moorcroft, Vigne and Cunningham thirty or forty years earlier, and it was played by British visitors to Kashmir in 1863. From there it reached the Punjab, at about the same time as it also arrived from the direction of Calcutta; by the

POLYANDRY AND POLO 149 early 1870s it was being played in England. The British modified and ‘improved’ the game (as their nineteenth-century ethos dic­ tated they must): invented their own rules, introduced the chukker system out of consideration for the horses, and generally tidied it up. The game of international polo was born. But in Ladakh, and other remote areas of the Karakoram like Hunza, Nagar, Chitral, Gilgit and Baltistan, people continued to play polo as they had always done. In 1935, Peter Fleming saw in Hunza a game practically identical with what is today known in Ladakh as Balti-style polo. The feature that struck him most was that a player was allowed to catch the ball, after which he need only ride between the posts with it to score a goal; ‘but’, Fleming adds, ‘since he may legitimately be thwarted by any method short of a knife-thrust, this is not as easy as it sounds’.4 In Ladakh the player is permitted to throw the ball between the posts, and a goal is awarded. There are no chukkers, but each half ends when either team has scored nine goals; the only foul is to cut across the path of another player’s horse, which is obviously dangerous. Otherwise there are no rules, least of all any regulating the size of the ground or the number of players. The size of the field is determined by how big a patch of level ground may be spared from cultivation in this rugged terrain of steep mountains and narrow valleys; and the strength of the team by the availability of good players. A traveller lucky enough to see polo played in one of the villages of the interior may still see something like the game Cunningham saw at Mulbekh in 1848, when twenty players on each side pursued the ball up and down a field 360 metres long and 75 broad. Although the Ladakh Chronicle records the laying-out of a polo ground in Leh in 1885, travellers’ accounts show that the game was being played up and down the main bazaar until well into the twentieth century. Here is an account of a game played in 1906: A game of polo is composed of all the sportsmen who like to join. The goals are fixed at either end of the street, and the players station them­ selves where it seems to them best. The roadway cleared, the onlookers all settled in the doorways, balconies, and upon the roofs of the houses, there enters at one end a little company of horsemen. These walk peacefully down the street until the report of a gun galvanises them into life, and they break into a fierce gallop. At the half-way mark the man

150 LADAKH in the centre of the bunch of tearing figures tosses a ball into the air, and as it descends hits it an all-sounding whack with a club that sends it spinning down the road, followed by an avalanche of pounding hooves. From the opposing goal-posts an individual darts out, and at the leaping ball, his charge taking him slap into the coming rush, and to what appears to be certain death. But he emerges yelling, riding like a madman, and brandishing his club — and with the ball describing parabolas in front of him. The State Compounder! if you please, and the fat Post Office Baboo at his heels, roaring like a bull and backing up like a Blucher. Hither and thither the players surge, the ball speeding up and down, cannoning from side to side of the street, and sometimes landing in a crowd of women perched on a house-top. After every goal the band breaks into paeans that mingle with the plaudits of the people. A brief rest, and they come pounding down the street again. Everybody holds their breath while the ball is tossed up and is dropping to meet the unerring aim of the club that sends it flying. Helter-skelter follow the excited players! Out darts the Compounder! The Post Office charges! The Yarkandi merchant saves! The Zemindar from up the road gains the ball! the Serai Manager takes it from him, and the Tehsildar strikes a goal with a blow from his club that sounds like the crack of doom! Phew! *Tis both hot and fast, is polo at Leh. (Fraser, The M arches o f Hindustan, pp. 120—1) The practice of starting and resuming play by tossing the ball and hitting it in a single action from mid-field while galloping full tilt towards the opposing goal remains current in Ladakhi polo today. That it requires a degree of skill which is beyond the command of most non-Ladakhi players is evident from the fact that it has been edited out of the international game; and players from outside who sportingly agree to take on a local team in the local version of the game are obliged to resort to expedients like having two players ride to the half-way point, the first of whom drops the ball for his companion to hit. Polo was introduced to central Ladakh from Baltistan, where Drew testified to its enormous popularity in the late nineteenth century. Some authorities say that it was brought to Ladakh by Sengge Namgyal whose mother was a Balti princess, early in the seventeenth century, and that it was Sengge who laid out the first royal polo ground, in the Murtse garden below Leh (now the site of the Defence Ministry’s Field Research Laboratory). Others aver that it was brought by the colony of Baltis settled at Chushot, a village just across the Indus, a few kilometres upriver from Leh.

POLYANDRY AND POLO 151 Indeed, these two accounts may not be incompatible. The date of the Chushot. settlement has not been established, but it may well have been as early as Sengge Namgyal’s reign; and considering the Baltis’ almost fanatical devotion to polo, it may not be too far-fetched to imagine the half-Balti Sengge bringing and settling a colony of his mother’s compatriots for the express purpose of playing polo and popularizing it in Ladakh. The Chushot Baltis, though in most respects fully assimilated to the Ladakhi way of life, have retained both their religion — they are Shia Muslims to a man — and a clear consciousness of their own identity. They still have all the old Balti enthusiasm for polo, and it seems to have been they who kept the game alive in the Leh area after the loss of Ladakh’s independence led to the withdrawal of royal patronage. Chushot is the only village in central Ladakh with its own polo ground, where every year on 21 March polo is played to celebrate Nauroz, the Persian (and Shia) New Year. Even now, many of the finest players in and around Leh are Baltis from Chushot. This does not mean that today there is any lack of enthusiasm for the game among the Leh Buddhists — quite the contrary; interest is universal, and there are a fair number of Buddhists among the front-rank players. But strangely enough, although horses and horsemanship were part of the way of life in old Tibet, polo seems not to have caught on in a big way in Buddhist Ladakh; its distribution in the western Himalaya is more or less coextensive with the penetration of Islam. Apart from the immediate neighbourhood of Leh, polo is today played only in those parts of the district that have a Muslim population, such as the villages of Turtuk and Bogdang in Nubra, down the Shayok river to the north-west. In Kargil district, the austere rule of the Muslim clerics to some extent put a damper on the game in the immediate post-Independence years; but it survived particularly in Dras, where it is said that the various villages can put as many as 24 teams in the field, and of recent years it has even made a comeback in Kargil and Chigtan. Languishing in and around Leh in the middle decades of the century, polo revived from the mid-1970s, thanks to positive steps taken by the district administration to encourage it. Exhibition and tournament matches have been held regularly since then, and as an integral part of the annual Ladakh Festival sponsored by the State Tourism Department, Ladakh polo has started to attract

152 LADAKH international interest, while the Leh Polo Club has secured affiliation to the Indian Polo Association. As played today, it is a slightly modified form of the original game, but still it makes few concessions to international polo. Usually there are six players on each side, and play lasts for an hour with a ten-minute break in the middle. There are no chukkers, but the hardy Zanskari ponies scarcely seem to suffer, even at the altitude of 3500 metres, and at the end of play are going as strongly as at the start, though the game is sometimes very fast. The presence of the Army gives a sharpened interest to the game, for polo in post-Independence India has been almost exclusively an army pursuit; but whereas in the late 1970s the army used to field teams made up of players of the international game — who no doubt were sporting enough to adopt the local style of play — today it is observed that they prefer to confront like with like, so that the army teams are almost exclusively drawn from the local unit, the Ladakh Scouts. Un­ doubtedly the most exciting matches are those in which a local civilian team takes on the army. Partisanship is intense on both sides, and freely expressed; and when the civilian team wins, the crowd goes wild. The Leh polo ground provides a spectacular setting. It is at the top of the town, and from the grandstand spectators look across to the bare foothills of the Ladakh range. To the left, on its rocky spur, is the square-hewn bulk of Sengge NamgyaTs palace, and crowning the peak behind it the temple and ruined fort of Nam­ gyal Tsemo. Here matches are played in the late afternoon when the sun, slanting across the ground, casts shadows that appear almost three-dimensional against the golden clouds of dust raised by the drumming hooves. There is no barrier to keep the crowds who throng to every match off the field; when the ball goes out, it is simply picked up and thrown back by someone from the mass of onlookers. Often the spectators are forced to retreat, swaying back against the press behind, when the mel^e of men and horses, intent on the ball, spills over the line, and right among them. At the start and finish of play, and to celebrate every goal, players of suma and daman strike up special polo music, which adds a final touch of excitement to an occasion whose atmosphere is unique. It all adds up to something as different from international polo as a goncha from a three-piece suit. And there is one other striking difference. Balti-style polo as played in Ladakh is not a rich man’s

POLYANDRY AND POLO 153 game. The Chushotis and others who make up the local teams are for the most part small farmers, or school masters, or shop­ keepers, or are employed in the various departments of the district administration as office messengers, drivers or syces. A few of the better off have their own horses, some of them very fine animals indeed; others ride departmental horses, or those belonging to the Polo Club. These players are unassuming men, without preten­ sions of any kind; but after a victory they walk as tall as kings, for polo is one of the things they live for. Notes and References 1. See below, p. 162. 2. John Crook, ‘Tradition, Development and Conservation in Ladakh’, in Crook and Osmaston, Himalayan Buddhist Villages, p. 804. 3. See below p. 220 for a discussion of the relationship between monastery and village. 4. Peter Fleming, News from Tartary, p. 369.

9 The Native Genius T he culture of the Ladakhi people is an intriguing mixture of Tibetan and non-Tibetan elements. Ladakh is an ex­ tension of the great Tibetan plateau, and is separated from Tibet’s religious and cultural centre at Lhasa only by a matter of distance, some 1500 kilometres, and not by any formidable phys­ ical barrier, such as the Great Himalaya which cuts it off from India. The population of central and eastern Ladakh is of pre- ponderandy Tibetan racial stock, and their language is an archaic form of Tibetan. Spoken, Ladakhi is said to have a somewhat similar relation to the speech of Lhasa as that which ancient Greek would have with modern Greek. Its distinguishing characteristic seems to be that numerous consonants, which survive in the spelling of standard Tibetan but have been elided from its spoken word, continue to be pronounced in Ladakhi. Even within Ladakh, there is a range of dialects, all mutually comprehensible; in general, the further west you go, into Purig and across the Line of Control into Baltistan, the more archaic and consonant-filled is the speech, while that of Zanskar is full of quaint and flowery idioms. Traditionally, Ladakhi has had no written form distinct from classical Tibetan. But of recent years a number of Ladakhi scholars have started adapting the Tibetan script to the uses of the colloquial tongue; and there is at least one periodical in Leh, Ladags Melong> the organ of the Students’ Educational and Cul­ tural Movement of Ladakh (SECMOL), which does the same. It would seem to be of the first importance for Ladakh’s educational and cultural development that its spoken tongue should achieve the dignity of a recognized written form. In the field of religious culture, the Tibetan influence remained paramount, in spite of the enormous distance between Ladakh and central Tibet, involving a journey of between three and four months. As over the whole area of Tibetan culture, from Bhutan

THE NATIVE GENIUS 155 Then as now, Ladakhi women dance to the strains ofsurna and daman played by Mon musicians: a sketch by a Ladakhi artist early this century. to Ladakh, from Mustang to Mongolia, the control exercised over the monasteries o f the various religious orders by their chief abbots in central Tibet inhibited any possible local initiatives in the direction o f developing distinctive forms o f the parent culture. This seems to have come about due partly to a unifying and centralizing tendency in the organization o f the religion itself, and partly to the deliberate policy o f a line o f kings which never, over the near-millennium o f its rule, lost sight o f the fact that it was an offshoot o f the ancient Tibetan dynasty. In the earlier centuries, the second o f these factors was perhaps predominant. For as long as Buddhism remained a living religion in India, the land o f its origin, both central and western Tibet continued to look in that direction for inspiration as to the fountainhead o f their faith. W hen that fountain dried up, as it did about the twelfth century, the religious establishment of central Tibet assumed a kind of pontifical authority such as was not known elsewhere in the Buddhist world. As far as Ladakh was

156 LADAKH concerned, this development was certainly fostered by the kings of the first dynasty, with what motivation or under what compul­ sion we can hardly guess. The early-established system of novices being sent to monasteries in central Tibet to get their higher qualifications lasted till the 1950s, and was surely the most effec­ tive of the various channels through which Tibet’s religious culture was transmitted to Ladakh. By the fifteenth century all the Ladakhi monasteries were affiliated to one or other of the monastic orders of central Tibet, of which the most important were the Ge-lugs-pa and the Drug-pa, the latter being particularly favoured by the Namgyal kings. Thus firm institutional links between Ladakh and Tibet were forged, which chimed in well with the predispositions of the dynasty, and which seem to indicate that Vajrayana Bud­ dhism, no less perhaps than Roman Catholicism, had developed a highly structured organization with authority radiating from the centre to the peripheries. The authority claimed by the Tibetan pontiffs in the chief monasteries of the different orders seems not to have been confined to doctrinal matters, but to have extended to the smallest details of ritual and iconography. Thus, to the extent that cultural forms were dependent on religion, it constituted a cultural as well as a religious stranglehold. With the exception of whatever paint­ ings and images remain in the earliest monasteries — those of Rin-chen-zang-po’s time, of which Alchi is the prime example — all the works of art in the gompas of Ladakh reflect the Tibetan style, and this is as true of the work of the present time as it is of past centuries. Religious painting is a living tradition, in the sense that it is carried on even today; but it is carried on strictly within the bounds laid down by an iconography that has ceased to develop. Every gesture of the figures represented, every detail of the background, every choice of colour, has a symbolic meaning, and there is no room for individual interpretation. There is a sameness about the murals in almost all the gompas, whether they depict the divine beings in their terrible or their benign aspect. The eye becomes surfeited with ravening jaws, flaming tresses, skull head-dresses, trampled and bleeding foes; with plump white clouds, five-pointed crowns, delicate hand gestures, the multi­ plicity of limbs and heads. The thangkas too — devotional paint­ ings on cloth, a kind of Buddhist icon — repeat the same motifs endlessly. The various styles of painting that an art historian can
