Behold in the courtyard, we youths and maidens are disporting ourselves with water. Who are those who presume to forbid us? Why should we not rejoice with water? Who knows how long this life will last? Why should we not rejoice with water? Who will live for ever in this world? (Slightly edited) Taru, My Home On the heights of Taru, my home, Up there on the heights a glacier is forming.

172 LADAKH When the glacier forms on the heights, It will give birth to a turquoise lake. When the lake forms on the low lands, And from the lake flows out bubbling water, Bubbling dancing water, It shows how blessed is Taru with good fortune. This is the stream of Taru, my home; Flowing impetuously, foaming and roaring. But my love, if you pass by, For you it will run gently and slow. Notes and References 1. Mark Trewin, ‘Rhythmic Style in Ladakhi Music and Dance’, in Schwalbe and Meier (eds), Wisscnschaftsgeschichte und gegenwartige Forschungcn in Nordwest-Indien. 2. Zi-Zi was a title given to Muslim queens of Ladakh, of whom there were several after Gyal Katun, Sengge Namgyal’s mother.

1 0 Change T he balance with the natural environment which is the basic characteristic of Ladakhi life is today threatened by the forces of change and so-called progress which no ancient society, brought face to face with the modern world, can hope to escape. As with all long-established pre-modern societies, that of Ladakh has evolved over centuries by a process of constant refine­ ment of response to the existing climatic and environmental con­ ditions; in the case of Ladakh, these involve severe limitations as regards both the climate and the carrying capacity of the land. The social and economic systems, the patterns of work and family life that generations of Ladakhis have developed to cope with these, constitute a seamless whole, which will be affected in its entirety by change wrought in any of its parts. But change is already coming, and radical change at that. Some change is due simply to the new linkages established between Ladakh and the rest of the world; other aspects to the deliberate application of modern techniques of economic development by a number of Government agencies, supplemented since the mid-1980s by the efforts of several organizations operating on a voluntary basis. While it may be assumed that all those involved in the process of ‘development’, Ladakhi or non-Ladakhi, freelance or working under the auspices of Government, share a common commitment to improving matters for the land and its people, there may be occasional doubts as to the wisdom or appropriateness of some of the programmes being applied. So delicate is the existing balance that clumsy tampering with it in the name of development is quite likely to do more harm than good. The Ladakhis’ methods of meeting the challenges posed by their inhospitable environment — methods evolved by painful trial and error over centuries if not millennia — have stood the test of time. It is the developer’s job to understand these, and build upon them.

174 LADAKH Student volunteers contribute their labour to the construction o f the school at the SECMOL village 15 km from Leh. Development must be based on local resources, local knowledge and local perceptions; it must take existing social and work pat­ terns into consideration; it must avoid a narrowly materialistic view o f‘progress’; it must foster self-reliance rather than increasing dependence on outside economic forces. Any other kind of ‘development’ can only be counter-productive, and can only end by destroying the society it sets out to improve. One programme about which there must be serious doubts is the distribution of imported foodgrains at subsidized rates. It is probably true that, whatever the excellence of its methods and its results, Ladakhi agriculture — whether or not it did in the past render the country self-sufficient in foodgrains — can no longer by itself support today’s growing population. And it is equally true that on the whole the Ladakhis themselves, especially the less well-off, welcome the Public Distribution System, which surely provides a cushion for the poor, especially in a year of bad harvest. On the other hand, the easy availability, and the cheapness, o f the

CHANGE 175 imported rice and wheat-flour — together with the various pres­ sures taking young people away from the land — can only serve to undermine traditional agriculture, and skew the natural pattern of supply and demand. In pre-modern Ladakh, whatever was imported was paid for at its market value, even though the country had only two exportable products — pashm and dried apricots. The never-ending circuits of the subsistence traders spread the available resources over every region, and ensured that, though by no standards a rich country, Ladakh’s economic independence was absolute. Today, thanks to the tourist industry, Ladakh is probably more prosperous than ever before. No one can argue against the provision of security systems for the really poor. But equally, in an uncertain world, it would seem difficult to justify a programme that tends to erode self-reliance and encourage dependence on hand-outs. Basic to Government’s development policies has been the im­ provement of communications. In the past, the simple needs of far-flung populations could be met either locally, or in the form of loads carried in on the backs of animals or men. Consequendy wheels, and the roads necessary for wheeled transport, were ir­ relevant. In the old days too, rivers proved formidable obstacles, often swirling through deep chasms, almost always rushing too impetuously over their stony beds to make fording safe or easy. Travellers’ tales of the last century are full of exciting accounts of river-crossings by rope-bridges swaying precariously over the foaming torrent beneath. But today, things are different. After twenty-five years of planned development, some three-quarters of all the villages of the two districts are now linked by a network of roads, most of them not metalled, it is true, but fit for heavy-duty vehicles; and the jhoola bridges of the old days are being replaced by steel girders. A considerable proportion of the development budget of the two districts continues to be devoted to the road- and bridge-building which is the sine qua non for implementing development projects in far-flung areas. The tapping of potential sources of power is another aspect of development on which Government is laying stress, with the construction of run-of-the-river hydroelectric projects, for which there seems to be a lot of scope. The ambitious (and expensive) four-megawatt Stakna Project on the Indus above Leh, one of the highest in the world, was plagued with problems from its very

176 LADAKH inception, and for many years the local people regarded it as little more than a bad joke. But now, although it has to be closed for nearly three months in winter when the frost-reduced flow of water is insufficient to spin the turbines, and a further month in summer to deal with the heavy load of silt brought down by the river, it does for the rest of the year provide a reasonable supply of power to Leh and the surrounding villages. At Kargil town, the long talked-about 3.75 megawatt Iqbal Power Project went on line in 1995; and there are not only plans for a giant 12-megawatt project at Chutuk, a village a few kilometres up the Suru from Kargil, but even talk of a 60-megawatt monster in the same region. Elsewhere, there is a welcome emphasis on smaller projects, be­ tween 100 kilowatts and three megawatts; and field trials of portable turbines, capable of generating five kilowatts, have shown encouraging results. At the same time, the Ladakh Ecological Development Group (LEDeG), Ladakh’s first home-grown voluntary agency, has initiated a programme of micro-hydel plants of under 10 kilowatts serving small villages; about 20 have already been installed by LEDeG staff, using local labour for the in­ frastructural work, and their operation and maintenance handed over to the village community. In winter, Leh is served by an old and noisy diesel-generating station, as are some villages without a convenient source of hydroelectricity; in others, where the dwellings are very scattered, individual solar photo-voltaic conversion sets are being installed. This startlingly high-tech programme was initiated by a Rajas­ than-based voluntary agency, the Social Work and Research Cen­ tre (SWRC), Tilonia; it has been so successful that it has now been taken up seriously by the administrations of the two districts. In the two districts nearly 3000 houses in the remotest areas now have after-dark lighting, powered by solar energy converted by photo-voltaic cells and stored in batteries, sufficient to run two light-points for about four hours; while the tent-dwelling Chang- pa are provided with similar solar-powered lanterns. The use of solar energy was pioneered by LEDeG. Emphasizing the importance of self-reliance and the use of renewable resources, the organization has, since its foundation in 1983, developed and popularized solar greenhouses and crop-dryers, solar cookers and water-heating systems, and solar space-heating by means of the black-painted and glass-covered south-facing walls known as

CHANGE 177 Trombe walls. All of these seem to be compatible with traditional methods, and have been not only widely accepted by the Ladakhis, but also taken up by the official development agencies of Govern­ ment. One particularly popular innovation has been the low-cost greenhouses — little more than sheets of heavy-duty UV-resistant polythene stretched over a low brick-wall infrastructure — of which several thousand have already been installed, with the aim that by the end of the century they will be available to every interested household. They serve to increase the growing season for vegetables by at least four months, in some cases making it last all year round; they also make possible the cultivation of exotic species like tomatoes. Ladakh is essentially a biomass-based economy, and any effec­ tive drive towards material progress must be largely directed towards agriculture and animal husbandry. As far as agriculture is concerned, the main constraint is the extreme shortage of water for irrigation, and hence of cultivable land. This is an area where the administration can, and indeed does, play a useful role, plan­ ning and executing large-scale irrigation schemes which are beyond the resources of private enterprise. The scantiness of the rainfall makes Ladakhi agriculture totally dependent on irrigation; but all those sources that lie within the scope of traditional tech­ nology have already been tapped. The 1980s saw work starting on two major projects, close to the towns of Kargil and Leh. The Khurbathang Canal, which taps the Wakha stream between Mul- bekh and Pashkyum to carry water sixteen kilometres to the barren plateau across the Suru river from Kargil, was complete by the end of the decade. This has brought nearly a thousand hectares under the plough, about half of which has been distributed among the local people, the remainder being worked by various official agencies. The alluvial silt of which the Khurbathang plateau is composed is so rich that cereals are said to yield forty-fold, probab­ ly more than twice the rate of increase in most areas of Ladakh; however the landholders are being encouraged to go in rather for cash crops of vegetables and fruit — especially apricots — which also do well there. Similarly with the Igu-Phey Project, planned to irrigate 4,800 hectares along the gently sloping alluvial tract, some 25 kilometres in length, on the left bank of the Indus opposite Leh, the first phase of which was completed in 1995. In consideration of the fact that its water comes from the Indus,

178 LADAKH which runs low in March and April when barley-fields have the greatest need for irrigation, the emphasis in Igu-Phey is being laid on the cultivation of fodder crops, principally the indigenous ol (lucerne), which takes most of its water in June, when Indus water is plentiful. This is likely to lead to a considerable increase of livestock and livestock products in the command area. The Igu- Phey canal doubles up as the water-supply for the three-megawatt Igu-Martselang hydroelectric project, which when complete will — it is hoped — enable the engineers to close the Stakna plant for long enough to construct silt-exclusion facilities. As well as these major projects, the administrations of the two districts have taken up a number of smaller schemes for the development of water resources, and have created irrigation poten­ tial over an area of nearly 5000 hectares, through the construction of canals and water-harvesting tanks. Some of the non-official agencies whose activities since the mid-1980s have been such a heartening feature of the Ladakh scene, are also undertaking in­ tegrated development schemes in limited areas, involving the application of modern appropriate technologies — including hydraulic tam pumps and solar pumps — to utilize as yet un­ tapped sources of water. For drinking purposes, the SWRC un­ dertook a programme of drilling for groundwater in Kargil town, with such success that the district administration commissioned it to install 25 handpumps. The search for groundwater has been extended to some relatively remote areas, and handpumps are said to be functioning successfully even in the vicinity of Durbuk, at nearly 4000 metres, in the freezing cold of winter. To supplement the water supply in Leh town, which now, coming mainly from natural springs, amounts to about half the actual requirement, an ambitious scheme to lift water from the Indus has been on the anvil since 1992, and is expected to be operational by the end of the century. Right from the start the development process all over India has relied heavily on Western-style technological interventions. In spite of the established excellence of Ladakhi crop husbandry official policy has taken for granted that it could be improved by the use of chemical fertilizers and the introduction of high-yield­ ing varieties of crops. Today in the Leh district, of which the total cropped area is about 17,000 hectares, the annual consumption of nitrogenous fertilizers fluctuates around 2000 tonnes, and of

CHANGE 179 phosphatic fertilizers between 500 and 1000 tonnes; the Agricul­ ture Department estimates that their contribution to the produc­ tivity of the district is in the region of 30 per cent. (The Kargil people seem to have less enthusiasm for the use of these fertilizers, having consumed only 461 tonnes in 1995.) The area under high-yielding varieties (HYV) — i.e. hybrid varieties introduced from outside — in the Leh district has risen from 16 hectares in 1981-2 to 4130 hectares in 1995-6, more than half being ac­ counted for by wheat, and the balance by grim and vegetables. In Kargil too the success of the HYV programme has been mainly in wheat. Considering the several varieties of barley already avail­ able in the two districts, their characteristics varying subtly ac­ cording to micro-local conditions, it seems hardly surprising that, even after 15 years of effort, so little headway has been made in identifying further ‘appropriate’ varieties. Even without the use of chemical fertilizers and HYV, the yield of cereal husbandry in Ladakh, which is believed to be about three tonnes per hectare, on average, is one of the highest in the world, and researchers wonder whether there is actually much room for improvement. These interventions, with their suggestion of the ‘technological fix’, and their perceived potential to disturb deli­ cately balanced ecological and social systems, are among the ones which have drawn the most criticism from environmentalists and other concerned observers of the Ladakh scene. LEDeG among its other activities has information and awareness-raising program­ mes for farmers, laying emphasis on the problems associated with high-tech agriculture as well as the excellence of the existing methods, and suggesting only those improvements that seem to be compatible with traditional systems. There are no doubt in the official hierarchy individuals who are sensitive to these concerns, and today words like ‘appropriate’ and ‘sustainable’ do appear regularly in Government documents. But it seems unlikely that any radical change of official mindset is in the offing — unless indeed the Leh Autonomous Hill Development Council were to make a conscious decision in favour of a ‘green’ mode of develop­ ment. It need not be assumed that all the Government interventions in the field of agriculture are as environmentally inappropriate as many people perceive the fertilizer and HYV programmes to be. One of the most sensible and pertinent programmes, very much

180 LADAKH in keeping with Ladakh’s traditions from the days when thousands of pack-animals regularly traversed Ladakh in the trade caravans, is the development of fodder production. Several varieties of lucerne (ol 'm Ladakhi) exist, of which the one known as Yarkandi ol is preferred, because it is perennial for a good many years, thus requiring a minimum of labour. In the late 1970s the Agriculture Department established a number of plots for the multiplication of its seed; twenty years later the seed production on private fields is measured in tens of quintals, and the area under ol rose from 26 hectares in 1981—2 to 1600 hectares in 1995-6. Similarly, though the scope for forestry is limited, whatever plantation has been done — along roads and river-banks and wherever the administration owns a patch of irrigated land — has been confined to the traditionally grown trees, poplar and willow, to both of which ancient methods of coppicing, peculiar to Ladakh, are applied, so that they produce repeated crops of fuel- wood and timber. Even before recent research revealed the mar­ vellous potential of seabuckthorn for water and soil conservation, and as one of the richest known sources of vitamin C, the ad­ ministration had embarked on a programme of plantation of this indigenous shrub. In the environment of Ladakh, where biomass is in such short supply, fuel is a critical consideration and too often the villagers have no choice but to burn animal dung. Seabuckthorn, which grows like a weed, can help to bridge the gap, thus making the dung available for its more appropriate use as natural fertilizer. The apricot programme is another example of development building on what Ladakh already has to offer. Dried apricots are Ladakh’s only cash crop apart from pashm, and since the 1970s, the district administrations have devoted a lot of attention to the fruit, experimenting with new strains and new methods of graft­ ing, as well as with improved methods of drying it. The area under apricot orchards is also being expanded, especially in the Kargil district. Even with all this, the production seems insufficient to meet the all-India demand, and it is difficult to find Ladakhi apricots in the bazaars of cities outside Jammu and Kashmir. As a result partly of the tourist boom, and partly of the establishment of marketing networks outside Ladakh, the return to the orchard- owner has increased dramatically, the price of apricots now being more than ten times that of 1976.

CHANGE 181 Given that agriculture is inseparable from livestock rearing, the two elements forming a single integrated system, it follows that animal husbandry is an equally crucial area for development. Apart from increasing fodder production, the administration’s efforts have mosdy been directed towards improving, where appropriate, the local breeds of livestock. But this is a matter to be approached with care, and Ladakh itself provides a cautionary tale in the fate of the Purig sheep. This little animal made a great impression on Moorcroft and Cunningham for though small, it gave a good quantity of wool — two clips a year — and its meat was par­ ticularly delicious. It gave two lambs a year and its great advantage was that it could be kept at practically no expense, as it was happy to eat almost anything — leaves, grass, straw, household refuse like vegetable parings and spent tea-leaves; Moorcroft even saw one nibble a bone. Opinions differ as to whether this litde prodigy is still extant in some of the remoter villages of the Kargil district. Certainly it is no longer as common as in the early nineteenth century — possibly because of well-intentioned but ill-conceived attempts to ‘upgrade’ it; more likely because of interbreeding between the local animals and the sheep of the Bakarwals, nomadic herdspeople who bring their flocks up in summer from the Jammu region, through the valleys of Purig to the high pastures of Dras and Rangdum. Like the nearly defunct Purig sheep, most of the breeds actually existing in Ladakh are well adapted to their environment. It would certainly be rash to assume that the introduction of exotic blood would in every instance improve upon the result of centuries of selective breeding by generation after generation of herdspeople, all of them having presumably a shrewd eye for the desirable qualities they were aiming for. A case in point is the chang-luk sheep, which constitutes a good part of the wealth of the Chang- thang nomads. This big-boned animal lives comfortably at 4500 metres, thriving on the nutritious grasses of the upland pastures, and giving a large quantity of rather coarse wool, which the herdspeople use for their own clothing, and sell to traders from Ladakh, where it is valued for its softness and warmth. It is also sold down-country, as its long staple makes it a useful raw material for the carpet industry. Until Chang-thang is fully opened up by motorable roads, its value as a pack-animal will endure. In short, it is an animal ideally suited to its environment. It is pleasant that

182 LADAKH this fact has been unequivocally recognized by the district authorities, so that plans, mindlessly conceived, to send merino sheep to Chang-thang to ‘upgrade’ the chang-luk stock were aban­ doned several years ago. On the contrary, the chang-luk has been recognized as an original stock which could be used to upgrade the sheep population of other highland areas outside Ladakh.1 Pashm, Ladakh’s only export apart from dried apricots, is the Chang-thang herdspeople’s main source of cash income. This valuable product, the basis of the multi-crore shawl industry of Srinagar, has had a decisive impact on Ladakh’s history, and probably provides the explanation why successive rulers of Kashmir, from Shah Jahan onwards, were so anxious to establish their suzerainty over this otherwise unpromising territory. From at least the seventeenth century the trade in pashm was controlled in favour of a caucus of Kashmiri middlemen, known as Tibet Baqals; even in the post-Independence era, the State Government tried, by means of its Pashmina Control Order, to maintain the salient feature of the system established under the Treaty of Tingmosgang in 1684 — namely that there should be no movement of pashm outside Jammu and Kashmir. This meant in effect that Ladakh’s entire output of pashm was reserved for the Srinagar shawl industry. It is difficult to imagine that, the tracks and passes of the high Himalaya being as remote and rugged as they are, the Pashmina Control Order was ever rigorously implemented; nor indeed is there evidence that the shawl industry of Himachal Pradesh was starved of pashm during the period (1953 to 1986) when it was in force. Although Government did attempt to maintain the price by lifting a certain proportion of the production, even the partial monopoly established by the Order, which as of old continued to benefit the Tibet Baqals, was enough to prevent the price of pashm finding its natural level, and probably to keep it lower than it should have been, thus short-changing the herdspeople, the primary producers. Since 1986 the trade has been unfettered; and 1995 saw the formation of the All Chang-thang Pashmina Growers’ Co-operative Marketing Society, which succeeded that year in negotiating an all-time high price of Rs 1700 per kilogram for first quality pashm. (This may be compared with the prices current in the years before the Tibet border was sealed in 1962, when Ladakhi traders could buy best-quality

CHANGE 183 pashm from western Tibet at Rs 50-60 per bhatti [1.8 kilo­ grams], and sell it in Leh for Rs 160-200 per bhatti.) It is only in the very recent past that any serious research into either the Chang-pa lifestyle, or the ecology of the pastures has been undertaken. Study of the grasslands ecology is a matter of particular urgency, especially in view of the recent opening up of Rupshu to tourists, both those travelling the Manali-Leh road, and those taking excursions from Leh. It may not indeed take very rigorous investigation to confirm the surmise that the ecology of these high-altitude grasslands is fragile, and liable to be gravely damaged both by the wheels of motor vehicles, and by the addi­ tional load imposed on them by the uncontrolled grazing of the ponies brought by tourist groups. There are other problems too. In the fairly recent past overgrazing has been an issue, especially since the Chinese occupation of Tibet forced some of the western Tibet Chang-pa to cross the border as refugees, bringing their livestock with them; and the Chang-pa are said to have com­ plained of the official policy of conserving the kyang; the wild ass, the increase in whose numbers has also tended to put pressure on the grasslands. The yield of the Chang-thang goat is very low, somewhere in the region of 200-300 grams of pashm annually; however the temptation to ‘upgrade’ the breed by the introduction of exotic stock has so far been resisted. Its pashm is finer than that of the central Asian and Mongolian goats, which yield a larger quantity, and the soft and delicate fabric it produces is at a premium. The main intervention on the part of the administration has been the establishment of farms for the production of superior animals through selective breeding, and up to 1995 some 2000 such bucks had been distributed to the herdspeople for the improvement of their flocks. Other means of helping the herdspeople of Chang-thang are the construction of kidding-sheds, to bring down the painfully high mortality among the kids, born in the coldest months of winter; and the establishment of fodder-banks, to provide feed for the animals in those winters — say one out of five or six — during which there is enough snow to cover the scanty pasturage on which they browse in winter as well as summer. These two programmes, implemented since 1979, have already led to a dramatic fall in mortality, and increase in the number of goats. This naturally is

184 LADAKH another factor tending to increase the pressure on the pastures, which underlines the urgency of the need for a comprehensive study of the grasslands, and if possible ecologically appropriate programmes to develop them. As for the ordinary goats, sheep and cows of the villages, they possess no such remarkable qualities as the Chang-thang animals, so there should be no theoretical objection to improving them by the introduction of exotic blood. Cross-bred merino rams are being successfully introduced to increase the wool yield of the local sheep; while there are programmes to increase the milk yields of the local goats and cattle by cross-breeding with exotic dairy breeds. Jersey cows do well in Ladakh, but in the field the farmer’s top priority is the production of dzo to yoke to the plough; it is only after enough of his cows have been put to the yak for this purpose that he is willing to use the services of the cross-bred Jersey bull. In the mid-1990s experimental programmes to explore the possibilities of using artificial insemination for the breeding programmes are being tried. There are breeding farms set up by the two administrations for pure and cross-bred Jersey cattle, Zanskar ponies, yak and, regrettably, Karakul sheep — which are not even an indigenous breed. There is also an Angora goat farm at Stakna where — apart from mundane activities like the dis­ tribution of bucks to villagers — experiments in cross-breeding are going on, with a view to the production of a new fibre: cashgorcu The civil administration is not the only agent of economic change in Ladakh. The impact of the armed forces — Army, Air Force and paramilitary forces — on the local economy, especially of the Leh district, has been considerable. Comprising an es­ timated 30,000-40,000 men, they have created a bulk demand for fruit and vegetables which has been met by a number of co-operative marketing societies, the total value of the business done in 1994-5 being an impressive 74 lakhs of rupees. The Army has also to some extent replaced the merchants of former times as customers of Ladakh’s traditional transporter communities. Many of their pickets are far beyond the network of motorable roads, and supplies are regularly carried up to them by horse- and donkey-transport belonging to the people of nearby villages, whether in the Dras-Kargil belt, Mulbekh and Bodh Kharbu in Kargil district, or in Nubra and other border areas of Leh district.

CHANGE 185 The transporters do even better from the army connection than the fruit-growers, the business conducted by the Ladakh Transport Co-operative Society in 1994—5 amounting to no less than 400 lakh. The vegetable-growers’ and transporters’ co-operatives are only a few of the co-operative societies that have been established in Ladakh since 1964. The flourishing co-operative movement in both the districts, but especially Leh, can be ascribed partly to the direction provided by an unusually committed departmental staff; and partly perhaps to the traditional spirit of co-operation which is such a striking feature of Ladakhi village life. A lot of the societies, it has to be admitted, are involved in the distribution of chemical fertilizers and sale of consumer goods. But there are many more in which local artisans, often women engaged in making shawls or carpets, have come together to pool their expertise and make the most of limited facilities by sharing them; and others in which the producers, whether of vegetables, pashm or transport, are able to negotiate with a single voice, thus ensuring to all a fair price for what they have to offer. In the rural areas of the Leh district, about 75 per cent of all families are involved in one or other of the 100-odd co-operative societies. It is their health and education programmes, more perhaps than the rest of the development programmes put together, that enable the administrations of the two districts to touch the lives of virtually every individual. These are particularly sensitive areas, in which inappropriate action can have an almost illimitable poten­ tial for harm; while conversely, enlightened policies, sensitively implemented, which take local conditions and the perceptions of the local people into consideration, may surely enhance both the economic and the cultural life of the region. An exciting develop­ ment since the mid-1980s has been the encouragement given to the traditional system of medicine — the Tibetan amchi system. In old Ladakh, amchis, like monks, used to proceed to Lhasa to get their training in the medical college there; when this ceased to be possible after the Chinese occupation of Tibet in 1959, the system began to run down. Its revival was initiated in the Leh district by the Leh Nutrition Project (LNP) of the Save the Children Fund, an international agency active in the region since

186 LADAKH 1979 and staffed mainly (now exclusively) by local people. The LNP’s initiative has been given solid backing by the Leh district administration, and links have been established with doctors and health administrators at the main Tibetan refugee settlement at Dharamsala in Himachal Pradesh. Not only has an amchi associa­ tion with its own training centre been established, but amchi health workers are employed by Government; and, most hearten­ ing of all, there is a definite trend for the younger generation of traditional amchi families, who previously were drifting away, to return to their ancestral vocation. Apart from amchis, Ladakhis started going in for Western medicine from about the 1960s, and many more qualified in the following decades, almost all of them opting to return and make their career in Ladakh. In the mid-1990s, the health services of the two districts are largely staffed by local people. As far as modern hospital care is concerned, the intense cold of winter and the shortage of running water are severe problems. Nevertheless, qualified observers report that the staffs of the district hospitals in Leh and Kargil do a fine job in difficult circumstances. The Sonam Narboo Memorial Hospital in Leh, built in 1979, is gradually being equipped with solar heating and under-floor central heating, which will make things a lot easier. Similar improvements are being made to the hospital in Kargil, and there are also plans for a new district hospital to be built on the Khurbathang plateau. Although in theory these hospitals serve their entire districts, the difficulties of communication limit their effective outreach to those areas within easy travelling distance. The people of remoter villages, many of them cut off completely from the district towns for several months in winter, are less well provided for. Medical services are available in the subdivisional headquarters, and in the Leh district each of these now has a local woman doctor. At the local level, the people’s basic health needs are met by para-medical workers, in primary health centres, medical aid centres and sub­ centres, dispensaries, and amchi centres. There are still villages so remote as to be outside this network; these are to be covered by community health centres, each staffed by a local person who has undergone training in the district hospital. In order to increase the acceptance of the system by the village women, especially in Muslim regions, there is emphasis on using the services of women in all these local centres. Public health interventions include a

CHANGE 187 WHO-sponsored leprosy eradication programme, TB control programmes, vaccination programmes, and mother-and-child welfare programmes. In all of these, the local health administra­ tions have had willing co-operation from the SCF. An urgent need is the development of an appropriate philosophy of education to ensure that the school curriculum is relevant to Ladakh, and also to help preserve its endangered heritage of music and oral literature. One cannot wish Ladakh to be cut off from the world as it actually exists, but the world-view presented to its children must be a balanced one, showing in true perspective the failures as well as the achievements of the material culture that is encroaching so rapidly on their lives. At the same time, a local orientation should inculcate pride in Ladakh’s traditional culture, and in a society which, striking a happy balance between individual and community rights and duties, has recent­ ly been described as showing a striking similarity to ‘Gandhi’s Utopia, the ideal he believed had existed only in the agrarian villages of ancient India.’2 Unfortunately, there is no evidence that the Education Depart­ ment which is largely manned by outsiders at the higher levels, is applying its mind seriously to evolving an education adapted to Ladakh’s peculiar conditions. . . . It is probably too much to expect Government to appreciate the damage that can be done by inappropriate educational policies. The initiative in evolving a type of education peculiarly suited to Ladakh’s needs may have to come from the Ladakhis themselves. The above passage from the earlier edition of this book was written in the early 1980s with, it must be admitted, more hope than confidence that such initiative would actually be forthcom­ ing. Twelve years on, it is a great pleasure to record how unjustified was the lack of confidence in the possibility of an appropriate Ladakhi response to a situation that was clearly becoming in­ tolerable. Till very recently, the ‘modern education* on offer added up to little more than a watered-down version of the mindless pedagogy, based on rote-learning, and derived ultimately from outdated Western models, that is current almost everywhere in India. In Ladakh, the problem was compounded by the fact that there is no standard written form of colloquial Ladakhi, thus from the start the children were obliged to pursue their studies in a foreign language, Urdu (the official language of the state ad­ ministration); by the time they might be presumed to have ac­ quired some proficiency in Urdu and its difficult script, they were obliged to switch to English for the higher secondary stage. The

188 LADAKH principal achievement of the village schools appeared to be to devalue the indigenous social and material culture in the eyes of the pupils, alienate them from the agriculture-based life and work of their homes, and in the end fail to qualify them for the Govern­ ment employment which had become for many their main goal. In both districts the failure-rate at school-leaving level (Class X) has for many years been around 85-90 per cent, while of those managing to scrape through, barely half have succeeded in qualify­ ing for college entrance (Class XII). The demoralization of young people, ten years of whose lives have been spent in unfitting them to play any useful role either within Ladakh or elsewhere, can be imagined. It is as if all the conditions for the creation of a huge lumpen underclass have been put purposely in place. The first small dent in this dismal scenario was made by the private Lam-dron School in Leh, founded in the mid-1970s, which eliminated Urdu from the curriculum, making English the medium of instruction right from the primary classes, and intro­ ducing the study of literary Tibetan. A few years later, with the assistance of the German Moravian Church, which retained fond memories of its defunct mission in Ladakh, a Christian school was founded, at which the quality of instruction was a distinct improvement on what was on offer in the Government schools. Similarly with the Suru Valley Public School, and other schools founded by groups of private citizens in the Kargil district, in­ cluding one at Dras. Meanwhile, there was an increasing number of Ladakhi students who somehow succeeded in making good, though many of them were from affluent families who had had their schooling outside the region, before carrying on to institu­ tions of higher learning or training. One of the most heartening things about contemporary Ladakh is that almost all of these have opted to return; they contribute to the growing Ladakhi presence in the administration and the professions, especially medicine. The recent establishment of colleges in Leh and Kargil means that now it is possible to get a degree without having to spend three of the most formative years outside Ladakh. But it was the efforts of a group of young Ladakhis, some of them the victims of the system, and others who had somehow escaped from it who, pooling their energies and ideas, determined to try to change the system from the bottom up. Constituting themselves into an organization, the Students’ Educational and

CHANGE 189 Cultural Movement of Ladakh (SECMOL), their achievement since they got started in 1988 has been impressive. Sensibly, they decided that, rather than setting up a whole chain of new institu­ tions, they should use the existing infrastructure of Government schools as a basis to work from. Accordingly they secured the co-operation of the Leh administration in launching Operation New Hope (ONH), with the aim of transforming the Govern­ ment system so as to render the education it imparts relevant and effective. Under ONH, village education committees have been formed to involve local communities, training and reorientation camps for teachers are held, the primary-school curriculum is being modified, and a beginning has been made in drawing up a series of textbooks relevant to Ladakh. By the summer of 1995, ONH principles were being implemented in no fewer than 35 government schools, with 80 more expected to be covered by the end of the year. SECMOL also runs education camps for young people who have been left high and dry by the system, and is creating, almost entirely by volunteer labour contributed by its students, an Alternative Village, some 12 or 15 kilometres out of Leh. The aim of this project is to provide a practical demonstration of traditional principles of self-sufficiency in agriculture and hor­ ticulture, as well as appropriate construction methods; it will also have a school running courses for students and teachers. The vision behind these programmes is remarkable; and if they are carried out with the energy and commitment with which they have been initiated, surely they have the potential to transform the prospects open to the younger generation of Ladakhis. SECMOL is only one of the self-help non-government organiza­ tions (NGOs) whose growth during the 1980s and early 90s has been such an encouraging feature of the Ladakh scene. The first of them was the Save the Children Fund, a UK-based interna­ tional development organization, which started work in Leh in 1979. Although its project in the Leh district is called the Leh Nutrition Project, its emphasis over the years has shifted from a narrow focus on nutrition, to integrated community develop­ ment, based largely on traditional values. Hence its work on reviving the amchi system. It also works closely with the local administration, especially in the field of public health. An

190 LADAKH innovative project in rural development has been the setting up of a co-operative in Shey and Chushot for the production of tsestalulu (seabuckthorn) juice. The seabuckthorn berry is one of the richest known sources of vitamin C; as well as encouraging the commercial exploitation of this, the LNP procures a significant proportion of the product for distribution to the Chang-pa, whose diet is generally deficient in this important nutrient. As far as education is concerned, it aims to fill some of the gaps in the Government system. It has sponsored some bright boys from far-flung villages to study in Leh, and in co-operation with the Lions’ Club has set up a hostel in Leh for such boys. The first two directors of the LNP were from the UK — remarkable characters both — but since 1985 it has been run entirely by local people; it is now an autonomous NGO, though maintaining close links with the SCF which continues to fund it. The LNP is pursuing the current SCF strategy, which lays less emphasis on funding, and more on the attempt to involve communities in income-generating projects. The SCF has also been active in Kargil since 1988, though it was not till well on into the 90s that its local project, the Kargil Development Project, was registered as an autonomous NGO. The KDP’s areas of concern are similar to those of the LNP, except that, according to the perceived needs of the district, there is a greater emphasis on women’s development, especially in educa­ tion and, equally crucially, in health-care. It has trained village women as community health-workers and auxiliary nurse-mid- wives; their duties include health education in the community, especially among women, and with particular reference to ap­ propriate child-care practices. It has plans to extend some of its activities to Zanskar, and in the meantime hopes to work itself out of a job in some of the regions where it has been active for a long time, by handing over its projects to the village communities. Apart from SECMOL, the main indigenous NGO is the Ladakh Ecological Development Group (LEDeG), whose foun­ dation antedates SECMOL’s by about six years. It is based on the ideas of E.F. Schumacher and other critics of universal contem­ porary trends in economics and development; and was born out of the fear that, exposed to these trends — via the purposeful processes of development initiated by the administration, as well as via the arrival of tourists, whose attitudes and image were so

CHANGE 191 alien to the social and intellectual assumptions common to all Ladakhis — Ladakh’s traditional culture was in danger of being submerged, if not destroyed completely. LEDeG’s philosophy emphasizes the intimate relationship between social and material culture, and believes that far-reaching changes in the economy, which radically modify people’s lifestyle, are bound to have a destructive effect on cultural attitudes and practices. While recog­ nizing that change is bound to come, and that Ladakh cannot be kept in isolation from the outside world like an exhibit in a museum, its members believe that there can be more than one model of change and development, and that the standard Western model is not necessarily the most appropriate one for Ladakh. Appropriate development should be that which not only is sus­ tainable, but reinforces the Ladakhis’ traditional self-reliance. Thus the use of solar energy to heat houses in winter, especially when this has been tapped by modifying traditional building techniques, is appropriate; building ‘modern’ houses of steel and concrete, inherently cold materials which have been trucked in at enormous expense, and heating them by stoves burning imported kerosene, is not. Recognition of the primary role of energy in determining the shape of any economy has led LEDeG to con­ centrate on research into the application of the renewable sources of energy with which Ladakh is so abundandy endowed: sun, wind, and water, with emphasis on solar energy. Important among its other economic and developmental activities are research into traditional methods of agriculture, comparing these with modern methods, and encouraging farmers to be selective in considering the options open to them; and a handicrafts programme designed primarily to enable villagers to earn in their homes an income to supplement what they get from agriculture. At the same time, LEDeG runs information, education and awareness-raising programmes, in the form of meetings and seminars, for both Ladakhis and non-Ladakhis, programmes for children, and didac­ tic — but usually humorous — dramas. LEDeG is affiliated to an international organization, the Ladakh Project, which helps it to get funding, and supports its work internationally. In its early years, it welcomed foreign volunteers, who made a valuable con­ tribution to its work; now its staff are entirely Ladakhi. Its head­ quarters is a handsome building in appropriately modified traditional style in Leh, and it has a considerable presence in

192 LADAKH Kargil; but most of its work is done in the villages. Loosely affiliated to LEDeG is the Leh Women’s Alliance, addressing the same concerns as LEDeG, but with particular regard to women’s affairs. Apart from LEDeG, and the SCF’s Kargil Development Project, there are only two other voluntary agencies operating in Kargil. One is the Rajasthan-based Social Work and Research Centre, which has its solar photo-voltaic workshop-cum-training- centre there, as well as its groundwater drilling operation, and a small project for the processing of apricots, in the traditional form of oil from the kernels, and the non-traditional form of jam. The other is Kargil’s only home-grown agency, the Youth Voluntary Forum, founded about the same time as SECMOL and with similar aims, though a somewhat wider range of activities. These include, as well as tuition courses for school-leavers to try to bring down the failure rate, efforts in health education and other ap­ propriate forms of development at the village level. Recently they have been entrusted by the district administration with a water­ shed development project. Of the ways in which the outside world impinges on Ladakh and brings change to the traditional life and attitudes of the Ladakhis, the most insistent is surely the arrival of the tourist industry. The presence of outsiders is indeed no new thing for Ladakh, and Leh’s more elderly citizens can still remember the days of the trans-Karakoram trade, when the direct routes to central Asia and Tibet were open, the bazaar was home to a colony of foreign merchants, and there was a constant coming and going of the caravans. In a sense, then, the arrival of the tourists is the continuation of an ancient tradition; but there are differences that make all the difference. In the first place, there is the sheer scale of today’s tourist influx — in 1994 some 15,500 foreigners as well as nearly 2000 Indian nationals are recorded as having visited the Leh district alone. Secondly, the merchants of yesteryear came from a similar intellectual environment and shared roughly the same framework of values and expectations as the local people, something which can hardly be said of today’s visitors. Certainly the tourist influx involves change and adjust­ ment on an altogether unprecedented scale.

CHANGE 193 There can be no doubt that in its purely economic aspect it has benefited a lot of people. Particularly striking is the eagerness and self-confidence shown by the Ladakhis in making the consid­ erable investments necessary to exploit this new source of profit. This is true even of the family of modest means who have built a room or two on to their house to accommodate paying guests during the season; the more prosperous have built hotels, a few of which, even if they fall short of five-star standards of luxury, can provide the amenities expected by all but the most exacting of foreign tourists. When considering what such investment in­ volves, it must be remembered that apart from the actual stones and mud of which the walls are made, every physical component has been carried in from outside, over the Zoji-la, and for Leh, over the two other passes as well. In the early years of the tourist boom some national-scale hotel chains established a presence in Leh, mostly in partnership with local entrepreneurs; however, it was not long before they were put off by the remoteness of the place and the shortness of the tourist season, and opted out. Remarkably, the entire hospitality industry in Ladakh, catering to visitors from all over the world, is based on local initiative and Ladakhi native wit; The initial investment made, the returns are sure. A people who have little other marketable surplus are enabled to sell their ser­ vices: an ‘invisible export’, in which moreover transport costs are paid by the consumer. It is not only the proprietors of hotels and guest-houses who stand to profit; employment is created in a whole range of tourist-related services, and shopkeepers benefit too. The relatively small scale on which tourist facilities have developed means that the newly generated income is dispersed, bringing to quite poor people profits which must make a consid­ erable difference to their lives. A side-effect of the tourist boom is the way in which it has spurred on the authorities to make improvements to the region’s amenities, from which the local people benefit too. One of these is the street-lighting in Leh, installed in 1995 at the expense of the Jammu and Kashmir Department of Tourism, which has revolutionized life after dark for locals and tourists alike. Again, road transport has been developed, though in the absence of any pollution-control standards for vehicles plying in Ladakh this sometimes seems like a mixed blessing. Poisoning with diesel

194 LADAKH fumes the crystal-pure air of Dras or Khaltse or the remote high-altitude plateaux of Rupshu, can hardly be regarded as an appropriate manifestation of‘progress’. However, the local people do take advantage of bus- and truck-services introduced primarily for the benefit of tourists, as they do of the almost daily flights between Delhi and Leh. Apart from these few infrastructural interventions, Government and the district authorities have taken only a limited part in the provision of tourist amenities, preferring to let private enterprise get on with it, and confining themselves to a regulatory role. The partial exception is the Ladakh Festival, an innovation of the last few years, developed under the auspices of the state Tourism Department. Yet even this was conceived as an extension of the traditional village archery festivals, with their interludes of dancing and changdzinking; while it has grown in scale over the years, it remains decentralized with its organization mosdy in the hands of village committees. The Tourism Department confines its part in the Festival to publicity and some funding; it may not be too far off the mark to think of it as a patron of culture, its role analogous to that of the kings of old. The Festival’s success is measured less in the number of tourists who attend the various events, than by the extent of local participation. Of the local enthusiasm there can be not the slightest doubt. In 1995, the various events were spread over five villages in the immediate vicinity of Leh, as well as more distant places, including Deskit and Sumur in Nubra, Karzok in Rupshu, Tangtse across the Chang-la, and Skurbuchan and Biama on the Indus below Khaltse. The number of local people involved as performers was estimated at 7000, and the audiences too were largely composed of locals. Not only the Leh people, even the less exuberant Kargilis have become infected by Festival fever. The Kargil Festival of 1995 was on a more modest scale than that of Leh, but even so eight teams contended for the trophy in archery, a sport which they take with extreme seriousness in Kargil. Troupes from seven vil­ lages of the district presented their characteristic dances in the town; the tented camp in which they were accommodated is reported to have been vibrant with the colour and music of their interaction. Polo is an integral part of the Festival in both districts, and for the first time a team from outside Ladakh — from Manipur —

CHANGE 195 took part in the Leh tournament. In Kargil the enthusiasm en­ gendered by the Festival has led to a revival of polo in Chigtan and other areas where it had been practically banned by the local religious authorities. Kargil town unfortunately lacks a polo ground of the dimensions of the Leh one; qualifying matches were held in Dras and Shergol, and for the semis and finals a makeshift arrangement was made to play diagonally across a square sports field down by the river.3 After twenty years of tourism, it is possible to attempt an assessment of the impact on Ladakh of an exposure to the outside world that many feared had all the potential to undermine its traditional culture. In drawing up a balance-sheet of upside and downside, benefits and drawbacks, there seems to be, perhaps surprisingly, a decided tilt in favour. The downside seems largely to consist of an extension of problems inherent in the whole process of modernization, of which tourism is only a part. In the towns, the effort to meet the expectations of visitors, used as they are to a less frugal lifestyle than the Ladakhis, has led to some inappropriate forms of development. Where there is a shortage of water and no sewage system, for instance, the provision of large numbers of flush toilets can have consequences that are better imagined than described. Good agricultural land on the outskirts of the towns has been lost to hotel construction — just one example, and perhaps not the worst, of how the hope of relatively easy profits leads to the irresponsible exploitation of scarce natural resources. On some of the popular trekking trails, fragile mountain ecologies are at risk equally from the boots of the walkers and the hooves of the ponies that carry their baggage; while elsewhere the activities associated with adventure tourism may, if not subject to strict controls, disturb the habitat of threatened wildlife. In the cold dry air of the mountains most garbage is not biodegradable, so unless the trekking groups and their local guides are conscien­ tious about retrieving and bringing back every last scrap of waste material, there is danger of the kind of pollution that is said to have degraded parts of the Nepal Himalaya almost beyond redemption. None of these are problems which could not be effectively tackled as part of a comprehensive tourism development and management plan. While tourism has till now been left to develop with a minimum of regulation, there are indications that the

196 LADAKH framing and implementation of such a plan is high on the agenda of both the state Tourism Department and the Leh Autonomous Hill Development Council. However, there are other more subde ways in which tourism accelerates the changes inherent in the whole process of modernization. The extreme disparity in lifestyle between the local people and the visitors can have an unsettling effect on the minds, especially of the younger generation. Even such a commonplace matter as the clothes and equipment of the tourists, symbolizing as they do a way of life till recently quite unfamiliar to the average Ladakhi, may often convey a falsely glamorous picture of the lands from which their owners come, and cause feelings of envy and deprivation. The other main prob­ lem relates to the seasonality of virtually every activity in Ladakh. The peak season for tourism-related work coincides with the busiest months of the agricultural year, and thus takes the young men away from their villages just at the time when their labour is most needed. True, it is arguable that the local guides and porters who accompany the trekking groups are only continuing the ancient tradition of kiraiyakashi. But in the days of the caravans, families were undivided, and there were few jobs other than kiraiyakashi to take them away from home; thus a family was rarely left without one or more adult males to attend to the field-work. Of course much of this is done by the women, as it always has been. But recent sociological research indicates rising levels of anxiety among village women left to take sole respon­ sibility for house, family and fields after the menfolk have been drawn away from their homes into a different kind of world. And yet the impact has been far from entirely negative. The response of the Ladakhis to the challenge that tourism poses demonstrates yet again their level-headedness and the resilience of their culture. Even the impressionable young, now the initial glamour has worn off, seem to take exposure to the affluence and technical wizardry of the developed world in their stride, while the more thoughtful have acquired some appreciation of the problems inherent in current Western models of ‘development’ and ‘progress’. This is partly due to groups like LEDeG, which has made a conscious effort, through dramas and other forms of propaganda, to inculcate an awareness of the downside of Western technological civilization, and a corresponding pride in Ladakhi culture. It is difficult however to imagine that LEDeG’s exhorta­

CHANGE 197 tions would have been effective had its listeners not had ears to hear in the first place. No one can be influenced by a message which is diametrically contrary to what they think and feel. One factor that has prevented Ladakh going overboard in its response to tourism may be the character of the tourists themsel­ ves. Ladakh’s altitude and remoteness, and the relative difficulty of access — flights in the tourist season being often fully booked weeks if not months in advance, while the 19-hour drive from Manali is a physical ordeal for all but the most fit — has tended to ensure that it gets a higher proportion of what might be described as ‘quality tourists’ than, say, Manali or Goa or Kath­ mandu. So far there are no indications of the hippy-and-drugs culture having spread to Ladakh; on the contrary, a fairly high proportion of the visitors seem to be drawn by an informed appreciation of some facet of Ladakh’s life and civilization. The effect of this has been to make many Ladakhis take a fresh look at their own life and culture. A way of life that they take for granted, they have suddenly realized, is regarded by outsiders as something worthy of study and admiration; and to some of them this realization has given an enormous access of cultural pride and personal self-confidence. This is not to discount entirely the fear, cogently expressed by sympathetic observers in the first few years after Ladakh was opened to outsiders, that tourism would result in cultural erosion and psychological disorientation; a fear based on observed changes in people’s attitudes.3 Even now there must be Ladakhis who react with anxiety and confusion to the pressures of modernization in general and tourism in particular. But per­ sonal observation and conversation with a considerable number of intelligent young Ladakhis suggests that such fears may have been exaggerated. The efforts of LEDeG and SECMOL, crucial as they have been in countering the more unfortunate effects of the changes that no Ladakhi can run away from, are themselves in part expressions of a resilient culture. A living culture, faced with enormous changes, will itself change in response. But there seems every ground for optimism that the challenge of tourism is one that this culture, changing as it must, will survive. The question of change, what change is desirable and what not, and what the impact of change actually is on society and

198 LADAKH individuals, is a hody debated one among Ladakhis and Ladakh- watchers. It would perhaps take a die-hard believer in what has been called the ‘Shangri-la’ theory of Ladakh to argue that Ladakh could or should be left untouched by the insistent presence of the modern world. After all, if any one thing is certain, it is that there is no way change can be prevented. Change manifests itself in a hundred ways. A villager from Chigtan learns to drive and becomes the driver of an official car. A doctor’s wife, planning to have a new goncha made, chooses not pattu as the material, but polyester. A Chang-thang shepherd on a trip to Leh to trade wool and butter for barley is amazed at the variety of human types he sees as he rubs shoulders in the bazaar with visitors from Tiruchirapalli and Tokyo, Abidjan and Aberdeen. The daughter of a well-to-do Kargil family, having passed her school-leaving exam in the local girls’ school, enters a course for BA in Srinagar. The second son of a polyandrous marriage in a Zanskar village refuses either to become a lama or to share his elder brother’s wife; he claims his share of the family land, as he is legally entitled to do, and splits an estate whose contours have remained un­ changed for generations. And yet, these are no more than symbols of much greater shifts. The modern world has come to Ladakh in every possible sense: physically by way of the new transport linkages; socially by the probably irrevocable modifications of traditional village and fami­ ly life; economically by the replacement of a barter by a money economy; technologically by the introduction of machines and other new ways of doing things; and intellectually by the mental adjustments necessary to cope with the new order. All these aspects of change are interdependent: just as traditional Ladakh was a single package, so is the modern world with which Ladakh is striving to come to terms. There is therefore only one fundamental question: Is it possible for Ladakh and the Ladakhis to control the change that is inevitable: to split the package that the modern world is offering, take what suits themselves and reject the rest? To do this — requires in the first place a clear-headed percep­ tion of what changes are desirable and what not. What kinds of intervention, for instance, would be appropriate to improve mat­ ters in the villages, where the majority of Ladakhis have their homes and where, particularly in the remote interior among the crumpled masses of the mountains, there is little likelihood of

CHANGE 199 change for the better unless a conscious effort is made? Already the winds of change and commercialization are being felt, and if left to blow uncontrolled they seem more likely to destroy than to enhance the systems already in place. To give just one example, changes in the village economy have a tendency to erode co-opera­ tive patterns of life and work; now increasingly when extra labour is needed for the spring sowing or for taking in the harvest, even local helpers expect a wage, rather than assuming a reciprocal arrangement. In the effort to control change, the aim must be to introduce such innovations as will not disrupt highly evolved social structures, but may yet improve the return that the farming or herding family can get from their land or their flocks, and thus furnish them with a margin, something to fall back on when times are hard. In the past, no doubt, Ladakh was an economically viable society without any outside intervention. But the viability of society as a whole must be a matter of small consolation to the individual who is feeling the pinch: to the Chang-pa family, for instance, who have lost four-fifths of their precious stock of pash­ mina goats because after five years there has been snow in Chang- thang. In addition, there can be no doubt that today social shifts are leading to an unprecedented increase in the population, and this implies an urgent need for both an expanded resource base, and increased opportunities for people to fulfil their potential within the existing social structure. It would seem, moreover, a matter of the first importance to raise the perceived status of the cultivator as a means to counteract the attraction that, in a rapidly changing world, is exerted by the towns. Whether change is controlled or not, the exposure of Ladakh to the outside world is going to result in increasing prosperity in Leh and Kargil. It is here that the tourists chiefly congregate; here that the administrative offices generate employ­ ment at many levels; here that the military have their headquarters; here that there are the only bazaars worthy of the name; here that construction booms; here, in short, that there are opportunities. Comparable conditions all over the world have had the conse­ quence of a rush from village to town, and the degradation of life in both. While the scale of the problem in Ladakh is such that urban blight does not seem to be an immediate danger (though the apparently uncontrolled ribbon development between the airport and the town in Leh is far from lovely), there is a real fear

200 LADAKH of rural depopulation. By enhancing the quality of life in the village — increasing the returns to the farmers and adding to their material comforts, while at the same time modifying the content of education to make it relevant to the concerns and prospects of the children — perhaps it may be possible to create a viable future for the villages and their younger inhabitants. The key to the whole problem is perhaps self-reliance, both individual and social. This does not necessarily mean the same thing as self-sufficiency, for probably Ladakh no longer has the capacity to feed a vasdy increased population from its small and finite area of cultivable land. What it does mean is paying your way in the world. In the attractions it holds for discriminating visitors from outside, Ladakh has, as it were, an exportable surplus which should enable it to do this. Surely there can be no question of turning the clock back, keeping Ladakh as a quaint anachronism, an exhibit in a museum whose existence is justified only by the number of visitors who come to gape at it. At the same time, most thinking Ladakhis are quite shrewd enough to realize that if the package on offer by the modern world is accepted lock, stock and barrel, along with its inevitable downside — the untrammeled consumerism, the commercialism, the aggressive economics that gives importance only to the profit motive, the crime, the vulgarity, the pollution, the overcrowding, the urban sprawl, the uncontrolled proliferation of motor vehicles, the despoiling of the natural environment — then Ladakh will no more be Ladakh and the tourists will stop coming. Not only that, but a society that has created a sustainable lifestyle in the harshest of climatic and environmental conditions, evolved a culture that may not be highly sophisticated but is as firmly based in the reality of human nature as any other, and integrated its religious ethos with outstanding success into the structures of daily life, will disappear for ever. Every lifestyle as highly evolved as that of Ladakh is an achievement of the human spirit, the destruction of which must be regarded as a major tragedy. The enterprise is not an easy one, and there are few places in the world which have succeeded to any significant degree in splitting the package. Probably one of them is Ladakh’s historical ally, Bhutan — at the cost however of restricting the entry of foreigners to a very limited number of rich people who can afford to meet the stipulation of a minimum daily

CHANGE 2 0 1 expenditure of US $400 (1995). Such a policy in Ladakh seems neither possible nor desirable. There are many both inside and outside the region who believe that the answer may lie in the LEDeG approach: do not assume that because a certain model of economics or technology has worked (if it has) in the Western world it is ipso facto appropriate for Ladakh; decentralize your decision-making and your economic structures; minimize your reliance for vital economic inputs on outside sources of supply over which you have no control; exploit the free and renewable sources of energy that are so abundant in Ladakh, and are only now being recognized by the conventional ‘developers’ as offering viable possibilities; remember that all systems and all processes are interdependent, and assess the impact that change in any one of them may have on all the others; understanding the relationship between technology and social structures, never fall into the trap of believing that because a technological in­ novation reduces someone’s workload or improves their produc­ tivity, it is necessarily an overall improvement; and introduce any change only after the fullest discussion with those whose lives are going to be affected by it. Above all understand your own culture and lifestyle and the values they embody, and take pride in them as something to be cherished, a unique and precious part of the human heritage. Ladakh does not have the same degree of control over its own affairs as independent and relatively prosperous Bhutan. Hitherto, the head of the district administration, known as the Development Commissioner, has been appointed by and is answerable to the State Government. The Indian system of district administration involves its officers in frequent transfers; a DC’s average tenure is two years or thereabouts. When the DC is an energetic, en­ lightened and dedicated individual, who has taken the trouble to study and understand the possibilities open to him, the develop­ ment process is likely to carry on in the right direction. Otherwise, perhaps not. Either way, beyond the broad framework of policy laid down by the State Government — reflecting preoccupations which may or may not be relevant to conditions in any given district, and which are likely in any case to be guided in part at least by political considerations — there can be little continuity, especially in view of the fact that the majority of the DCs of both districts have been non-Ladakhis, the effective length of whose

2 0 2 LADAKH tenure is shortened by the several months it inevitably takes them to get a grasp on the local conditions. But as of September 1995 the Leh district does have its Autonomous Hill Development Council, (of which the DC is Chief Executive Officer). The Council consists of Ladakhis elected by Ladakhis, and has very considerable powers, including the disposal of substantial development funds. With Ladakhis at the helm of affairs in one of the region’s two districts, there may be a good chance of directing Ladakh’s development into a channel that will enable it to avoid the worst features of the Western model and retain the qualities that make it so special for so many people. The Council members, more than any outsider, may be expected to appreciate the worth of the foundation that is theirs to build on — the wisdom of generations with its hard-won understanding of the importance of maintaining a balance between humankind and nature; and to use it as the starting point for a kind of development that will bring Ladakh in line with the twenty-first century without severing its links with the past. Lall (ed.), New Delhi, 1981, p. 282. 3. See especially, Helena Norberg-Hodge, Ancient Futures: Learning from Ladakh.

1 1 Buddhists, Muslims and Christians O f the three religions represented in Ladakh, the Chris­ tians form a tiny minority, not much more than 100 persons, almost all of them in and around Leh. Bud­ dhists and Muslims are found in more or less equal numbers, with a preponderance of Buddhists in the north and east, and of Muslims to the south and west. Kargil town, Dras, the Suru valley as far as Parkachik, Pashkyum and Shagkar-Chigtan are almost exclusively Muslim; Mulbekh and Bodh Kharbu have mixed populations, with a distinct Buddhist majority. Zanskar is basically a Buddhist area, the small Muslim community there being the descendants of immigrants from the Jammu region who came in the wake of Zorawar Singh’s invasion. The Indus valley from Khaltse upstream, and downstream on the right bank (where the Brokpa practise their own variant of the religion) is almost uniformly Buddhist, and so are Chang-thang and Nubra. The exceptions are a small but influential Muslim population in Leh itself and a few of the surrounding villages, and a few Muslims in the remote villages of Turtuk and Bogdang, away down the Shayok river to the north-west. For centuries Ladakhi life and ways of thought have been moulded by Buddhism, the particular form followed in Tibet and Ladakh being the Vajrayana, the Vehicle of the Thunderbolt. Its external expressions are everywhere. At the approach to every village there are a number of chorteru, the stupas of ancient Indian Buddhism, referring in their origin to the grave-mounds erected over the divided ashes of Gautama Buddha. The erection of these was accounted a work of piety, and the greatest number of them is found in and around the ancient royal residence at Shey, fourteen kilometres up the Indus from Leh. Those in or near the gompas

204 LADAKH The Imambara at Trespon, up the Suru valley from Kargil. often contain sacred objects: block-printed scriptures, or clay images, or offerings of ritual cakes; while inside the temples, made o f beautifully worked metal and precious stones, they serve the purpose o f reliquaries. They are often situated near the approach to a village to bar the way to the entry o f evil spirits. There are eight different types of chorten, the different shapes symbolizing different aspects of the Buddhist way. For example, the one with a tapering spire in thirteen sections or ‘wheels’ represents the progress o f the soul towards enlightenment. Like every other religious structure, the chorten is to be circumambulated in a clockwise direction, symbolizing the unity of the soul’s awakening with the movement o f the planets: the unity o f all things in the cosmic order. A Buddhist as a matter o f instinct and reflex will never pass a chorten even casually without keeping it on his right; and he may undertake ritual circumambulation, which has the purpose of cleansing the soul, increasing merit, and ultimately bringing the soul nearer to enlightenment. Sometimes chortenAike

BUDDHISTS, MUSLIMS AND CHRISTIANS 205 structures fulfil the function of a gateway, the main building being raised off the ground to form an arch, under which a road passes. The inner walls of these are often painted with religious motifs, and the small ceiling-space filled with a mandala. Near the chorten, or isolated in the midst of the desert, are seen long, straight, apparently functionless walls. These are mani walls. One or two metres in height, and two or three metres broad, they vary in length from a few metres to almost a kilometre. They are built of sand and rubble, and are faced with stones carrying inscriptions — a prayer or an invocation — engraved in the beautiful Tibetan script. Most often this is the mantra, Om Mani Padme Hum — ‘Hail the Jewel in the Lotus’ — an invocation of secret tantric significance, particularly associated with the Bod- hisattva of Compassion, Avalokiteswara. Mani walls like chorten, are to be passed on the right; at a mani wall accordingly the footpath bifurcates, so that both going and coming the wayfarer may keep it on his right. Prayer-flags printed with texts and invocations, and prayer wheels twirled in a clockwise direction similarly serve a symbolic purpose. The prayer-wheel, whether an enormous cylinder let into the wall of a monastery to be set in motion by the hands of the faithful as they pass, or set on paddle-floats to be kept in perpetual rotation by the action of running water, or on a handle spun by a gende movement of the wrist, bears witness to the Buddha’s conception of the law of all existence — dharma — symbolized by the endless motion of the wheel. It may indeed be wondered how far ordinary Ladakhis cherish a deep appreciation of the inwardness of the beliefs connected with these common objects, highly charged with symbolism as they are. They spin their prayer-wheel, scrupulously keep a chorten or mani wall on their right as they pass, without every time necessarily thinking of the cosmic turning of the Great Wheel of all existence. For, as with all the great religions of the world, and perhaps, because of its complexity, most of all, there has developed in Buddhism a deep split between popular practice and belief, and the higher teaching of theology. This split is surely emphasized by the fact that those who take the religion seriously, and want to go deep into its teachings, have to undergo initiations that must tend to set them apart from their believing but less committed fellows.

206 LADAKH Indeed, many of the doctrines are difficult and subde in the extreme. Central to Buddhism in all its forms is the idea that every soul has the capacity to reach the state of enlightenment, and thence nirvana — the extinction o f its separate identity in union with the cosmic soul; in other words, to become a Buddha. The Mahayana goes further, and adds a principle of compassion, so that no enlightened soul can be content to accept nirvana for itself while a single other soul remains bound to the wheel of mundane existence with its perpetual suffering — suffering being the very nature and concomitant of separation from the soul of all things. Hence the ideal of the Bodhisattva, who might be defined as a potential Buddha, an enlightened soul, which for aeons forgoes nirvana, returning to the world in bodily form over and over again, to work for the liberation of all souls. This central principle, it is true, is not difficult to grasp. But from it emanated a most complicated system of Buddhas and Bodhisattvas — Gautama Buddha, or Sakyamuni, being con­ sidered as but one of a series of historical Buddhas of different eras.
